UAL'S REQUEST for a government loan guarantee was rejected for a third time. The decision forces the
United Airlines parent to scramble to raise about $2 billion in financing in order to emerge from Chapter 11.
The company is expected to seek more wage and benefit concessions from its employees.
Delta Air's creditors formed a committee to pursue restructuring talks, a step to prepare for a possible
bankruptcy-court filing.
---
Consumer spending climbed 1% in May, fueled by income gains. The Fed is likely to raise rates to 1.25%
from 1% tomorrow.
GM said June U.S. vehicle sales will trail expectations, contrasting with more-upbeat forecasts offered by
foreign rivals.
---
Oil prices fell $1.31 to $36.24 a barrel, the lowest Nymex close in over two months, sparked by the handover
of sovereignty in Iraq.
---
The Dow industrials reversed a rally to fall 14.75 to 10357.09. Some investors blamed slipping GM sales and
Mideast violence.
---
Shell said 30 employees so far have been questioned by the SEC regarding the oil company's overstatement
of energy reserves.
---
Fund holders will receive fee rebates from funds that were hurt by market timing, as part of a settlement with
regulators.
---
Most mutual funds performed within a small range this quarter. Energy funds did well; gold and emerging
markets were weak.
---
Amazon filed a countersuit against Toys "R" Us, asking for over $750 million and an end to its contract with
the toy retailer.
---
The Supreme Court agreed to hear a case that could clarify what investors must prove to sue companies
over stock losses.

Beazer Homes USA, Western Gas And Champion Fall, as Delta Rises
Small-Stock Focus
Beazer Homes USA, Western Gas And Champion Fall, as Delta Rises
By Cynthia Schreiber Dow Jones Newswires
670 words
29 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Home builders helped to knock small-capitalization stocks lower as investors worried about
the highly anticipated Federal Reserve meeting.
Interest-rate sensitive home builders, after posting solid gains last week, were among the session's worst
performers ahead of the Fed's two-day policy meeting on interest rates, which starts today. Beazer Homes
USA (NYSE) shed $3.17, or 3.1%, to $98.12, and Champion Enterprises (NYSE) fell 14 cents, or 1.4%, to
9.69.
Large-cap tobacco stocks showed strength after a federal-court judge cleared the way for cigarette makers to
request an immediate appeal of a ruling in a government racketeering case. But moves among small tobacco
stocks were mixed: Carolina Group (NYSE) rose 35 cents, or 1.4%, to 24.62 while Vector Group (NYSE) fell
45 cents, or 2.7%, to 15.96.
The Russell 2000 Index slid 3.60 points, or 0.61%, to 584.10, and the S&P SmallCap 600 Index eased 1.32,
or 0.45%, to 291.75. The Nasdaq Composite Index of large-cap and small-cap stocks shed 5.65 points, or
0.28%, to 2019.82.
The early transfer of power in Iraq set a positive tone for stocks early on, but the market pulled back in
afternoon trading as concern about the Fed's next interest-rate move crept into the market.
Although the Fed is widely expected to raise rates by one-quarter percentage point tomorrow, "it's not a sure
thing," said Peter McCorry, senior trader at Keefe, Bruyette &Woods. A sharper increase in rates could rattle
investors.
A drop in crude-oil prices helped to fuel gains in the transport group. On the Big Board, Delta Air Lines rose
42 cents, or 6.4%, to 7.03, and Continental Airlines rose 39 cents, or 3.7%, to 11.05. Trucking company USF
added 85 cents, or 2.5%, to 34.90.
But shares of energy companies went the opposite way. Western Gas Resources (NYSE) fell 1.41, or 4.3%,
to 31.29, Vintage Petroleum (NYSE) lost 48 cents, or 2.8%, to 16.68 and Cimarex Energy (NYSE) eased 44
cents, or 1.5%, to 29.16.
More strides were made by footwear stocks after large-cap Nike posted a rise in quarterly earnings late
Thursday. Wolverine World Wide (NYSE) gained 48 cents, or 1.9%, to 26.28 after saying it signed a contract
with Russ Berrie to make and market toys that extend Wolverine's Hush Puppies brand. Russ Berrie (NYSE)
slipped six cents, or 0.3%, to 19.37.
Gold prices fell, as did stocks related to the precious metal. Meridian Gold fell 23 cents, or 1.8%, to 12.55,
and Coeur D'Alene Mines lost five cents, or 1.2%, to 4.14 and Hecla Mining declined seven cents, or 1.2%, to
5.77, all on the NYSE.
Taser International added 85 cents, or 2%, to 42.94. The Scottsdale, Ariz., stun-gun maker said it received a
$436,000 follow-on order from the Dayton, Ohio, police department.
Drugstore.com fell 33 cents, or 9.3%, to 3.21. The Bellevue, Wash., online retailer said it is the subject of a
lawsuit, seeking class-action status, alleging that the company made false and misleading statements about
its financial condition.
Allegheny Technologies (NYSE) soared 1.82, or 12%, to 17.44. The Pittsburgh steel producer said it expects
to post a second-quarter profit because of improving market conditions and special items.

--- Page 8 ---
Page 8 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Actuate jumped 40 cents, or 11%, to 4.09 after upbeat comments on the South San Francisco, Calif.,
business-software company appeared in Barron's, a sister publication of The Wall Street Journal.
Triquint Semiconductor lost 41 cents, or 7.5%, to 5.06, after Lehman Brothers downgraded the stock to
underweight from equal weight, citing slower growth in the handset market.

Gold Surges 2% on Buying by Banks, Speculators
Commodities Report
Gold Surges 2% on Buying by Banks, Speculators
By Gavin Maguire OsterDowJones Commodity News
377 words
25 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold prices on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange rose 2% on heavy
bullion bank and speculative buying, spurred by reports of bombings in Turkey, renewed U.S. dollar
weakness and the scaling of key technical chart levels.
The price of the nearby-month futures contract jumped $8.10 to $403.10 an ounce. The most-active August
contract gained $8 to $403.50 an ounce.
Turkey's capital, Ankara, was hit by a bomb blast overnight, then reports emerged of a car bombing in
Istanbul shortly before the Comex open.
This prompted a rise in safe-haven interest in bullion and steered spot prices on the global market through
recent resistance in the $398 to $399 region before the start of trading in New York.
As the U.S. dollar languished near recent lows early yesterday, follow-through buying interest appeared amid
the investment bank and technical-chart following speculative communities, taking August gold futures
through the psychologically significant $400 level as Comex trading began. The market hit a 10-week high on
the open before pulling back slightly on profit-taking but rallied back to set a new session high of $404 in late
trading.
While gold remains significantly off of its April highs of about $433 because of a lengthy bout of speculative
liquidation spurred by the emerging likelihood of a rise in U.S. interest rates, it is trading more than $25 off of
its May low of $372.50. Renewed geopolitical tensions across the Middle East, combined with the U.S.
dollar's recent sluggish performance against its rivals, have served to steer investors back into the precious
metal in recent weeks
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Prices rose as an escalation in a strike by Norwegian oil workers threatened to shut down the
country's oil output. Nymex August crude oil rose 36 cents to $37.93 a barrel.
ORANGE JUICE: New York Board of Trade prices rose sharply as the market rose to seven-week highs on
technical chart-related buying. July orange juice rose 1.85 cents to 60.20 cents a pound.
---
Jennifer Chiariello contributed to this article.

RTI, Olin Gain as Metals Group Gives Stocks a Slight Boost
Small-Stock Focus
RTI, Olin Gain as Metals Group Gives Stocks a Slight Boost
By Marcelo Prince Dow Jones Newswires
467 words
21 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Small-capitalization shares traded in a narrow range and ended slightly higher, led by a rally in
the metals group.
Among the gainers in the metals group, RTI International Metals rose $1.25, or 8.9%, to $15.28, and Olin
gained 25 cents, or 1.5%, to 17.14, both on the New York Stock Exchange.
Gold rallied and lifted stocks associated with the precious metal. Coeur D'Alene Mines rose 10 cents, or
2.4%, to 4.22 and Hecla Mining gained 19 cents, or 3.3%, to 5.99 on the Big Board. Meanwhile, Stillwater
Mining rose 1.36, or 9.8%, to 15.26, buoyed by a brokerage upgrade.
More broadly, concerns about higher interest rates kept trading subdued and pressured other small-cap
groups. "As long as the tightening process is hanging over the market, there won't be a lot of upside," said Bill
Strazzullo, chief market strategist at State Street.

The `I' Word
The `I' Word
By David Malpass
1,262 words
18 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A10
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
One of the most important challenges in economics is to understand the causes of inflation and deflation,
and then to avoid both. The problem is far from trivial -- inflations and deflations have become relatively
common and are damaging when they occur. Thus, monetary policy presents a huge opportunity for faster
U.S. and global growth.
Until just a few months ago, many thought the U.S. still had a deflation problem -- not to mention a jobs
problem and even a growth problem. Now, the concern has flip-flopped to inflation, causing wide fluctuations
in bond yields, mortgage rates, interest rate expectations and currencies.
In monetary policy terms, these are exciting times. At 1%, the Fed-funds rate is stunningly low. The
economy is actually accelerating from a fast growth rate, as shown by recent employment reports and
Wednesday's jump in industrial production. I expect a mild burst of inflation just ahead, the result of the
weaker dollar in 2002 and 2003.
Interest rate futures have priced in significantly higher rates over the next two years. This helped the dollar
recover some in April and May. These market-based indicators argue that the expected interest rate hikes
may be enough to stop further dollar weakness, allowing the inflation burst to come and go.
In looking back at the huge swings between inflation and deflation, two key conclusions jump out. First,
changes in the value of the dollar had a major impact on the price level. When the dollar was weaker than its
10-year trend, there was upward pressure on prices, and vice versa. The pattern was predictable. Dollar
weakness in the 1970s brought inflation and the recessions of 1974, 1980 and 1982. Dollar strength in
1998-2000 caused deflation pressures that showed up first in gold and commodity prices, then in the broader
inflation indices. Dollar weakness in 2002-2003 is now putting upward pressure on prices, again starting with
gold and commodities and working its way through the general price level in a piece-by-piece fashion.
Second, the standard model -- trying to measure the slack in the economy -- simply didn't work in anticipating
deflation or inflation pressures. For one, the "output gap" was very hard to measure, given the economy's
dynamism. More importantly, changes in the value of the dollar were so large that they dominated other
factors.
It should also be clear that inflation and deflation rates themselves have not been suitable for managing
monetary policy because they have such a long lag from past monetary policy. Even monitoring inflation
expectations is not helpful since the near-term inflation outlook is the result of past events. The analogy of
driving a slow-to-turn supertanker is apt. If the pilot directs the rudder based on the past course (historical
inflation) or even the current course (inflation expectations), the ship will zig-zag, wasting fuel and risking
shoals.
The growth-maximizing course is for the Fed to raise interest rates fast enough to keep the dollar stable,
while explicitly recognizing that there may be a material bulge in the inflation rate over the next two years
due to past dollar weakness. As inflation bubbles up in coming months, some will want to raise interest
rates even faster to get ahead of it, just as they wanted to cut rates below 1% in 2003 to get ahead of the
fading deflation pressures already addressed by 2002's dollar weakness. This won't stop the inflation bulge
or maximize growth.
It is comforting that increases in interest rate expectations have caused strength in the dollar, confirming that
markets are confident in the Fed and the U.S. economy; just not in the 1% interest rate. While interest rates
are far below neutral, it looks as if the distortions haven't accumulated to the point of causing serious
damage, meaning rate hikes will have a positive effect on the economic outlook. However, in the absence of
clear guidance from the Fed, the market will likely assume the worst -- that the Fed will push rates up as long
as extra inflation persists, even if it causes an overshoot on the high side. Just as markets worried their way
into deflation psychology after the fact in 2003, they may worry themselves into a deeper inflation

--- Page 21 ---
Page 21 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
psychology in 2005. The Fed can avoid this by explaining that the upward pressure on prices is the result of
dollar weakness in 2003 and will pass.
In the '90s, Alan Greenspan achieved ever-lower inflation amid fast economic growth. The U.S. has an
opportunity to make another great monetary leap forward by creating a conceptual bridge to a stable price
environment, one that achieves low, relatively steady CPI inflation. Only dollar stability offers that bridge. If
the dollar is stable, neither inflation nor deflation can be substantial. In the alternative, dollar instability,
inflation and interest rates will continue to fluctuate over a wide range, the costly zig-zag process we have
been experiencing.
The starting point for a dollar stability standard is to recognize that sustained currency weakness is
inflationary while sustained currency strength is deflationary. The connection is somehow still controversial in
economics. It shouldn't be. If an item is priced in dollars and the dollar weakens, it makes sense that the
dollar price will rise at some point. Likewise, yen prices had to fall in the '90s due to the yen's strength,
causing a deflation spiral. It would be a major pro-growth change in economics if this currency stability logic
were accepted and incorporated into monetary policy.
The market is clearly prepared to welcome a dollar stability standard under which the Fed would watch
market-based indicators, especially the value of the dollar, to set interest rates and help avoid harmful
inflation and deflation. The Fed and particularly its current chairman have immense credibility in fighting
inflation. The dollar's value changes instantly when the Fed alters its monetary policy direction, giving clear
feedback. This provides an easy path in which the Fed uses dollar stability to combat inflation.
The market implications of a change to a dollar stability standard would be particularly favorable. As recently
as March 25, the heavily traded futures contract for the December Fed-funds rate stood at 1.26%. This
indicated that the bond market accepted the Fed's then-held view that deflation was a slightly higher risk than
inflation and that the Fed would "be patient in removing its policy accommodation" as stated in the Fed's
March 15 statement. By June 16, billions of dollars had evaporated as bond yields rose. The December
contract had jumped over a full percent, to 2.3%, based on new data and new Fed signals.
---
Going forward, the proper level of interest rates depends heavily on whether the coming inflation bulge is
perceived as temporary or lasting. If the Fed were using forward-looking indicators, particularly the value of
the dollar, to set interest rates and pursue price stability, it would add substantially to non-inflationary U.S.
growth. This would be quickly reflected in stock prices, just as the pro-growth 2003 tax cut was.
The debate is straightforward -- do changes in the value of the dollar affect the price level? They clearly do,
so dollar stability provides a bridge to price stability and a useful guide in combating the coming inflation
pressures.
---
Mr. Malpass is chief economist at Bear, Stearns.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Stabilizing Exchange Rate Won't Control Inflation" -- WSJ June 22,
2004)

The Inflation Game
The Inflation Game
By Michael H. Moskow
1,128 words
15 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Over the past three years, the Federal Reserve's accommodative monetary policy has supported both of our
primary goals of maximum sustainable growth and price stability. Not only did it provide useful stimulus to real
economic growth, it helped prevent inflation from falling to undesirably low levels. However, as the economy
has improved, concerns about the sustainability of growth and falling inflation have been replaced by
concerns about the possibility of rising inflation.
One of the perks of my job is that I get to talk to many different people -- from international investment
bankers to local community groups. The insights and concerns that people share are an important
complement to the statistical analyses that Fed economists produce. In recent months, people have been
worried about increases in the prices of many goods and services; gasoline, metals, housing, and health care
prices are the most frequently cited.
Of course, such reports aren't just anecdotes. This year, broad measures of consumer inflation have risen
from the extremely low rates we saw late last year. One of the key challenges for Fed policy makers is
judging to what extent such developments are symptomatic of a more general increase in inflationary
pressures and to what extent they are transitory fluctuations in what are often volatile monthly data.
Many analysts like to point to a single economic indicator that they believe to be a good barometer of
underlying inflationary pressures. Common favorites include gold and commodity prices. Although there's a
venerable history of tracking such indicators, I've always found it difficult to know when their movements
reflect general inflationary pressures or when they're being driven by more idiosyncratic forces. Often it
seems to be the latter. Though rising gold prices are often claimed to foreshadow higher inflation, since
1984 movements in gold prices and general inflation have been virtually uncorrelated. Thus, it's not
surprising that formal statistical analyses show that single indicators like gold have little ability to predict
inflation. Indeed, given its frequent ups and downs, one could say -- with apologies to Paul Samuelson --
that the price of gold has predicted nine out of the last five bouts of inflation.
Other analysts see results like this, throw up their hands and say we might as well give up on forecasting
changes in inflation and simply predict that next year's inflation rate will be the same as this year's. In fact,
core inflation in the U.S. generally does not change very quickly and -- as some of my colleagues at the
Minneapolis Fed reported a few years ago -- forecasting next year's inflation with this year's rate did
remarkably well between 1985 and the late '90s. Not surprisingly, however, this method did not do well during
less tranquil inflationary periods. And, obviously, such forecasts offer no guidance on when inflationary
pressures are building or waning.
There is a useful observation to make at this point. Predicting inflation as accurately as possible is only one
component of our forecasting exercise. Another is learning from our mistakes. Understanding the reasons
that forecasts are off the mark often yields important insights into the workings of the economy. This requires
a view or theory of the inflation process -- something that is missing from forecasts simply based on the price
of gold or last year's inflation rate.
An important case in point was the recent acceleration in productivity. Inflation forecast errors provided early
indication of the staying power of the extraordinary step-up in productivity growth that occurred in the second
half of the '90s. Many forecasters thought that the robust economic growth during the period would generate
higher inflation. But inflation kept coming in lower than expected. Over time, we began to understand that
the economy was not experiencing the resource constraints and associated cost pressures that we had
expected because the underlying trend in productive capacity had increased. Although many economists had
conjectured that higher productivity growth was here to stay, the inflation data provided useful confirmation
on its persistence.
Another way of describing the lessons from the inflation forecast errors of the late '90s is that we learned that
actual output was not as high relative to potential output as we'd thought. Many statistical studies show that

--- Page 27 ---
Page 27 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
indicators of resource utilization can help predict inflation. The output gap -- the difference between potential
and actual output -- is simply one framework used to gauge resource slack. Of course, the gap is difficult to
measure, and factors other than the level of resource slack affect inflation. Indeed, inflation is the outcome
of complicated interactions among the structure of the economy, inflationary expectations, monetary policy,
and unforeseen events. This means that forecasting and explaining inflation requires a combination of
economic theory, statistical analyses, and judgment. Accordingly, monetary policymakers look at a wide
variety of economic data and models in their search for general patterns of consistency that signal incipient
inflationary pressures.
One way to find and quantify these patterns is to use statistical techniques. At the Chicago Fed, we have
been publishing our Chicago Fed National Activity Index (CFNAI) since March 2001. The index is a statistical
summary measure of 85 economic indicators that provides useful predictive information about turning points
in inflation. Still, we wouldn't advocate exclusively using the CFNAI to forecast inflation; it's just one tool in
the box.
---
I've discussed several alternative approaches to forecasting, and it is fair to ask how well these methods have
done over time. For example, rising gold prices in 1979 may have provided a useful signal of inflationary
pressures then, but a track record of continued success is crucial. How good has the Fed been at predicting
inflation? Recent studies by David and Christina Romer of the University of California and Christopher Sims
of Princeton have found that the Federal Reserve Board staff's inflation forecasts have performed better than
those of professional forecasters over the last 20 years. Perhaps this is because Fed forecasts are informed
by a wide range of economic indicators, a variety of analyses, a healthy dose of anecdotal information, and
judgment.
Over the past 25 years, inflation has come down from double-digit rates to a pace consistent with effective
price stability. This is an important achievement. Of course, we must continue to be vigilant in monitoring
developments that pose a risk to this accomplishment. Currently, the federal-funds rate is very low, and this
degree of policy accommodation cannot be maintained indefinitely. Sifting through the data and analyzing
them from a variety of perspectives will continue to be an important input into our assessment of the
appropriate stance of monetary policy.
---
Mr. Moskow is president and CEO of the Federal Reserve Bank of Chicago.

Page 28 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.

Ryan Beck. "There are fears again about how much inflation is going to be a part of this marketplace."
The Russell 2000 Index of small stocks fell 11.45, or 2%, to 557.67, and the Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600
Index lost 4.49, or 1.6%, to 278.99. The Nasdaq Composite Index of large-cap and small-cap stocks declined
29.88, or 1.5%, to 1969.99.
Stocks sensitive to interest rates, including home builders, were among the day's poorest performers. On the
Big Board, Beazer Homes USA lost 2.28, or 2.4%, to 94.07, while Champion Enterprises slipped six cents, or
0.7%, to 9.21.
Investment-services stocks also were weak. Investment Technology Group (NYSE) lost 95 cents, or 7%, to
12.60, after Jefferies &Co. downgraded the stock to hold from buy. Knight Trading Group fell 55 cents, or
4.9%, to 10.67, and LaBranche &Co. (NYSE) tumbled 35 cents, or 4.1%, to 8.28.
Internet stocks, which were among last week's poorest performers, resumed their slide. Digital River lost
1.32, or 4.2%, to 30.40, while CNET Networks edged down three cents, or 0.3%, to 9.93.
Stocks in the computer-chip group also declined: Silicon Storage Technology lost 1.07, or 9.1%, to 10.69;
LTX fell 76 cents, or 8.3%, to 8.39; and Cohu dropped 1.30, or 7%, to 17.30.
Shares of 99 Cents Only Stores (NYSE) plunged 6.38, or 31%, to 14.10. The City of Commerce, Calif.,
discount retailer lowered its second-quarter outlook, citing distribution problems, high gas prices and
post-strike promotions from Southern California supermarkets.
Drugstore.com plummeted 1.85, or 38%, to 3.06. The Bellevue, Wash., provider of online health and beauty
products lowered its second-quarter and 2004 forecasts. In addition, the company said its chief executive, Kal
Raman, has decided to leave the company.
Cyberonics rose 1.19, or 6.5%, to 19.58, based on renewed optimism that a hearing today regarding the
Houston company's implantable device to treat depression will go well.
Shaw Group (NYSE) fell 2.23, or 18%, to 10.05. The Baton Rouge, La., provider of construction services late
Thursday said the Securities and Exchange Commission launched an informal inquiry into the company.
Vail Resorts (NYSE) climbed 1.23, or 7.9%, to 16.80. The Colorado ski-resort operator said its fiscal
third-quarter net income soared from the year-earlier period.
Audiovox lost 1.09, or 7.1%, to 14.37. The Hauppauge, N.Y., consumer-electronics company agreed to sell
its wireless handset unit to large-cap UTStarcom for $165.1 million.

Forest Oil, Vintage Petroleum Rise as Crude Rallies
Small-Stock Focus
Forest Oil, Vintage Petroleum Rise as Crude Rallies
By Cynthia Schreiber Dow Jones Newswires
672 words
11 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Small-capitalization stocks rose by a hair, underperforming the broad market amid a rise in
crude-oil prices.
The commodity's advance helped to place shares of oil companies among the session's strongest
performers. Forest Oil gained 81 cents, or 3.3%, to $25.33; Vintage Petroleum advanced 51 cents, or 3.3%,
to 16; and Unit rose 73 cents, or 2.6%, to 28.70, all on the New York Stock Exchange.
Conversely, airline stocks fell as oil prices rose. High fuel costs, investors fear, could hurt corporate profits.
AirTran Holdings fell 76 cents, or 5.3%, to 13.71 and Continental Airlines shed 44 cents, or 4.1%, to 10.34,
both on the Big Board. Northwest Airlines fell 59 cents, or 5.7%, to 9.86.
"The focus of the market over the past few days and over the next few weeks will continue to be on the
outlook for inflation, the outlook for energy prices and the debate on how high the [Federal Reserve] will
need to raise interest rates in the months ahead," said Michael Sheldon, chief market strategist at Spencer
Clarke LLC.
The Russell 2000 Index of small stocks rose 0.54, or 0.09%, to 569.12, part of a weekly gain of 1.36, or 0.2%.
The S&P SmallCap 600 Index edged up 0.19, or 0.07%, to 283.48, and gained 2.55, or 0.9%, on the week.
A stock upgrade of large-cap Nucor forged gains in the steel group. Ryerson Tull advanced 22 cents, or
1.6%, to 14; Carpenter Technology rose 45 cents, or 1.5%, to 31.08; and Allegheny Technologies closed up
23 cents, or 1.7%, at 13.86, all on the NYSE.
Northwest Pipe rose 1.15, or 7.1%, to 17.35. The Portland, Ore., maker of welded steel pipes said it expects
second-quarter earnings of 40 cents to 45 cents a share, up from six cents a share a year earlier.
After heavy selling on Wednesday, gold stocks enjoyed a bounce. On the Big Board, Coeur D'Alene Mines
rose eight cents, or 2%, to 4.19 and Meridian Gold gained 18 cents, or 1.5%, to 12. Canyon Resources
advanced 10 cents, or 2.9%, to 3.60 on the American Stock Exchange.
Biotechnology stocks were weak after large-cap Martek Biosciences reported disappointing earnings.
Regeneron Pharmaceuticals tumbled 1.16, or 12%, to 8.65; NPS Pharmaceuticals declined 1.18, or 5.4%, to
20.68; and Immunomedics lost 28 cents, or 5.2%, to 5.16.
White Electronic Designs fell 83 cents, or 14%, to 5.16. The Phoenix maker of microelectronic and display
components and systems for the military and commercial markets said a reallocation of military spending may
force it to report an unexpected loss for the third quarter.
Shares of 21st Century Holding climbed 1.21, or 6.3%, to 20.50. The Plantation, Fla., financial-services
holding company backed its forecasts for revenue and profit growth for 2004 and 2005.
Analogic lost 5.29, or 11%, to 42.03. The Peabody, Mass., maker of medical-diagnostic and security
equipment said third-quarter earnings fell 74% from the same quarter last year.
JoS. A. Bank Clothiers shed 2.64, or 7.9%, to 30.97. The Hampstead, Md., men's clothing retailer said it has
received a subpoena from the New York attorney general that requests documents related to the company's
advertising and in-store promotions.
BindView Development rose 53 cents, or 20%, to 3.18. Janney Montgomery Scott initiated investment
coverage of the stock with a "buy" rating, saying the Houston software company is poised to benefit from
strong growth in the market for vulnerability assessment and management.

revenue growth forecast for the second quarter.

Canyon Resources, OmniVision Join Flight From Gold and Chips
Small-Stock Focus
Canyon Resources, OmniVision Join Flight From Gold and Chips
By Cynthia Schreiber Dow Jones Newswires
460 words
10 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold and computer-chip stocks led small-capitalization shares lower as investors pared
positions in the broad market.
Gold producers saw their shares tumble as the price of gold fell amid a strengthening dollar. Gold prices
tend to move down when the dollar strengthens because the dollar-denominated metal becomes more
expensive to foreign buyers.
In the gold group, Coeur D'Alene Mines lost 24 cents, or 5.5%, to $4.11, and Hecla Mining fell 34 cents, or
5.4%, to 5.98, both on the New York Stock Exchange. Canyon Resources dropped 29 cents, or 7.7%, to 3.50
on the American Stock Exchange.
Stock downgrades of large caps Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing and United Microelectronics weighed
on the computer-chip group. M-Systems Flash Disk Pioneers shed 1.10, or 6.5%, to 15.77; Micrel lost 77
cents, or 5.5%, to 13.24; and Transmeta fell 15 cents, or 6.4%, to 2.19.
OmniVision Technologies plunged 7.84, or 31%, to 17.63. The chip concern issued earnings and revenue
estimates for the first quarter that were below analysts' expectations. The company also said it may have
understated fiscal 2003 and 2004 revenue.
Overall declines came in light trading volume as investors wait for the Federal Reserve meeting on interest
rates and the handover of power in Iraq. "We've been in a drift mode since mid-April because at the end of
June and in early July, you are going to have some huge market-making events," said Tim Heekin, director of
trading at Thomas Weisel Partners.
The Russell 2000 Index of small stocks lost 9.33, or 1.6%, to 568.58, and the Standard &Poor's SmallCap
600 Index fell 3.56, or 1.2%, to 283.29. The Nasdaq Composite Index of large-cap and small-cap stocks shed
32.92, or 1.6%, to 1990.61.
Homebuilders were weak after the Mortgage Bankers Association said its weekly market composite index of
mortgage-loan applications fell 8.9% from a week earlier. Beazer Homes USA lost 3.41, or 3.4%, to 96.68,
and Champion Enterprises fell 25 cents, or 2.6%, to 9.34, both on the Big Board.
A stock downgrade of large-cap Genentech hurt the biotech group. Nabi Biopharmaceuticals lost 1.43, or
8.7%, to 15.01 and Enzon Pharmaceuticals shed 1.13, or 8.2%, to 12.63.
Jakks Pacific jumped 1.15, or 6.1%, to 19.90. The Malibu, Calif., company said it expanded its licensing
agreement with large-cap Marvel Enterprises to produce TV games based on The Incredible Hulk and other
characters.

Centex, Newmont Give Up Ground --- D.R. Horton, Toll Brothers, Barrick Gold Also Drop After Greenspan Comments
Abreast of the Market
Centex, Newmont Give Up Ground --- D.R. Horton, Toll Brothers, Barrick Gold Also Drop After
Greenspan Comments
By Karen Talley Dow Jones Newswires
682 words
9 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Stocks rose as the price of oil fell and investors accepted stern comments about interest
rates and inflation from Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan.
But Mr. Greenspan's comments did appear to hurt home builders and gold producers.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average gained 41.44 points, or 0.40%, to 10432.52; the Nasdaq Composite Index
rose 2.91 points, or 0.14%, to 2023.53; and the S&P 500 added 1.76, or 0.15%, to 1142.18.
Mr. Greenspan "allayed some of the fears that the Fed wouldn't be as vigilant on the inflation front," said
Michael Farr, president of money manager Farr, Miller &Washington. Traders also said a significant drop in
crude-oil futures was a key factor in stocks' recovery.
Still, home builders were hurt by Mr. Greenspan's comments and news that small-cap Dominion Homes
(Nasdaq) cut second-quarter sales projection because of higher interest rates. Centex dropped $1.70, or
3.4%, to $47.95; D.R. Horton fell 99 cents, or 3.3%, to 29.28; and Toll Brothers fell 1.20, or 2.8%, to 41.01.
The prospect of interest rates rising faster than expected lifted the dollar, causing some selling of
precious-metals stocks, which are seen as a safe haven when the dollar is weaker. Newmont Mining lost 48
cents, or 1.2%, to 39.49; Barrick Gold fell 20 cents, or 1%, to 20.28; and AngloGold Ashanti lost 60 cents, or
1.7%, to 33.80.
In other stock action, Kmart Holding (Nasdaq) jumped 4.33, or 7%, to 66.33 and Sears, Roebuck rose 2.11,
or 5.5%, to 40.41 on industry scuttlebutt that Sears plans to buy some of Kmart's stores. Spokesmen at Sears
and Kmart declined to comment, but the talk came on the heels of Kmart's agreement last week to sell as
many as 24 store locations to Home Depot for as much as $365 million. Dow component Home Depot
advanced 18 cents to 35.93.
Barr Pharmaceuticals fell 2.53, or 6.1%, to 38.80. Analysts at First Albany and Friedman Billings Ramsey said
they see increased competition for the generic-drug maker's contraceptive products.
WebMD (Nasdaq) rose 56 cents, or 6.4%, to 9.36 on optimism about what the online health-information
provider might say after the closing bell at a Goldman Sachs health-care conference.
But another Internet company, Ask Jeeves (Nasdaq), fell 2.05, or 4.8%, to 40.62. The Web-search provider
registered to sell $400 million of securities that include common and preferred stock, raising concerns about
dilution.
Guidant rose 2.43, or 4.5%, to 56.61. Shares of the medical-device maker, which have been falling, were
upgraded to "buy" from "hold" by Deutsche Bank Securities, which said the stock was at an attractive entry
level.
First Marblehead gained 1.55, or 4.5%, to 35.85. The provider of services for processing and selling
education loans raised its outlook for fiscal 2004, citing fees it expects from student-loan securitizations.
Red Hat (Nasdaq) dropped 1.15, or 4.1%, 27.03. Banc of America Securities downgraded the stock to
"neutral" from "buy," saying it believes upside to fiscal-first-quarter results is already priced into shares of the
provider of open-source software, including Linux.
Nuveen Investments rose 96 cents, or 3.6%, to 28.01. Some money managers say mutual-fund companies
that haven't drawn regulators' ire will benefit as investors buy the stocks.

--- Page 36 ---
Page 36 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Tribune fell 1.76, or 3.6%, to 46.92. The media company will cut staff and spending because of unexpectedly
slow advertising growth.
Apple Computer (Nasdaq) rose 54 cents, or 1.8%, to 30.35. Pacific Crest Securities started coverage of the
stock at "outperform," saying initiatives the company is taking in digital entertainment and media should
accelerate revenue growth and profitability.

Continental Airlines Posts 8.2% Jump
Small-Stock Focus
Continental Airlines Posts 8.2% Jump
By Cynthia Schreiber Dow Jones Newswires
546 words
3 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Airline stocks helped small-capitalization shares advance, despite losses in the semiconductor
group.
Business updates from a host of computer-chip companies, including large-caps Fairchild Semiconductor
International and Xilinx, failed to impress investors, who sent most stocks in the group lower.
Triquint Semiconductor, which reaffirmed its second-quarter revenue forecast, lost 18 cents, or 3.2%, to
$5.50, as analysts took issue with the chip supplier's optical-networks business.
Brooks Automation fell 1.06, or 5.2%, to 19.40. Smith Barney initiated investment coverage of the chip
concern's stock with a "sell" rating, calling it "overvalued."
Airline stocks, however, took off as the price of oil pulled back from Tuesday's record: Continental Airlines
rose 86 cents, or 8.2%, to 11.30, and Delta Air Lines gained 39 cents, or 6.9%, to 6.08, both on the New York
Stock Exchange. Northwest Airlines climbed 49 cents, or 5%, to 10.38.
As investors watched the price of oil slide, they also waited for the May employment report, due tomorrow.
Barry Hyman, equity market strategist at Ehrenkrantz King Nussbaum, said the report "is probably the last
critical data point that the Fed can use to justify a [rate] increase in June."
All told, the Russell 2000 Index of small stocks gained 1.07, or 0.19%, to 573.56, and the S&P SmallCap 600
Index rose 0.71, or 0.25%, to 283.50. The Nasdaq Composite Index of large-cap and small-cap stocks shed
1.79, or 0.09%, to 1988.98.
Biotechnology stocks rose after a positive assessment of large-cap MedImmune. Savient Pharmaceuticals
jumped 17 cents, or 7.3%, to 2.51; Cell Therapeutics gained 45 cents, or 6.3%, to 7.57; and Cell Genesys
rose 37 cents, or 3.6%, to 10.60.
Oil-services stocks pulled back in tandem with crude oil. Superior Energy Services fell 13 cents, or 1.4%, to
9.15, and Maverick Tube shed 26 cents, or 1.1%, to 22.61, both on the Big Board. Global Industries lost 28
cents, or 4.9%, to 5.42.
Gold stocks fell as the precious metal's price dropped. Hecla Mining shed 23 cents, or 3.6%, to 6.15, and
Meridian Gold lost 26 cents, or 2%, to 12.58, both on the NYSE. Bema Gold slipped 6 cents, or 2.1%, to
2.75, on the American Stock Exchange.
Some copper-related stocks also slipped after Morgan Stanley lowered its earnings estimates on large-cap
Freeport-McMoRan Copper &Gold. Olin (NYSE) eased 9 cents, or 0.5%, to 16.53.
Allos Therapeutics shot up 87 cents, or 45%, to 2.82. The Westminster, Colo., company's cancer treatment
RSR13, initially rejected by a federal panel of cancer experts, won partial approval from the Food and Drug
Administration.
Cardiac Science rose 12 cents, or 5.2%, to 2.42. Chindex International will market the Irvine, Calif.,
company's Powerheart-brand automated public-access defibrillators on an exclusive basis to hospitals and
medical-supply dealers throughout China. Chindex, a health-care company in Bethesda, Md., closed up 24
cents, or 2.6%, to 9.54.

High Oil Prices Cast a Shadow Over the World's Recovery
Going Global
High Oil Prices Cast a Shadow Over the World's Recovery
By Michael R. Sesit
921 words
2 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Paris -- AT TIMES BOTH the bulwark and the bane of global economic growth, oil -- known as everything
from black gold to Texas Tea -- is again emerging as the make-it-or-break-it factor for the world economy.
Crude oil is hovering at its highest level in more than two decades, and yesterday futures prices rose to a new
high of $42.33 a barrel, up $2.45, on the New York Mercantile Exchange. The surge in oil prices has caught
investors' attention, even though oil has traded much higher in the past when adjusted for inflation.
Crude-oil prices quadrupled during the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries embargo of late 1973
and early 1974, nearly tripled with the 1979 Iranian Revolution, and more than doubled in 1990 just before the
first Gulf War. The key question today: Will a world still in the midst of a fragile economic recovery soon be
confronting yet another oil "shock"?
Economists credit a variety of causes for the recent surge in oil prices, including fears of potential supply
disruptions relating to heightened Middle East tensions. In addition, there is the unreliability of Iraqi exports,
and the weekend siege at the Saudi Arabian oil hub of Khobar has heightened fears of instability inside the
world's largest oil exporter. Another cause is the ever-growing global demand for oil, led by strong Chinese
and U.S. economic growth coupled with tight U.S. energy inventories. Some analysts also attribute at least
some of the recent surge in oil prices to speculation by hedge funds and bank proprietary traders.
Last but not least is a long-range concern: "Oil is a nonrenewable resource, and as the world economy
expands over the years, we are consuming more and more of it," says John Ip, an economist at Morley Fund
Management in London.
If earlier shocks are any guide, the result could be higher inflation and a global recession. Much depends on
whether price increases are sustained. Short-term spikes in oil prices usually have only a limited impact on
the world economy. But, if the increases hold for an extended period, they could reduce world economic
growth by roughly half a percentage point, maybe more, says Mr. Ip.
Simply put, the more money consumers spend on petroleum products, the less they have for other goods.
And the more oil prices increase the cost of finished goods, the bigger the reduction in consumers'
purchasing power. At the corporate level, higher oil prices spell greater costs, lower investment, and reduced
profits -- and eventually layoffs.
Globally, the beneficiaries of higher oil prices are, of course, the major oil producers, including Saudi Arabia,
Nigeria, Kuwait, Venezuela, Iran, Mexico and Russia. Hurt most are oil-dependent Asian developing
countries. On average, they use more than twice as much oil as developed countries to produce a single unit
of economic output.
Among the major industrial nations, Japan is hit with a double-whammy: It is a huge oil importer, and 45% of
its exports go to other Asian countries. Japan's economic growth rate would be substantially slower in 2005
and 2006 if oil remains near $40 a barrel, rather than a base comparison of $28, according to a recent
analysis by Dresdner Kleinwort Wasserstein. Meanwhile, Japanese inflation would rise.
Under the same scenario, high oil prices would slow growth in U.S. gross domestic product by 0.2 percentage
point in 2005 and half a point in 2006, while inflation would be 0.6 and 0.7 percentage point higher in 2005
and 2006, than would be the case if oil were priced at $28 a barrel. The 12-nation euro zone would have
similar inflation results, but $40 oil would shave growth by 0.3 percentage point in 2005 and another 0.4
point in 2006.
Although high oil prices do the world economy no favors, some strategists think investors may find an upside
in energy-company stocks.

--- Page 50 ---
Page 50 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Dresdner analysts like Spain's Repsol YPF, Norway's Statoil, France's Total, Italy's Eni and Exxon Mobil in
the U.S. George Lee, an analyst at Odey Asset Management in London, also says oil-service companies,
such as Schlumberger and Norway's Smedvig, are worth a look. In contrast, Morley fund managers are either
neutral or short on the oil sector.
There are differences from past shocks, some economists argue. Today's prices reflect increased demand,
not reduced supply that characterized past shocks.
Moreover, Michael Lewis, head of commodity research at Deutsche Bank in London, contends that by various
measures oil isn't so expensive. Oil would have to climb to above $50 a barrel to match where it stood during
the 1990-1991 Gulf War, in inflation-adjusted terms, he says. "And relative to incomes, oil prices would have
to rise to just over $80 a barrel to bring them into line with the 1974-to-1985 period," he says.
He also calls oil cheap when compared to other staples. He points out that in Safeway Inc. supermarkets in
Maryland, if shoppers wanted a barrel of milk, they'd pay $138 a barrel at current prices. A barrel of orange
juice would be $192 and a barrel of Evian mineral water would be $246. But oranges grow on trees; oil
doesn't. SUVs don't run on mineral water. And, Mr. Lewis acknowledges, "Nobody's blowing up cows."
---
Jon E. Hilsenrath contributed to this article.

Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...and Not: Crude Held the Sweet Spot in May
--- Rising Oil Prices Resonated With Stock, Bond Markets, Causing Most Assets to Fall
By Craig Karmin
652 words
1 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
If you didn't buy crude in the month of May, chances are you didn't make money.
The price of a barrel shot up more than 5% during a month in which it set repeated record highs. The higher
prices in turn helped generate a ripple effect throughout global markets that caused a wide swath of stocks,
bonds and other once-highflying assets to retreat. Following a selloff in April for most assets, the rout last
month has global investors a bit unnerved and pining for the bullish days in the second half of 2003.
"We've had an incredible winning streak, but we're past that now," said Steve Bleiberg, global strategist for
Citigroup Asset Management. "We're back in an environment where there are good months and there are
bad months."
May was clearly the latter. With continuing concerns about China tightening monetary policy to slow down
its economy before it overheats, and with the U.S. Federal Reserve widely expected to begin its own
tightening cycle this month, previous big winners sold off. Emerging-market stocks and bonds, most
Japanese stocks and many European markets ended the month lower than where they started. Chinese
stocks fared better than most, however, and Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index rose just more than 2%.
One of the hardest-hit national markets was India's, where a panicky reaction to election results caused the
Bombay Sensex Index to fall 17% one day last month. The market rebounded after India's Congress party
selected Manmohan Singh, a well-regarded reformer, to be the new prime minister. But the government's
stated intentions to scale back the privatization program promoted by the previous government raised fresh
concerns, and the Sensex fell nearly 16% for the month.
Oil and commodities were the notable exceptions to the pervading gloom. The Reuters CRB Commodity
Futures Index rose 1.7%, and gold-bullion prices edged up after tumbling in April. The price of crude surged
above $41, putting pressure on oil-importing nations around the world. Higher oil prices reflected a new surge
in demand as economies world-wide continued to expand. Less encouraging has been bottlenecks in
distribution, though these are considered more of a temporary problem.
Most worrisome has been the so-called terrorism premium -- the contribution to higher oil prices from
renewed fears that an attack could cripple a Middle East supplier of oil. Some investors fret that this premium
may remain a factor in global markets for years to come. Sure enough, terrorist attacks against oil-related
targets this past weekend in Saudi Arabia could further boost the oil premium, even though supplies coming
out of the kingdom appeared to be safe.
"In essence, it's like a permanent tax," Mr. Bleiberg said of the oil price.
Still, not all analysts are ready to write off the year for global markets and assets. Michael Hartnett, global
macrostrategist for Merrill Lynch, noted that world-wide economic growth still looks healthy, and corporate
earnings, from the U.S. to Japan, have been coming in largely ahead of expectations. "There is some hysteria
here about oil prices," Mr. Hartnett said. "But even if prices stay at $35 to $40 a barrel, that's irritating, not
disastrous."
Indeed, U.S. assets did better than most in May. The Standard &Poor's 500 Stock Index was up more than
1%, and the dollar continued to hold its own after a rough time last year, falling about 1.1%. The Dow Jones
U.S. Technology Index gained more than 5% on the month but is still down for the year; some investors
suggested that the sector avoided the May selloff by taking its lumps earlier.

Metals Stores Prove Magnets For Consumers
Small Business
Metals Stores Prove Magnets For Consumers
By Paul Glader
1,116 words
1 June 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Pittsburgh -- WALT CHUBECK needed a perforated sheet of steel, not a big piece, but just enough to build
sluice boxes that he and his 10-year-old son would use on a coming gold-prospecting trip to Montana, Idaho
and Canada.
He knew just where to go: Metal Supermarkets. "This is the one place I knew would have it," said Mr.
Chubeck, as he made his way through aisles lined with sheet metal stacked on blue racks and 12-foot bars of
aluminum rising out of bins.
Metal Supermarkets calls itself "the convenience store of the metals industry" and operates on the notion
that, sometimes, customers want a 4-inch bar of stainless steel instead of a truckload, a sheet of diamond
plate steel to make running boards for their SUV, or an aluminum pole to support a birdhouse in their
backyard.
With 82 franchises around the world, Metal Supermarkets is filling the void between modern hardware stores
such as Home Depot Inc. and Lowe's Cos., which have limited selections of metals, and huge so-called
service centers such as Chicago-based Ryerson Tull Inc., which cater to industrial buyers and often require
bulk orders.
"We are getting a lot of business that they don't want to service," says Mike Young, the chief executive of
Metals Supermarkets, which is based in Toronto. Mr. Young expects world-wide sales to increase almost
10% this year, to $80 million from $73 million, and the company plans to add five franchises.
The chain, started in the late 1980s, is benefiting from the resurgence in home-remodeling projects fueled, in
part, by low interest rates and the popularity of home-improvement shows like "Trading Spaces" and
"Extreme Makeover: Home Edition," along with E.W. Scripps Co.'s home-related channels DIY-Do It Yourself
Network and Home &Garden Television. But while a metallic look is popular in many home designs, metal is
one material that has proved difficult to work with and hard to find for the average do-it-yourselfer.
Customers pay a premium for convenience. On average, for a given item, franchises charge about 60% more
than steelmakers and 30% more than service centers. "It's like a 7-Eleven instead of a Cub Foods. You will
pay more for your toilet paper but there is one on the corner," says Michael Boschelli, a sales manager for the
Earle M. Jorgensen Co.'s 640,000-square-foot metal service center facility in Schaumburg, Ill. Jorgensen
supplies metal to Metals Supermarkets, which average about 3,000 square feet.
Mr. Boschelli says customers should know exactly what kind of metal they want because many types of
metals tend to look the same. Carbon steel tends to rust, he says, after six months of sitting on a shelf, while
stainless steel and aluminum do not. Metal Supermarkets officials say 95% of franchise owners have a
background in the metals industry and can explain the difference between materials to customers.
Indeed, Somi Ahluwalia, who owns the Pittsburgh franchise of the chain, was president of steel companies in
India and the U.S. before he bought a franchise in 1997 and opened a 3,000-square-foot Metals
Supermarkets store. With sales expected to nearly double this year, he is hiring two new employees and
considering opening two more locations in the city.
There are no shopping carts, but forklifts move through the aisles. Saws are near the loading door to cut
metals to customer's specifications. A delivery truck is available to drop off heavier orders for people who
can't fit them in the family station wagon.
Typical customers are engineers in charge of maintenance for manufacturing companies, but there is also a
steady stream of do-it-yourself, home-improvement buffs. As the industrial look seems to be growing in some
home design, a few more consumers are stopping in to buy sheet metal to make back splashes above the
sinks in their kitchens. Other homeowners have bought metal to use as kick-pads for a front door.

--- Page 53 ---
Page 53 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Rich Moore, 57 years old, stopped in the store here recently to buy a $6.13 steel part for a drive-shaft loop he
was building for a 1969 Dodge Dart he drag races with other "guys who grew up in the 1960s." Making the
part himself cost $20, while buying the part from an auto parts store would have cost $150.
Artists sometimes buy metal to design their own gigantic wind chimes. Bar owners buy copper sheets to use
as counter tops and brass railings for walkways and bar footrests, while strip-club owners buy brass poles for
their dancers to twirl around, as do some gym owners, who offer fitness classes called "cardio strip tease," in
addition to spinning, yoga and Pilates.
Mr. Ahluwalia is seeing more government-contract business with the Coast Guard, Army and local authorities.
He recently sold batches of steel to an Army unit in Pennsylvania that is beefing up its Hummers with extra
protection in case they are dispatched to Iraq. "We had an old lady call up and say, `My house is falling, what
should I do?' " says Mr. Ahluwalia. "We told her to call an architect and we would be happy to sell her
materials if she needs them." While 80% of Mr. Ahluwalia's business arrives by phone, 20% walks into the
store.
Franchises do have some competitors in local independent metal stores and Metal Express, a Chicago
company that is owned by a larger service-center company. Metal Express has about 15 franchises and does
business over the Internet.
Several steel companies and independent entrepreneurs started up Internet-based companies -- ranging in
name from clickforsteel to MaterialNet and using a variety of platforms and models -- to sell steel in
unconventional ways during the tech boom in the 1990s. But most of those efforts either flopped, changed
names or are still struggling to turn a profit. U.S. Steel Corp. closed its start-up venture, Straightline Source,
last year; the operation had lost $134 million since its inception in 2001.
Mr. Ahluwalia believes some people find buying metal online daunting and prefer to do some up-close and
personal browsing. "You want to feel the metal and see what you need," he says. He has reported no
problems with shoplifting at his supermarket -- and no need for metal detectors at the doors -- mainly
because staff could easily recognize someone trying to sneak a cumbersome sheet of steel or a bar of
aluminum out the door.

Drivers Are Switching to Car Pools --- Mass Transit Gains Favor, As Consumers Seek Ways To Fight Gasoline Prices
Cars
Drivers Are Switching to Car Pools --- Mass Transit Gains Favor, As Consumers Seek Ways To Fight
Gasoline Prices
By Russell Gold and Susan Warren
781 words
26 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Drivers are increasingly hopping on mass transit, car-pooling and seeking out special deals to counter rising
gasoline prices, which nationally crossed $2 for a gallon of regular for the first time this month and still are
rising.
Though gasoline isn't at record highs after adjusting for inflation, the $2 mark is turning out to be an
important threshold.
The agency that runs the four airports in the Los Angeles area, where gasoline prices are among the highest
in the nation, has seen a 20% increase in employees who bike, car pool or take a van to work. "People can't
afford to drive anymore," says Devon Deming, car-pool program manager for Los Angeles World Airports.
A focus group of suburban Seattle residents last week told the Washington State Department of
Transportation that they had begun to car-pool and avoid unnecessary trips. And riders on the 72-mile
commuter rail line from Miami to West Palm Beach, Fla., increased 5.5% in April from a year earlier.
It's easy to see the attraction. The national average for regular unleaded gasoline was $2.06 a gallon this
week, up 55 cents in six months. Adjusting for inflation, gasoline hasn't been this expensive since the fall of
1985, long before the three-ton Hummer.
Today, consumers are spending about 2.8% of their disposable income on gasoline, and the amount may rise
to 3% before the climb is done. But that is still well below the 5% spent in the early 1980s, when gasoline
nearly hit $3 a gallon in inflation-adjusted terms. "This implies that current gasoline prices could rise
significantly before affecting consumer spending habits," says Bruce Lanni, an energy analyst at A.G.
Edwards who compiled the statistics.
Still, groups that encourage sharing rides have seen a spike in interest. Connie Dice, vice president of
Rideshare Co., a Hartford, Conn., nonprofit that coordinates car pooling and van sharing, says that recent
gasoline price increases haven't affected participation. But the number of calls to Rideshare's toll-free number
has jumped 41% over the past six weeks compared with the year-earlier period.
One caller, Frankie Twining, started taking the van to her job as an Aetna customer-service representative
earlier this month. "Instead of going over 500 miles a week, I'm only driving 150 miles. That's a huge saving
on gas," she says.
The popularity of Boeing Co.'s alternative commute programs has increased as the price of gasoline surged
in the Pacific Northwest, where the company has 52,000 employees. "In recent months there has been a
distinct and sustained rise in participation," says spokesman Dean Tougas.
Some people are taking more unusual measures to avoid the gasoline pumps. Kenneth Senkel, a Dallas-area
law student, and his wife decided to convert their 2002 Dodge Ram pickup to run on propane fuel. "We've just
watched these prices go up and up and up," he says. "We were complaining about the high prices, and I got
the idea in my head about just converting this thing." The conversion will cost more than $2,000, but his truck
will travel the same distance on 76 cents of propane as it would on a gallon of gasoline.
Consumers are also on the hunt for the cheapest gasoline. Costco Wholesale Corp., known for its discount
prices, said drivers pumped 20% more gasoline at it is stores in April than the year before. Filling her
sport-utility vehicle Friday at the RaceTrac gasoline station in Grand Prairie, Texas, Ruth Scott, a U.S. Postal
Service supervisor, says she had hoped to get to the Wal-Mart store five miles away, where gasoline is $1.86
a gallon, but settled for $1.89 because she was running on fumes. "I'm constantly thinking about how much
gas I've got and how long I can make the tank last," she says. She says she is reducing trips to the store and
combining other errands with her work commute to conserve.

--- Page 59 ---
Page 59 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Meanwhile, mass-transit agencies and environmental advocates are gearing up to use pricey gasoline in
advertising campaigns. "It's a wonderful sales pitch," says Mindy Mize, who helps encourage use of
alternative-fuel cars in North Texas.
But will it last? Many observers think such efforts will fade as soon as prices retreat. "It does swing up," says
John Cox, president of the Partnership, a Southern California group promoting mass transit and
environmentally friendly transportation technology. "But it also swings back."

Oil, Rates and Iraq Keep Stock Indexes On Road to Nowhere
Thursday's Markets
Oil, Rates and Iraq Keep Stock Indexes On Road to Nowhere
By E.S. Browning
395 words
21 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WALL STREET calls it a buyers' strike, and it isn't a pretty sight. Investors stay away, volume dries up and
stock indexes go almost nowhere.
Now it has happened for the second time this week, as worries about oil prices, interest rates, inflation and
the U.S. involvement in Iraq combine to keep people away from stocks in general.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell a minuscule 0.07 point, less than one-thousandth of one percent, at
9937.64. The blue chips languished below 10000 for a fourth consecutive day, down 5% on the year.
Only 1.21 billion shares traded on the New York Stock Exchange, the fifth-lightest day of the year. Nasdaq
trading, at 1.5 billion shares, was the year's sixth-sleepiest. In fact, five of this year's six slowest Nasdaq days
have come since the middle of last week.
Crude-oil futures fell 58 cents to $40.92, but remained near Monday's record of $41.55, calming no one's
nerves.
"There is so much anxiety in this marketplace about external issues that you see this light volume," said Jay
Suskind, head trader at brokerage house Ryan Beck in Florham Park, N.J. "People don't know whether to
buy or sell, so you are seeing little of either."
Treasury bonds rose after two economic reports -- on business activity in the Philadelphia region and on last
week's unemployment claims -- suggested that economic growth is slightly less robust than was thought.
The Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.08%, or 1.58 points, to 1896.59, down 5% this year. The broad Standard
&Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.05%, or 0.51 point, to 1089.19, down 2% on the year.
A day after posting its biggest gain in two months, gold declined. In major U.S. market action:
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 16/32, or $5 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, fell to 4.717%. The 30-year bond was up 28/32 to yield 5.431%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 112.89 yen, down from 113.07; the euro fell against the
dollar to $1.1936 from $1.2011.

A Stock Rally Stalls in the U.S. --- Blue Chips Give Back Early Gains As Rising Oil Prices, Interest Rates Continue to Weigh On the Market
Wednesday's Markets
A Stock Rally Stalls in the U.S. --- Blue Chips Give Back Early Gains As Rising Oil Prices, Interest
Rates Continue to Weigh On the Market
By E.S. Browning
1,052 words
20 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCK INDEXES rallied around the world except by day's end in the U.S., where a strong morning advance
ran out of steam, leaving the Dow Jones Industrial Average below 10000 for a third consecutive day.
Beaten down by rises in oil prices and market interest rates, both of which seemed to threaten the strong
economy, the blue chips yesterday closed down 30.80 points, or 0.31%, to 9937.71 -- and are now down 5%
on the year.
The Dow had been up 124.70 points at its morning high, near 10100, but couldn't hold the gains and began to
fall in midafternoon.
"The market rolled over and really didn't put up a fight the whole way down," said stock trader Michael Driscoll
at New York brokerage firm Bear Stearns. "It is just a continuation of the problems and the things that have
been going on for the last couple of months," he added, citing the continuing rise in interest rates and oil
prices, and "just a growing pall over the market when it comes to Iraq."
As has repeatedly happened this spring, signs of strong earnings and robust economic growth were
overwhelmed by surging oil prices, rising inflation and international unrest.
The day began on a buoyant note, with sharp U.S. stock gains fueled by rising markets abroad. Stocks were
up 1.4% in London, 2.2% in Frankfurt and 2.4% in Tokyo. Oil prices were falling and traders were celebrating
Hewlett-Packard's forecast of stronger-than-expected sales to come.
But as the day wore on, oil prices turned up again, finishing at $41.50 a barrel, just short of Monday's record
$41.55. News reports said a U.S. helicopter had mistakenly killed more than 40 Iraqis who were celebrating
at a desert wedding by firing guns. The yield of the 10-year Treasury note -- a benchmark for many market
interest rates -- rose again, pushing back above the much-watched 4.75% level. A report from the Mortgage
Bankers Association indicated that, with interest rates rising, mortgage applications are declining, softening
one of the economy's strongest underpinnings.
Gold rose $7.10 to $382.90 an ounce in New York, its biggest gain in two months, although it remained well
below its 15-year high of $427.80, hit in April.
Technology stocks, helped by Hewlett-Packard and by upbeat comments from the chief executive of
International Business Machines, clung to small gains. Hewlett-Packard and IBM both are Dow components.
Hewlett finished with a gain of more than 3% and IBM more than 1%.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, filled with technology stocks (although not including either Hewlett or IBM)
finished with a gain of 0.02%, or 0.35 point, at 1898.17, still down 5% this year. The broad Standard &Poor's
500-stock index fell 0.26%, or 2.81 points, to 1088.68, down 2% on the year.
The volatile day did at least generate more-normal trading volumes, after an anemic Tuesday, with more than
1.5 billion shares trading on the New York Stock Exchange.
Traders said that the strong foreign markets brought foreign investors back into the U.S. in the morning,
helping support U.S. stocks. Some foreign investment institutions had been selling U.S. stocks to raise capital
and cover losses at home when foreign markets sagged on Monday, and now they were putting some of that
money to work.
Short-sellers, who bet on stock declines, saw the big gains and hurried to unwind their bets, which led them
to buy stocks, pushing the market up further as the day began. Foreign markets were helped by indications
that India's new prime minister will encourage development, and that Japanese growth has been stronger

--- Page 64 ---
Page 64 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
than expected. Two senior Chinese officials hinted at a Beijing financial forum that they aren't planning to
raise interest rates to slow the economy.
Although U.S. stocks failed to match the performance of their foreign relations, some investors said they
remain hopeful that the U.S. will rebound before long. The optimists pointed out that the much-watched
Standard &Poor's 500-stock index is managing to avoid plunging significantly below its 200-day moving
average.
"We are putting in a base," said Joseph Keating, chief investment officer at money-management firm
AmSouth Asset Management, an arm of AmSouth Bankcorp. in Birmingham, Ala. Markets have been roiled
by "the tug of war going on between the rise in interest rates and the continuing growth in earnings," he said,
adding that he thinks the Federal Reserve will keep rate increases mild, pleasing investors.
"I really think the Fed will pursue a very, very gradual policy and will stay behind the curve," Mr. Keating said.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the Big Board, where 1.53 billion shares traded, 1,777 stocks rose and 1,511 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 13/32, or $4.0625 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.780%. The 30-year bond was down 19/32 to yield 5.493%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 113.07 yen, down from 114.18, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.2011 from $1.1949.

Big Money: When 5 Cents is Worth $3 Million
Big Money: When 5 Cents is Worth $3 Million
By Thaddeus Herrick
1,061 words
20 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE 1913 Liberty Head V nickel is to coin collectors what an important Picasso is for collectors of art. Today,
a New Orleans retailer is expected to disclose the sale of one coin for $3 million in a private transaction.
A rare coin with a storied past, the nickel provides a glimpse at the rising value of rare U.S. coins, up at least
20% in the past two years. Experts say coin prices tend to follow the price of gold, with both rising on
inflation fears. The last sale of a 1913 Liberty Head in 2001 garnered $1.85 million.
Like art, the Liberty Head V nickel represents more than a good return to collectors. Five were thought to
have been minted on the sly in 1913, when the U.S. switched to the Buffalo nickel after 30 years of the
Liberty Head. They turned up seven years later under a cloud of suspicion and have generated considerable
intrigue ever since.
"This is arguably the most famous U.S. coin," says John Albanese, president of John Albanese Numismatics,
in Far Hills, N.J., who worked as an intermediary broker for Blanchard &Co., the New Orleans retailer. "I could
see this being a $10 million coin someday. After all, we're living in a world of $100 million paintings."
Blanchard &Co. declined to disclose the name of the buyer or the seller.
The $3 million transaction is believed to be the largest ever of a U.S. coin by a retail brokerage house to a
buyer. Still, the 1913 Liberty Head V isn't the priciest U.S. coin. In 1999, an 1804 silver dollar sold for $4.14
million in an auction. Two years ago, a 1933 $20 gold piece went for $7.59 million at an auction.
A decade ago, those two coins probably would have been the only coins to have traded for more than $1
million, says Mike Sherman, vice president of the Professional Coin Grading Service in Newport Beach, Calif.
Today, Mr. Sherman says, a dozen U.S. coins probably could go for that.
After peaking in the late 1980s, the market for rare coins fell off in the 1990s as investors flooded into the
stock market. The coin market has gained momentum as the stock market stalled during the past several
years. Investors, spooked by steep losses in the stock market, have sought to deploy assets in other, more
tangible realms, such as art, coins and houses.
In some cases, recent price gains in rare coins have outpaced growth in gold prices, in part because a little
buying makes a big impact in the relatively small collectible coin market, which Mr. Sherman estimates at no
more than $3 billion. "It's like a boulder falling into a bathtub," he says, adding that investments of as little as
$50 million can move the market.
But Mr. Albanese says that like art, coin collectors who manage the best returns are those who buy with
investment as an afterthought. "The objective has to be building a great collection," he says.
On its own, the 1913 Liberty Head nickel is considered rather unspectacular. A flat coin with little detail or
relief, it features the Liberty Head on one side and a "V" on the other. Its value is in its history, especially the
uncertain origin of the five coins, which appear to have involved Samuel W. Brown, an employee of the
Philadelphia Mint where the nickels are believed to have been struck.
Word of the five coins surfaced in 1919, after Mr. Brown placed an advertisement in a publication called the
Numismatist seeking to buy them. Soon he announced that he had acquired them, but most suspect he had
obtained the coins at the Mint through an engraver named George T. Morgan. It is suspected that Mr. Morgan
produced the coins, an act that while unlawful today was legal at the time, according to a history compiled by
the Professional Coin Grading Service.
"Some pieces are made to generate interest among collectors," says Robert Hoge, Curator of American
Coins and Currency at the American Numismatic Society in New York. "The 1913 nickel was made with that
in mind."

--- Page 68 ---
Page 68 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
As Mr. Brown parted with his rare coins, they eventually fell into the hands of celebrated collectors, among
them Egypt's King Farouk and Jerry Buss, owner of the Los Angeles Lakers. Both King Farouk and Dr. Buss
at one time owned the coin sold today, the most publicized of the five, which is known for another previous
owner as the Olsen specimen.
Unlike some rare coins, which were minted especially for dignitaries, the 1913 Liberty Head nickel has earned
something of a populist reputation.
During the Depression, a Texas millionaire known as B. Max Mehl built an advertising campaign for his coin
encyclopedia around the nickel, offering a $50 reward for anyone who found one and generating a wave of
public interest in the coin. Streetcar conductors are said to have slowed traffic as they examined the coins
from passengers. "It became part of folklore," Mr. Albanese says.
In 1974, not long after the Olsen specimen sold for $100,000, the nickel was again thrust into mainstream
when it was featured on the television show "Hawaii Five-O." The episode, called the "$100,000 Nickel"
featured a thief who joined a con artist to steal the coin, only to get busted in the end.
In 1975, coin enthusiasts waited in long lines to see the nickel at the American Numismatics Association
convention in Los Angeles, where a guard stood close by.
One of the five nickels was deemed lost for 40 years, only to turn up in 2003 with great fanfare. It is on loan to
the American Numismatic Association in Colorado Springs, Colo., which owns another of the nickels. Another
is owned by the Smithsonian Institution in Washington, D.C. The remaining two, including the Olsen
specimen that just sold, are in private hands.
---
No Wooden Nickel
The three most expensive coins bought in U.S. sales:
COIN: 1933 Saint-Gaudens $20 Double Eagle, Sotheby's, 2002
SOLD: $7.59 million
COIN: 1804 Draped Bust Silver Dollar, Auction, 1999
SOLD: $4.14 million
COIN: 1913 Liberty Head V Nickel, Blanchard &Co., 2004
SOLD: $3.0 million (est.)

NEW YORK -- Small-capitalization stocks dropped to 2004 low points in tandem with the major stock indexes.

Pentagon Suspends Payments Of $160 Million to Halliburton
Pentagon Suspends Payments Of $160 Million to Halliburton
By Neil King Jr. and Russell Gold
693 words
18 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WASHINGTON -- The Pentagon says it has suspended nearly $160 million in payments to Halliburton Co. as
part of a lingering dispute over the charge for feeding U.S. troops and other personnel in Iraq.
The suspension, which Defense Department officials said was necessary "to protect the Army's financial
interests," could complicate the provisioning of troops in Iraq if it disrupts payments to Halliburton's many
subcontractors who cook and serve about 300,000 meals a day.
Some Defense officials suggested the financial cost to Halliburton's Kellogg Brown &Root unit could increase
substantially as the two sides feud over the bills for feeding U.S. personnel. KBR has billed the U.S. nearly
$800 million for food charges going back to late 2002 at more than 50 dining facilities in Iraq and Kuwait. KBR
has been paid for most of it.
KBR was notified of the suspension Thursday, the day before Pentagon auditors deemed the company's
billing system for all its Iraq-related contracts "inadequate" in an audit. KBR has a 10-year, multibillion-dollar
contract to provide a host of logistical and support services to the military, including feeding the soldiers,
washing their clothes and setting up military bases.
The audit, which hasn't previously been released, criticizes KBR for "deficiencies" that resulted in billings "not
prepared in accordance with applicable law and regulations and contract terms." In one of its more stinging
rebukes, the Defense Contract Audit Agency said KBR had "no documented mechanism" for assuring that its
many subcontractors billed properly and kept to their contract terms. The audit agency also said that despite
months of work, it still couldn't determine the "adequacy" of KBR's own internal auditing abilities.
In a written statement, a Halliburton spokeswoman said the Houston company was working with the Army to
resolve "outstanding issues" and said the company believes it will "ultimately be reimbursed for these costs."
The auditors' analysis was a "flawed interpretation," according to the company. The statement also said that if
the company isn't paid, it would withhold payments from the subcontractors that actually run the dining halls.
The dispute over food bills goes back to early this year, when Pentagon auditors first alleged that KBR had
billed for substantially more meals in the first half of 2003 than were actually served. In March, KBR submitted
more documents and argued heatedly that its bills were "proper and reasonable" and should be paid in full.
Last week, in an in-house Defense Department memo, one of the Pentagon's top auditors concluded that
KBR had failed to provide adequate documentation for its food billing. The Pentagon continued to believe the
number of meals billed "significantly exceeds the actual head count served since October 2002," the memo
said.
If Pentagon auditors stand by that assessment, the next move would be to disapprove payment, meaning
KBR would have to write off at least $160 million. Auditors expect to make that determination by the end of
June, at which point the Army Field Support Command would have the final decision. Pentagon officials
cautioned that the final sum could be substantially higher once the auditors complete their work.
The auditors said KBR appeared to base its billings on classified estimates of the number of troops that
needed to be fed that couldn't be verified. In a written response to the auditors, KBR said this conclusion was
"not only unsubstantiated but also premature."
In the last two quarters, Halliburton recorded $76 million of profit on Iraq-related work on $4.3 billion in
revenue. On its Army support contract, Halliburton earns a 1% profit on allowable expenses and then could
get an additional 2% performance award. The continued billing dispute appears likely to sow further unrest
among KBR's subcontractors, many of whom have had to skimp for long stretches. Paul Morrell, president of
Event Source, a Salt Lake City subcontractor that operates six dining halls in Iraq, said he was disappointed
with KBR. "I'm not comfortable doing business with them in the future," he said.

Dollar Wobbles as Iraq Blast Prompts Move to Safer Assets
