Dollar Wobbles as Iraq Blast Prompts Move to Safer Assets
By Jamie McGeever Dow Jones Newswires
377 words
18 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar mostly weakened after the killing of the president of the U.S.-appointed Iraqi
governing council prompted investors to shift funds out of the U.S. currency and stock markets world-wide.
Abdel-Zahraa Othman Muhammad, also known as Izzadine Salim, was killed along with several others by a
car bomb in Baghdad, spurring a move into what traditionally are considered safer assets, such as the Swiss
franc, gold and U.S. Treasurys.
Most of the action in global currency markets took place before North American trading began. The New York
market showed little enthusiasm and prices moved little in uneventful trading.
Dealers shrugged off a report showing that manufacturing activity in New York state rose last month less than
economists had expected. They also largely ignored the latest U.S. portfolio flows data from the Treasury
Department, which showed strong net inflows into U.S. securities in March but net equity outflows and
broadly weak demand for all assets from the private sector.
Late yesterday, the euro has strengthened to around $1.2022 from $1.1881 late Friday in New York, and it
was at 137.39 yen, compared with 135.75 yen. Europe's shared currency hit fresh two-month highs yesterday
at 137.50 yen.
On the back of the yen's weakness against the euro, the dollar was steady against the Japanese currency at
114.29 yen, compared with 114.25 yen late Friday, but had weakened against the Swiss franc to 1.2765
francs from 1.2963 francs. Sterling strengthened to $1.7683 from $1.7600.
News that manufacturing activity in the New York Federal Reserve district slipped in May from April kept the
dollar under selling pressure in yesterday's U.S. session Monday.
The Indian rupee rebounded from an early sharp fall to end stronger against the dollar after the country's
central bank said it will continue to intervene in the market to meet any shortfall in the supply of foreign
exchange. The dollar closed local trading at 45.455 rupees, off an eight-month high of 46 rupees and lower
than Friday's close of 45.517 rupees.

India Stocks Shudder, With a Wide Impact --- Blue Chips Slide 105.96 Points, Settling Below 10000 Yet Again; Safety of Bonds Proves Alluring
Monday's Markets
India Stocks Shudder, With a Wide Impact --- Blue Chips Slide 105.96 Points, Settling Below 10000 Yet
Again; Safety of Bonds Proves Alluring
By E.S. Browning
971 words
18 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. STOCKS took another beating, hit by disquieting news from Iraq, Turkey, India and Japan, on top of
record oil prices and continuing expectations that interest rates at home are set to rise.
In the face of all that bad news, even many optimists simply stopped buying. The Dow Jones Industrial
Average sagged 105.96 points, or 1.06%, to 9906.91, its lowest close since Dec. 5. It was only the second
time this year that the blue-chip average finished below 10000; the first time was Monday of last week. The
industrial average has been down for three weeks in a row and has fallen 5% this year.
Investors shifted money to the relative safety of Treasury bonds, which jumped in value. The bellwether
10-year Treasury note rose 23/32, or $7.1875 for every $1,000 invested. The gain drove the note's yield
down to 4.691%. As recently as Thursday, amid expectations that the Federal Reserve would raise its target
for short-term interest rates as soon as next month, the yield hit 4.86%.
"Right now, the market is really focusing on these geopolitical events," said Warren Epstein, head trader at
brokerage firm Kabrik Trading in New York. "The market has been fighting off the wolves for a couple of
weeks, but it is a murky picture. You have interest rates that are going to head higher. And in the foreign
news, you aren't just talking about Iraq. You have problems in India, bombings in Turkey. You have Japan. It
creates uncertainty and when the market has uncertainty, stocks go down."
Traders said they saw signs of foreign investors selling U.S. stocks in order to raise capital and cover losses
at home. But they said selling in the U.S. was orderly.
"There was no sense of panic," said John O'Donoghue, senior stock trader at New York brokerage firm Credit
Suisse First Boston. After a sharp drop at the opening bell in response to the foreign news, he noted, stocks
essentially moved sideways.
Still, the day's news was relentlessly bad. Crude oil rose 17 cents to $41.55 a barrel, its highest finish since it
began trading on the New York Mercantile Exchange in 1983. Fears spread that higher oil prices could spark
broader inflation and put a damper on growth.
Helping spur the oil-price rise was the death of the head of Iraq's Governing Council in a suicide bombing.
Investors also received news reports that a roadside bomb containing the poison sarin exploded near a U.S.
military convoy in Iraq.
On top of the worries about terrorism, oil and the Middle East tinderbox, the Iraq trouble reinforced Wall
Street's nervousness about President Bush's prospects in the November election. Several traders said the
electoral uncertainty has been damaging stocks.
Japan's Nikkei Stock Average fell 3.2% after news reports said one of Japan's four largest banks would report
a large loss due to bad loans. India's stock market fell 11%, amid fears that the Congress Party's return to
power will stifle India's emergence as a technology hub. Another bomb went off in Turkey.
The bad news also drove investors back into gold, which had been sagging amid the dollar's recent rebound.
Gold rose $2.50 an ounce in late New York trading to $379.50, still well below the 15-year high of $427.80,
hit in April.
The dollar was mixed, edging up against the yen but falling against the euro.
All three major U.S. stock indexes finished at their lowest points since last year.

--- Page 79 ---
Page 79 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 1.06%, or 11.60 points, to 1084.10, now down 2.5% this
year. The Nasdaq Composite Index, full of volatile technology stocks, fell 1.45%, or 27.61 points, to 1876.64,
off more than 6% on the year. The Nasdaq is at its lowest point since October.
Even the Russell 2000 small-stock index, which had been outpacing big-stock indexes, fell to its lowest point
since December, dropping 1.55%, or 8.42 points, to 535.34. It is down 3.9% this year.
Since hitting a high in February, the Dow industrials are down 8%. The S&P 500 has fallen 6% from its 2004
high, the Nasdaq 13% and the Russell 12%.
"This is a market that really has had everything but the kitchen sink thrown at it," said Mr. O'Donoghue of
Credit Suisse First Boston. Given all the bad news, it hasn't done that poorly, he said. Some investors still are
betting that positive earnings news will rescue stocks in the future.
But as for the near term, "I really don't see much good news on the horizon," Mr. O'Donoghue said.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 1.31%, or 1.83 points, to 137.64.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.43 billion shares traded, 1,042 stocks rose and 2,285 fell.
Bonds gained. The 30-year bond was up 31/32. Its yield, which falls when the price rises, dropped to 5.43%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 114.29 yen, up from 114.25, while the euro rose against
the dollar to $1.2022 from $1.1881.

Interest Rates and Middle East" -- WSJ May 18, 2004)

Best on the Street (A Special Report): 2004 Analysts Survey --- Oil-Field Services &Pipelines
Best on the Street (A Special Report): 2004 Analysts Survey --- Oil-Field Services &Pipelines
By Russell Gold
600 words
17 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
R8
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
W. NEAL McATEE wasn't keen on too many companies last year. By July, the oil-field services analyst for
Morgan Keegan &Co., a subsidiary of Regions Financial Corp., Birmingham, Ala., had a "buy"
recommendation on just one stock: Core Laboratories NV. This picky approach vaulted him to the top of this
year's Best on the Street survey of oil-field services and pipeline analysts.
Many analysts gave up on Core after an acquisition intended to boost its reservoir-management business
failed to produce expected growth. But Mr. McAtee, based in Memphis, Tenn., believed in Core's longtime
management team. It was well-placed confidence. The Amsterdam-based company, which trades on the New
York Stock Exchange, used strong cash flow to buy back stock, and rewarded Mr. McAtee with a 47% return
for the year.
The 40-year-old analyst also liked specialized drilling-equipment supplier Superior Energy Services Inc. of
Harvey, La., because it dominates the market for plugging old wells in the Gulf of Mexico. But he downgraded
the stock to "hold" in mid-June, soon after Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan testified before
Congress about falling supplies of natural gas. It turned out to be a good call. Superior returned 33% for the
less than six months he rated it a "buy."
For 2004, Mr. McAtee stills likes Core and Superior Energy Services. And in March of this year, he upgraded
his rating on Lone Star Technologies Inc., a Dallas-based maker of pipes and other products for oil
exploration.
Other top stock pickers did well by focusing on pipeline companies organized as master limited partnerships,
which focus on producing income for shareholders through stable assets such as oil and natural-gas
pipelines, gathering systems and processing plants. Analysts' job was to figure out which partnerships made
the best acquisitions.
Second-place finisher Ronald Barone of UBS in New York, a unit of UBS AG, benefited from good calls on
Magellan Midstream Partners, Tulsa, Okla. He rated it a "buy" through early December, when he switched to
"hold" and avoided a late-year slide. Mr. Barone says he thought the run-up in price was over and worried the
company might not do so well if interest rates rose. The stock returned 81% while he rated it a "buy."
The 60-year-old's other stellar performer was GulfTerra Energy Partners LP of Houston. He rated the stock a
"buy" for the year and was rewarded with a 65% return. GulfTerra owns a network of pipelines and platforms
in the Gulf of Mexico, which are well positioned to increase cash flow as numerous deepwater oil discoveries
begin pumping. For 2004, he still favors GulfTerra, which is expected to merge with Enterprise Products
Partners LP later this year.
The third-place winner, John Tysseland of Raymond James &Associates Inc. in Houston, a subsidiary of
Raymond James Financial Inc., recommended GulfTerra and Magellan for the year. But he did even better
with Crosstex Energy LP, a Dallas owner of natural-gas pipelines and processing plants. The analyst says the
company's management team "was able to add a lot of value to the asset they bought" by signing up more
producers to pump natural gas -- and therefore revenue -- through its pipelines. The stock returned 86% while
he rated it a "buy."
Mr. Tysseland, 30, thinks Crosstex will continue to do well in 2004. He also likes Energy Transfer Partners LP
of Tulsa, Okla., which has pipeline-expansion projects under way.

Buyer Beware: As Investors Rush Into China, Cautionary Tales Start to Pile Up --- China Life Says It's `Gold Mine' But Fails to Mention...
Buyer Beware: As Investors Rush Into China, Cautionary Tales Start to Pile Up --- China Life Says It's
`Gold Mine' But Fails to Mention Probe By Government Auditors --- Scandals as Signs of Progress
By Peter Wonacott
2,560 words
17 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SHANGHAI -- When China Life Insurance Co. listed shares on the New York Stock Exchange last December,
it boasted of its "leading market position" and "high growth opportunities."
It didn't mention that the Chinese government's powerful audit arm was poring over its books. And in
mid-January, when the auditors delivered to China Life's parent a draft report detailing widespread
irregularities, China Life told the public nothing. It first acknowledged that something was up on Feb. 3, the
day after the auditors announced some of their findings. By that time the stock price had already been
dropping. Now regulators in the U.S. and Hong Kong are investigating.
China Life's debut as a public company presents a lesson for investors lured by the rapid economic growth of
China. Many investors have rushed to buy chunks of former state monopolies, believing these companies are
leading the race to profit from the Chinese boom. But companies dressed up for an overseas stock-market
listing often have yet to grasp Western standards of disclosure. And the listed companies typically retain
hard-to-understand ties to unlisted state-owned parents.
"In the post-Enron era, these black boxes are becoming a real concern," says Roger Robinson, chairman of a
commission that advises the U.S. Congress on Chinese economic and security matters. "As corporate
governance gains prominence, Chinese issues will receive more scrutiny."
Already controversies over China Life and other companies have damped investor mania, although the
shares of many big Chinese companies listed in the past few years remain well above their offering price.
Shanghai-based Semiconductor Manufacturing International Corp. announced just before its March listing in
the U.S. and Hong Kong that, contrary to earlier statements, it would need additional financing for working
capital through 2005. The company's share price fell below its offering price. China Minsheng Banking Corp.
admitted it fabricated a 2000 shareholders meeting at which a name change was supposedly approved. The
Shanghai-listed bank has postponed a $1 billion stock offering in Hong Kong.
When China Life offered shares, demand outstripped the number of available shares by a factor of 25. Within
two weeks, the price on the New York Stock Exchange zoomed to nearly double the offering price. Today, the
shares have lost most of those gains.
China Life calls the government audit a "routine review" and says it disclosed the results promptly after it
received them in final form in April. It says a contract requires its parent to pay $8.15 million in back taxes and
fines resulting from the violations, so China Life shareholders won't suffer. Although it has reported an
"informal inquiry" by the U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission, China Life says it was told by the SEC
that the inquiry isn't meant to suggest that any laws were violated. A spokesman for the SEC declined to
comment.
In response to a Wall Street Journal query, China Life has for the first time acknowledged another audit
investigation in 2002 by the Ministry of Finance that covered the company's operations in several provinces
and cities. The ministry's probe, which hasn't been reported outside China, criticized China Life for its lack of
"management controls" and violations of "standard accounting procedures," according to a ministry official
who participated.
Some China watchers think the disclosure disputes and audit reports are actually a positive sign that China is
transforming itself into a capitalist economy in tune with Western standards. Chinese companies were first
allowed to sell shares to the public just 20 years ago, compared with the centuries that Western stock
markets have had to develop. The notion of full disclosure is even newer. The Chinese government says it
hopes the attention of foreign investors will transform sleepy state enterprises into globally minded
corporations.

--- Page 84 ---
Page 84 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The government wants to see these companies subjected to harsh market scrutiny," says Lin Shoukang,
head of capital markets for China International Capital Corp., the joint Chinese-U.S. investment bank that
underwrote China Life's offering.
Among the investors who believe that Chinese companies are worth a gamble is Warren Buffett. The
billionaire investor's company, Berkshire Hathaway Inc., owns 1.3% of petrochemicals giant PetroChina Co.,
which was listed on the New York Stock Exchange in 2000. Although PetroChina shares have fallen recently,
they still trade at more than twice their starting price. Despite recent falls, the six biggest Chinese stocks
trading in New York climbed an average of 36% between the time they listed and May 14.
The company now called China Life was born in 1949, the year of the Communist revolution, as the People's
Insurance Company of China. For years, People's Insurance had a monopoly on all kinds of insurance. In the
1980s, the government let some competitors into the market. In 1999 People's Insurance was split into four
independent units, one of which focused on life insurance and was named China Life.
China Life controlled half of the country's life-insurance market, which has been growing at more than 35% a
year. There was just one problem: Its finances were a mess.
During the 1980s and 1990s it sold many life-insurance policies that were more like annuities, offering
customers regular payments after retirement with a guaranteed rate of return on the money they had paid in
over the years. When China's central bank cut interest rates seven times between 1996 and 1999, China
Life found itself paying out more to policy holders than it could earn through bank deposits. Although China's
insurance regulator later allowed companies to cut their guaranteed rates, many unprofitable policies festered
on China Life's books.
China Life's expansion relied on another risky strategy: hiring unlicensed agents to hawk policies. As the
company would later acknowledge in its prospectus, 37% of its agents as of June 30, 2003, didn't hold the
proper government license.
Such problems were familiar territory for China International Capital Corp. Known as CICC, it is a joint
venture between state-owned China Construction Bank and the New York investment bank Morgan Stanley.
CICC's bread and butter was getting state-owned giants such as China Telecom Corp. and China Petroleum
and Chemical Corp. to list overseas.
In 2002, CICC decided to tackle a China Life listing, bankers say. CICC's head, Levin Zhu, is the son of
former Chinese premier Zhu Rongji and once worked for Credit Suisse First Boston. The younger Mr. Zhu,
whose Chinese given name means "cloud coming," is also a weather expert, having written a doctoral thesis
on Asia's summer monsoons at the University of Wisconsin.
Levin Zhu got personally involved in the effort to restructure China Life for a New York listing, according to a
CICC colleague and another investment banker involved in the deal. A CICC spokeswoman declined to
comment or to make Mr. Zhu available for an interview, saying it was "not convenient at the moment" to
discuss his role in the China Life issue.
By March 2003, China Life's underwriting syndicate expanded to include Citigroup Inc., Credit Suisse Group's
Credit Suisse First Boston and Deutsche Bank AG. CICC needed the global heavyweights to package the
company for overseas investors.
The investment bankers knew overseas investors would shun a life insurance company with a portfolio of
unprofitable policies. Their solution: Split China Life into two companies, a fully state-owned parent and a
subsidiary. The parent would inherit all the liabilities that might scare off investors. It even signed an
agreement to pay any fines resulting from violations dating from before the split.
The subsidiary, scrubbed clean, would inherit profitable policies, the sales force and other assets. Although
the subsidiary would still have tens of thousands of unlicensed agents, investors were assured that insurance
regulators never enforced the license requirement.
"In these massive restructurings, all the historical problems and everything else that doesn't make economic
sense get kicked out of the bucket. And then they list the bucket," says Carl Walter, chief operating officer of
J.P. Morgan Chase &Co.'s China businesses and co-author of the book "Privatizing China." Mr. Walter wasn't
involved in the China Life listing.
Underwriters raced to get China Life ready, recognizing the feverish demand for shares in promising Chinese
concerns. In one sign of the times, a small producer of vegetables called China Green Holdings Ltd. saw
investors request 1,600 times as many shares as the company had to offer before it was listed on the Hong
Kong exchange. In June 2003, China Life formally split into the parent, called China Life Insurance (Group)
Co., and the subsidiary with the almost-identical name of China Life Insurance Co. Some top executives held

--- Page 85 ---
Page 85 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
posts in both companies, including Wang Xianzhang, who serves simultaneously as president of the parent
and the subsidiary.
Just six months later, after $100 million in bankers' fees, China Life made its debut on the New York Stock
Exchange. Only 27% of its shares were available to the public; the other 73% stayed in the hands of the
parent company. The offering raised $3.5 billion.
"I would compare China Life to a gold mine with rich proven deposits," said Mr. Wang in an interview with the
Chinese-language Caijing Magazine before the listing. "Investors can get handsome returns with relatively
little input."
Those lucky enough to buy China Life shares at the initial offering price paid $18.68 per share. As soon as
trading started on Dec. 17, the price jumped to $23.25. By Jan. 2 the price hit a peak of $35.60, 91% above
the offer price.
(MORE)
Many eager investors probably hadn't heard of an agency called the China National Audit Office. Its job is to
scrutinize the spending of anything in China that's part of the government -- from the remotest county clerk's
office to the biggest state-owned company. For much of 2003, the National Audit Office's investigators were
poring over the 2002 records of China Life, and they didn't like what they saw.
Among the auditors' findings: China Life's investment in its 32-story headquarters complex broke a Chinese
law barring insurance companies from commercial real estate projects. The Beijing complex was designed by
American architects and is topped by a spire that resembles the Empire State Building.
An audit official who was involved in the investigation says his office delivered a draft report to China Life's
parent company in mid-January of this year. The draft was secret, but on Jan. 30, a Friday, China's audit
chief delivered a nationally televised speech in which he outlined some of China Life's violations. China Life's
New York-listed shares slumped 4.6%.
The next Monday, Feb. 2, the National Audit Office posted the speech on its Web site. The violations included
using unlicensed agents to sell insurance and fraudulent handling of some policies.
While China Life shares recovered briefly in February, they have taken a battering since then from worried
investors. The company disclosed the SEC investigation on April 27. Hong Kong's Securities and Futures
Commission said April 28 that it is inquiring into "the circumstances of the listing of China Life" and "assisting
the U.S. SEC in its inquiry."
Analysts at the same investment banks that underwrote the offering are recommending caution on China Life.
Citigroup rates the company a "sell." Its April 26 report criticizes management for being stingy with
information on new premiums and claims, calling China Life's disclosure in this area "extremely
disappointing."
Credit Suisse rates China Life "underperform" but says it's "probably unrealistic" to expect excellent
disclosure from a newly listed company.
Mark Mobius, president of Templeton Emerging Markets Fund Inc., agrees. "There's a whole culture that has
to change," says Mr. Mobius, whose fund bought China Life shares and then sold them shortly after trading
began. "Until then, it is buyer beware."
What remains unclear is how much, if anything, China Life's underwriters knew about the National Audit
Office's investigation when they were preparing its prospectus. Representatives for the four lead underwriters
declined to comment. "If you don't ask a direct question, Chinese executives aren't going to volunteer
information," says Hao Yansu, a dean at the Central University of Finance and Economics in Beijing and
adviser to several Chinese insurance companies.
Meanwhile, there is still a veil of secrecy around the separate Ministry of Finance investigation from 2002 that
examined China Life's books from the year before. It showed that a branch in northeastern Jilin province
fraudulently recorded transactions to meet sales targets, an executive of the branch said in an interview.
Another branch in southeastern Jiangxi province was cited for recording $800,000 in phony construction
costs as a way of getting money from headquarters that could be put aside for other uses, says an executive
of that branch.
A Ministry of Finance official in Jilin says the investigation took place from May to September 2002 and was a
"routine inspection task." A ministry spokesman declined to comment on the investigation. A spokesman for
China Life confirmed the Ministry of Finance audit report, but said it "related only to . . . a few branches of the
former China Life Insurance Co."

--- Page 86 ---
Page 86 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
China Life officials insist the company is still a good investment. A group of executives returned last week
from wooing fund managers in Asia, Europe and the U.S. China Life also has hired a London public-relations
firm, Citigate Dewe Rogerson. Bruce Shu, a Citigate director, says China Life has "maintained or increased
market share in all . . . target segments."
China Life shares stood at $20.33 in 4 p.m. composite trading Friday on the New York Stock Exchange.
In announcing earnings last month, Mr. Wang, China Life's president, said the company is profitable, now that
it is no longer responsible for the unprofitable policies that were left with its parent. "All in all, the company
fulfilled the promises made to its shareholders and is committed to delivering good corporate governance and
increasing transparency," he said.
But another senior China Life executive calls the brouhaha over disclosure a "painful process" and adds: "I
think other Chinese companies will learn from our experience. We have to change old habits."

The Greenspan Market
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Greenspan Market
835 words
13 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The U.S. economy may be zipping along, but the animal spirits in the stock market are looking decidedly
bedraggled. Yesterday the Dow was on a roller coaster, falling more than 160 points before recovering for a
small gain. The index lost 123 points last Friday and 127 points on Monday, closing below 10,000 for the first
time this year. This comes despite rising corporate earnings and productivity, plus another positive jobs report
showing that companies hired 288,000 additional workers last month.
Given that the market usually anticipates what the real economy will be doing some months out, this has a lot
of people trying to figure out what bad news may lie in store. The standard reasoning is that Wall Street is
merely getting used to the idea of Chairman Alan Greenspan and the Federal Reserve raising interest rates.
And perhaps that is part of the story.
But it's not a very satisfying explanation because the Fed hardly seems eager to raise rates right away, or
very rapidly when it does move. Rather, it is telegraphing a softly, softly approach, cautiously making sure the
markets are prepared psychologically. And even when rates do rise, they will remain low by historical
standards. At this point there is hardly reason to fear that an overzealous Fed is going to choke off the
recovery.
Quite the opposite. Here's an alternative theory: The one thing that markets cannot abide is uncertainty. And
at present we have plenty of that, and in multiple varieties.
The revelations of prisoner abuse in Iraq not only represent a setback to the American efforts to stabilize that
country, but they may be used by domestic critics of the war to undermine public support for victory. The
Presidential election remains a tight race, meaning that economic decision-makers don't know what kind of
policies (e.g., tax increases) they will face after next January. Oil at $40 a barrel despite plentiful supplies also
increases anxiety.
But perhaps the greatest source of uncertainty is the evidence of renewed inflation and the response of the
Federal Reserve. It was astounding that the Federal Open Markets Committee's statement after its meeting
last week still referred to the possibility of deflation, stating that "the risks to the goal of price stability have
moved into balance." While acknowledging that some recent data show rising prices, "long-term inflation
expectations appear to have remained well contained," the central bankers concluded.
It's true that the recent declines in commodity prices including gold have to be counted as good news. These
have proven to be reliable leading indicators of brewing inflation in the past. But the recent slackening in
demand for raw materials may be due to temporary factors, such as Beijing's efforts to engineer a soft landing
for the red-hot Chinese economy, and the subsequent retreat of some speculative buyers.
Meanwhile, the statements of individual Fed governors and presidents also suggest that they aren't in an
inflation-fighting mood. Yesterday Chicago Fed President Michael Moskow told CNBC, "The type of
inflation increases we've seen this year have been transitory," and predicted a "gradual pace" of rate rises.
The main reason the Fed claims to be so sanguine is that it has its eye on the gap between the economy's
productive capacity and its actual output. Mr. Moskow has indicated that this gap is narrowing, and this may
provide some justification for tightening. But it should give everyone pause that even as consumer prices
have started to rise, the Fed is still fixated on excess capacity on the factory floor rather than on price signals
in the real economy.
It's striking how the Fed governors seem to have been surprised by the strength of the present expansion.
Even after two brisk quarters of growth last year, and even as the economy was growing at a 4.2% annual
rate in the first quarter, the official Fed line was still that the risk of deflation actually outweighed that of
inflation, and rates would remain at 46-year lows for a considerable period.

--- Page 90 ---
Page 90 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
We may well be seeing that such an excessively easy monetary policy can end up having the opposite of
the intended reassuring effect on the markets. Investors want to believe that the Fed has the nerve to raise
rates, if that's what is required to help forestall any new inflation, even if this is an election year. An early and
sharp increase in the Fed funds rates might even be called for as a way to signal its seriousness and reduce
uncertainty about when and how much it is going to move.
The consensus now is that this Friday's April inflation report will come in at 0.3% -- a still-brisk annual rate of
3.6% -- compared with March's unexpected 0.5%. But should consumer prices once again surprise on the
upside, we'd say the sooner the Fed moves the better.

Bears Are Roaming Far and Wide --- Rate Worries Threatening To Burst Yet More Bubbles In Diverse World Markets
Going Global
Bears Are Roaming Far and Wide --- Rate Worries Threatening To Burst Yet More Bubbles In Diverse
World Markets
By Michael R. Sesit
894 words
12 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C18
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Paris -- FINGERS CROSSED. With U.S. bond yields climbing ever higher and the specter of the Federal
Reserve raising short-term interest rates looming ever nearer, one global financial-market bubble after
another -- bonds, property, commodities, currencies and emerging-markets stocks and bonds -- is popping or
threatening to blow.
So far, global stock markets have been spared. But some skeptics contend stock markets may be living on
borrowed time. The rally these markets saw from October 2002 to January 2004, albeit coming from a low
start, "was probably just a bounce in a long-term bear market," says Michael Belkin, president of Belkin Ltd.,
a Bainbridge Island, Wash., investment-advisory firm that forecasts financial-market trends.
He adds that, "the potential for a stampede in the opposite direction in everything is enormous" given that
many investors are holding long positions -- bets that the only way is up -- in other financial markets just as
U.S. and other nations' interest rates appear to be headed higher. Already, signs of that stampede can be
seen on the horizon.
Since March 12, for instance, the yield on 10-year U.S. Treasury bonds has climbed to 4.8% from 3.7%, while
the bond's price -- which moves inversely to its yield -- has fallen 8.7%.
The price of oil is higher than almost every analyst had predicted a year ago, but other commodity prices are
sinking. Base-metals prices are down 13% from their recent highs of April 19, and the price of gold has
tumbled 12% since the end of March.
From their highs against the dollar in February and March, the euro, yen and pound sterling have fallen by
between 8% and 8.7%. And in the past month, emerging stock markets have lost 13%, according to Morgan
Stanley Capital International.
What's more, money managers and economists are trying to decide whether China, Asia's new behemoth,
will undergo a hard or soft landing as its leaders attempt to cool down their economy's near 10% growth rate.
All these markets are taking a hit because hedge funds and other speculators had piled in with borrowed
money that was many multiples the capital they had. Employing so-called carry trades, they borrowed dollars
at low interest rates and invested the proceeds in higher-yielding assets. But with U.S. interest rates rising,
many of the trades either are becoming or threatening to become unprofitable. As a consequence, the
speculators and other investors are making a mad dash for the exits, dragging markets down with them.
Meanwhile, individual investors are going deeper in debt to either refinance existing homes or take out bigger
mortgages to purchase new ones. The result: a bloated real-estate bubble on the verge of bursting, says
Morgan Stanley Chief Economist Stephen Roach in a report to clients last Friday.
Some point to the Fed as having fostered this speculative, bubble-creating fever. To deal with one problem --
the residue from the bursting of the late 1990's stock-market bubble -- the Fed pushed interest rates to
46-year lows. But that cheap money in turn created a new set of bubbles in other markets.
While their performance this year certainly isn't setting the house on fire, major world stock markets have so
far managed to escape the carnage. James Montier, a global equity strategist at Dresdner Kleinwort
Wasserstein in London, suspects that is because the people making the big bets with borrowed cash largely
avoided the big markets, except for a few individual sectors such as technology. But he warns: "The start of
the unwind in the highly leveraged areas of the global reflation trade is just a hint of what's to come."
So how far can major stock markets around the world fall? Mr. Belkin contends all the way back to their 2002
lows. Depending on which index is measured, that could translate into declines of between 22% and 43%. If

--- Page 92 ---
Page 92 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
that starts to happen, investors may need to look for solace to none other than Abraham Lincoln, who said:
"The best thing about the future is that it comes only one day at a time."
---

Major world stock-market indexes could fall a lot lower -- if they return
to earlier lows.

Manitowoc, Toro and Joy Global Slump Amid Pressure on Group

Job Alert
Job Alert
By Brian Wesbury
1,121 words
11 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Less than three months ago, conventional wisdom placed the U.S. in a "jobless recovery" and fears of
deflation were rampant. Now, after non-farm payrolls have increased by 625,000 in a two-month period and
inflation data have turned up, some are worried about an overheating economy and Fed rate hikes. Bond
yields have jumped by 100 basis points in the past six weeks and the stock market has gone wobbly. The
abruptness of this shift in market psychology is breathtaking, but not unprecedented: Economic psychology
has irresistible momentum. When things are going well for the economy, very few believe that they will ever
go bad. And when things are going badly, people have a hard time believing they will ever improve.
For example, the recession of 2001 caught most forecasters by surprise. It officially started in March, but well
into the year, conventional wisdom was that the U.S. would avoid a downturn. The same is true of deflation.
In 2001, after so many years of inflationary fears, most economists considered deflation inconceivable,
almost laughable. Yet in 2002 and early 2003, it became enemy No. 1. This year, the opposite has occurred.
Just weeks ago, investors were certain that the federal-funds rate would end 2004 at 1%, unchanged from
current levels, because the economy was weak and inflation low. Today, the market has priced in a 2%
federal-funds rate by year's end.
The main reason most investors are surprised by economic developments is that they put an undue weight
on the views of Fed members. Those views are always subject to change without notice. Inexplicably, as late
as Nov. 15, 2000, the Fed was still warning that the threat of inflation was greater than the threat of
recession. Just six weeks later, on Jan. 3, 2001, the Fed cut interest rates by 50 basis points at an
emergency meeting as it became apparent that a recession was under way.
This year, on March 16, the Fed's post-meeting statement said that the threat of deflation was slightly greater
than that of inflation. Now seven weeks later, the Fed could easily hike rates before its next official meeting,
because both the economy and inflation are much hotter than expected -- a 180-degree turn in less than 60
days.
The actual economy does not change this rapidly. In fact, the economic boom and inflationary pressures that
are so visible today have been building for well over a year. But because the Fed pays more attention to jobs,
wages and capacity utilization than it does to sensitive market indicators, these developments seemed to
come from nowhere.
Each of the Fed's favorite economic indicators lags the actual strength of the economy by many months. The
jobs lag is particularly long. This is especially true of the non-farm payroll data, because the Bureau of Labor
Statistics relies on corporate state unemployment insurance filings to estimate total employment. Those data
are only available with a lag of roughly six months, and the BLS is still basing its estimates on second-quarter
2003 data -- a war quarter.
Another reason payroll jobs have lagged the overall economy is that the survey undercounts the
self-employed. For example, the payroll survey estimates that there are 311,000 realtors in the U.S. But the
National Association of Realtors claims a million members. Moreover, at least 80% of all realtors are
independent contractors. They do not pay unemployment insurance and therefore are not counted by the
BLS.
Many have argued that the payroll survey is the most reliable survey in a purely statistical sense. This is true.
It has a very large sample size and is not reliant on volatile population estimates. But what does a perfect
measure of payroll employment mean if that is not where the real growth in jobs is taking place? Making very
important policy decisions based on this statistic alone is a highly questionable undertaking.
Another problematic area is capacity utilization. In March, the Fed estimated that the nation's factories were
operating at 75.2% of total capacity, and said that this indicated little inflationary pressure. However, the
Institute for Supply Management, in its first-quarter survey of purchasing managers, placed capacity

--- Page 95 ---
Page 95 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
utilization at 85.6%. Technology growth has increased the "obsolescence factor," and the Fed's data often
includes capacity that is no longer truly useful to U.S. companies.
The Fed has suggested that its policy decisions are aided by risk-management techniques. Yet it bases
monetary policy on questionable data. This seems to be a high-risk strategy. The good news is that the Fed
has a better option. If it paid more attention to gold, commodity prices, the yield curve and the value of the
dollar, it might avoid making such abrupt changes in policy. And if investors paid more attention to these
markets, they would be a step ahead of the Fed, rather than a step behind. In 2000, the yield curve was
inverted, gold and commodity prices were falling and the dollar was surging. These sensitive leading
indicators predicted the recession and deflation of 2001.
In the past two years, the yield curve has been very steep and the dollar has been weak. And despite recent
declines, gold and commodity prices are up significantly from their 2001 lows. While the world has been
watching data that may (or may not) measure anything useful, market signals have correctly forecast a surge
in growth and rising inflationary pressures.
The Fed's Phillips Curve model, which assumes that inflation cannot rise as long as employment growth is
slow and capacity utilization remains low, has led the Fed to maintain the easiest monetary policy stance
since the mid 1970s. This model has been proven wrong. Inflation is now on the rise. The ISM price index
rose to 88 in April, the highest level since 1979. Oil prices are at $40 per barrel. And after stripping out both
food and energy prices, the "core" CPI increased 2.9% at an annual rate during the three months ending in
March.
The evidence clearly shows that deflation and disinflation have not been a worry for quite some time. Market
signals warned of this last year, while lagging economic data gave no warning whatsoever. Maybe President
Bush should appoint an "Economic Surgeon General," who could put a warning label on Fed policy
pronouncements. How about this? -- "These policies are based on lagged data, not market signals, and
investors should make decisions based on them at their own risk." At least then, investors might not find
themselves caught so off guard.
---
Mr. Wesbury is chief economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens &Thompson, Inc., in Chicago.

Gold Declines to Lowest Level Since 2003 as the Dollar Gains
Commodities Report
Gold Declines to Lowest Level Since 2003 as the Dollar Gains
By Gavin Maguire OsterDowJones Commodity News
447 words
10 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold futures on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange plunged more than
$9 a troy ounce Friday to their lowest level since October 2003, on selling caused by the sharply stronger
dollar and improving outlook for the U.S. economy.
The most-active June contract fell $9.30 to $379.10 a troy ounce.
The dollar was buoyed by a surge in U.S. jobs, which fueled expectations that a rise in U.S. interest rates
was approaching. That, in turn, tarnished gold's allure as an alternative to the U.S. currency and prompted
speculators to rotate out of the metal and into the greenback.
Momentum-following funds also waded in on the sell side, as did some funds that recently established short
positions in the market, to keep the pressure on gold through most of the day.
With the market slightly thinner than usual because of the absence of many East Asian participants due to
public holidays, prices lost ground at a swift rate during the New York morning and fell more than $10 below
Thursday's close by noon.
Physical demand from market participants based across the Middle East and elsewhere remained intact
throughout, however, and managed to steady June futures prices for the last hour of action in a $378 to $380
range.
Some preweekend, safe-haven buying spurred by the intense geopolitical landscape also emerged during
the last hour of action, dealers said.
Regardless, market watchers agreed that additional downside pressure can be expected if the dollar
continues to rise against its major rivals.
Paul McLeod, vice president of precious-metals trading at Commerzbank in New York, said that gold's
somewhat muted response to the dollar's softer tone earlier last week and the prevailing historically high price
of oil have damped whatever bullish sentiment remains in the gold market, and therefore have increased the
likelihood of more weakness.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures hit $40 a barrel, a 13-year high, on the Nymex soon after the session began as
investors fearful of further terror attacks targeting oil supplies loaded up on contracts ahead of the weekend.
June oil prices ultimately rose 56 cents to $39.93 a barrel after trading as high as $40.
COTTON: Futures on the New York Cotton Exchange rallied to a five-week high and hit an exchange
daily-trading limit amid bullish fundamentals as traders expect fewer acres to be planted in cotton for the
2004-2005 crop. The July contract rose three cents to 64.75 cents a pound.

Summer-Fun Inflation
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Summer-Fun Inflation
By Paula Szuchman and Nancy Ann Jeffrey
2,283 words
7 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
W1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Vacations, theme parks, ice cream -- and sticker shock. Paula Szuchman and Nancy Ann Jeffrey find that
after years of steady prices, your wallet will feel the heat this season.]
EVERY YEAR at this time in Pottstown, Pa., members of the Elks Club 814 Little League team put on their
purple-and-gold uniforms, recite the team pledge to "play fair and strive to win" and take to the field with
dreams of a championship. But this rite of summer is costing their parents more this season. The registration
fee is $35, up from $10 the past two years.
"Problem is, the money has to come from somewhere," says Roy Reifsnyder, league co-treasurer, who
attributes the rise to pricier uniforms, an increase in insurance premiums, and even more-expensive infield
dirt (trucking it in costs more, thanks to higher gas prices). "But we've got a great season ahead of us."
With summer approaching, the batter's up, surf's up . . . and so is the cost of fun. After several years of
holding prices constant for fear of putting off recession-weary Americans, the companies that keep the
season humming are starting to charge more. At the Ritz Carlton, Montego Bay, Jamaica, a double room runs
$225 this summer, compared with $169 a year ago. Admission to Pennsylvania's Hersheypark is $37.95, up
$2, while a round-trip ferry ticket to Fire Island in New York just went up a buck, to $12 -- the first increase in
five years. Inflation has also hit such bare necessities as swimsuits and bug spray.
To come up with our gauge of summer-fun inflation, Weekend Journal checked out the prices for goods and
services you'd need at the beach, ballpark or backyard pool. Taking some cues from the way the U.S. Bureau
of Labor Statistics does its monthly Consumer Price Index, we created a "basket of goods" to see how prices
for the items compare with last year's. In our case, it's more like a picnic basket. With 15 items, including
kayak rentals and flip-flops, it's a lot smaller and less scientific than the government's. Our result: The
summer-fun inflation index is up 2.6% over last year -- a little more than the overall U.S. inflation rate.
In many cases, the higher prices reflect increases in the cost of key underlying commodities, such as oil, beef
and dairy products. With oil nearing $40 a barrel -- the highest level in more than a decade -- a tank of gas for
the family's SUV road trip will naturally be more expensive. But you should also expect to paying more for
sunglasses and inflatable pool toys. That's not only because shipping costs are higher, but also because oil is
an ingredient in plastics.
Another factor: Retailers who decided against raising prices in recent years say they now feel more
comfortable passing on these costs. After all, there have been months of improvement in consumer spending,
and retailers say customers will accept paying more as the economy recovers and the job market improves.
In Durham, N.C., Burt's Bees says it held off raising prices on its herbal insect repellent, among other
products, even as prices for "essential oils" such as lemongrass and citronella have risen over the past few
years. Last month it lifted prices by 10% to 15% on about 10 of its 150 products that "weren't meeting our
profit goals," says Renee Quimby, vice president of sales and marketing.
Inflation has become a growing concern for the economy recently, after three years in which prices for most
consumer goods stayed essentially flat. Overall, inflation continues to be low. As recently as January, the
Labor Department's so-called core consumer-price measure (excluding volatile food and energy prices) was
at 1.1%, a 37-year low. For March, the core index climbed to 1.6%, still modest by historical standards. And
the Federal Reserve, which would typically raise interest rates to combat inflation, left them unchanged this
week -- though the Fed signaled it may be more willing to lift rates in coming months.
Indeed, some of the items in Weekend Journal's summer-fun basket stayed stable or even fell in price. A
four-piece fly-fishing rod at outdoor outfitter Orvis, for example, is $198, just like last summer. The average
cost of top-selling sunblocks at drugstore.com held steady at $9. And stock up your pool shed: Chlorine costs
are at a three-year low thanks to inexpensive supplies from overseas.

--- Page 102 ---
Page 102 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Even if inflation is unlikely to derail anyone's summer plans, it can still be irritating -- particularly when it
involves the very activities meant to take your mind off your bank account. Disney World in Orlando, Fla., is
charging $54.75 for a one-day adult ticket, a 5% increase from last year and the biggest jump in dollar terms
in 15 years. Disney says guests are getting good value for their money. Nearby Universal Studios is raising
its adult-ticket price by $2.80 to match Disney's price, saying the new number is competitive. Meanwhile, the
International Association of Amusement Parks &Attractions says admission prices are rising nationwide due
to parks' spending on new rides and the higher cost of fuel.
The hotel and airline industries are capitalizing on pent-up demand, as vacationers hit the road again after the
Sept. 11 strikes and the recession. On the island of Aruba, where summer is typically slower, hotel rates are
up about 5% from last year, in part because hotel operators were emboldened by a 13% rise in U.S. visitors
during the first three months of this year. "People have become a little spoiled in the past few years," says
Myrna Jansen-Feliciano, managing director of the Aruba Tourism Authority.
In the case of some national and state parks, entry and use fees are rising to make up for declines in public
funding. At Acadia National Park in Maine, the week-long entry pass will double to $20 from $10, in part to
pay for shuttle-service expansion. In California, budget problems are causing state parks to raise rates; the
Seacliff State Beach in Aptos is now charging $39 a night for a beachfront campsite permit, up from $26 last
year.
Fans of the nation's pastime are taking a hit, too. At Chicago's Wrigley Field, a beer costs $4.75, up a quarter
from last year. In Boston, a hot dog at Fenway Park is also up a quarter, to $3.75. Not to be outdone, hot
dogs at Yankee Stadium are up a quarter to $4.50 and game programs with score cards just popped up to
$7, from $5 last season. The average price for a hot dog at a major-league baseball stadium is now $3.05 (up
from $3 last season) while a beer costs $5.17 (up from $5.08), according to Team Marketing Report of
Chicago. Average ticket prices are up 6% to $19.82.
For Yankee fan Greg McGunagle, a day at the ballpark cost more than he expected. After munching a hot
dog and guzzling a few beers at $8 each at a recent game, he says he had to make a second trip back to the
ATM to get more money to go out with his buddies after the game. "It was annoying," Mr. McGunagle, 23,
said. A spokesman for the Yankees declined to comment on pricing decisions.
As for those hot dogs, part of the issue is rising beef prices. Protein-heavy diets are leading to higher demand
at a time when supplies are actually shrinking -- thanks to mad-cow-related import bans and smaller domestic
herds. Chicken prices are also up, by 30% in the past year, because feed prices are higher and chicken
processors generally raise their prices when cattle processors do.
It's even trickled down to the Little Leaguers back in Pottstown. The concession stand at Novak Park has
boosted prices of roast-beef and barbecued chicken sandwiches by 50 cents. Says a league spokesman: "It's
the best barbecue in town."
Some merchants are playing a guessing game as to which costs to raise and by how much. At Allied Marine
Center in Traverse City, Mich., owner Phil Hamner is going to keep the rate for a basic boat "check-up" to
$25. But for repairs he's raising his hourly labor charge to $68 after keeping it at $60 for the past three years.
One factor in his decision: Other boat shops are raising prices, too. "There is a psychological disadvantage of
being too low," he says. "If people think you are too cheap, they start to wonder about your service."
Of course, not everyone is raising prices. Viola Holmgren, owner of Auntie Vi's Children's Rental Center of
Cape Cod in South Yarmouth, Mass., is keeping her rental fees steady at $15 a week for items such as
beach umbrellas, towel packages and coolers. "It seems to work for me," she says.
---
Dow Jones Summer Fun Index
Using as our guide the Bureau of Labor Statistics' "basket of goods" --
200 categories of items including milk, tuition and gasoline that help
determine
inflation -- we set out to create our own basket. Weekend Journal
looked at goods and services from flip-flops to ferry rides, talking to mom-
and-pop stores, national chains, industry trackers and trade organizations to
determine current and past prices for summer-related goods and services. We
added up the prices of everything in our basket and compared it with the
total for last year. The bottom line: By our count, the cost of fun is up
2.6%.

Canyon Resources, Bema Advance
Small-Stock Focus
Canyon Resources, Bema Advance
By Cynthia Schreiber Dow Jones Newswires
407 words
5 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gains by gold miners and steelmakers helped small-capitalization stocks outpace the broad
market after the Federal Reserve held interest rates steady.
Gold rose, bolstering stocks related to the metal, as the U.S. dollar declined ahead of the Fed's decision.
Canyon Resources jumped 38 cents, or 14%, to $3.15 and Bema Gold climbed 29 cents, or 12%, to 2.72,
both on the American Stock Exchange. Hecla Mining rose 30 cents, or 5.2%, to 6.13 on the New York Stock
Exchange.
Shares of steelmakers rose after Merrill Lynch said in a research note that it saw rising steel prices amid
strengthening demand. On the Big Board, Ryerson Tull gained 79 cents, or 7%, to 12.12; Carpenter
Technology rose 1.37, or 5%, to 28.75; and Allegheny Technologies jumped 47 cents, or 4.4%, to 11.17.
The Fed decision was "more like pain delayed than pain eliminated," said Brock Ganeles, head of capital
markets at Merriman Curhan Ford &Co. "We had a big spike when everyone was excited that rates didn't go
up," he said. "But that means a rate hike looms ahead of us."
As a result, small stocks finished off session highs, but they did outperform the overall market. The Russell
2000 Index of small stocks rose 4.16, or 0.74%, to 569.64 and the S&P SmallCap 600 Index rose 1.85, or
0.66%, to 280.80.
Casino stocks slumped after Merrill Lynch downgraded its stock ratings on large-caps Mandalay Resort
Group and MGM Mirage. Alliance Gaming fell 79 cents, or 3.1%, to 24.38 and Aztar dropped 45 cents, or
1.7%, to 25.93, both on the NYSE. Argosy Gaming (NYSE) shed 96 cents, or 2.6%, to 36.58, after CIBC
World Markets downgraded the stock.
Biotech firms also saw their shares decline. Savient Pharmaceuticals fell 34 cents, or 9.5%, to 3.25 after the
company posted a lower first-quarter profit than it did a year ago. Albany Molecular Research fell 1.71, or
11%, to 14.53 after posting a drop in first-quarter earnings.
Intelligroup plunged 1.89, or 33%, to 3.90. The Edison, N.J., provider of technology-outsourcing services said
customer delays and longer sales cycles hurt its first-quarter revenue, prompting it to narrow its 2004 revenue
guidance.

House Money: How One Executive Reignited Goldman's Appetite for Risk --- Outsider Mr. Blankfein Places Big Bets and Others Follow; Profiting...
House Money: How One Executive Reignited Goldman's Appetite for Risk --- Outsider Mr. Blankfein
Places Big Bets and Others Follow; Profiting With Firm's Cash --- Handling Trading Blow-Ups
By Susanne Craig
2,468 words
5 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
In early 1995, reeling from trading losses, a group of Goldman Sachs executives huddled around a
conference table in the firm's downtown Manhattan offices plotting strategy. They shot down one idea after
another.
"We are talking ourselves out of everything," Lloyd C. Blankfein, a longtime manager of Goldman traders,
says he told his colleagues. To get the message across, Mr. Blankfein walked out of the meeting and placed
a multimillion-dollar bet -- his first ever with Goldman's own cash -- that the dollar would rise against the yen.
In the end, Mr. Blankfein won on two fronts: He correctly predicted the dollar's upward move, and his decision
to lead by example helped reignite Goldman's appetite for taking on risk. In the years since, traders managed
by Mr. Blankfein and his lieutenants have delivered hundreds of millions of dollars of profit to Goldman by
making winning bets with the firm's own money on everything from interest-rate movements to oil prices.
Those plump profits have fueled the rapid rise of Mr. Blankfein, 49 years old, who late last year was named
president and chief operating officer of Goldman Sachs Group Inc. And while Chief Executive Officer and
Chairman Henry M. Paulson Jr., himself only 57, is considered unlikely to leave anytime soon, Mr. Blankfein
is considered by insiders as the leading candidate to succeed him and grab one of Wall Street's most coveted
brass rings.
Mr. Blankfein may be the perfect symbol of how Goldman, which went public five years ago this week, has
evolved from an elite private partnership with a reputation as a staid adviser on corporate mergers to a
hard-driving public company that increasingly is focused on using its own capital to drive its business.
"Lloyd is hard to deny right now," says Jon Corzine, a former Goldman chief executive who's now a U.S.
senator from New Jersey.
Mr. Blankfein's rise is all the more striking because in many ways he's the ultimate outsider at the ultimate
insider's firm. Raised in housing projects in New York City, he put himself through Harvard College and
Harvard Law School with a combination of scholarships and financial aid. He started out litigating tax disputes
for the film industry in Los Angeles, driving to Las Vegas for weekend blackjack jaunts. He landed at
Goldman through the back door, taking a job as a gold salesman at its J. Aron &Co. subsidiary in 1981 after
the investment bank itself turned him down.
Even today, the way he dresses has been known to turn heads at Goldman. He used to walk around his
office without shoes -- though since he got promoted in December he keeps them on. In January, he showed
up for a risk-committee meeting in boots and black corduroy pants. "I think maybe I should go home and
change," Goldman executive Gary Cohn says Mr. Blankfein whispered to him.
But Mr. Blankfein is the key player in Goldman's emergence as Wall Street's boldest trading firm. Under him,
Goldman has slashed lower-margin businesses like stock trading and concentrated its resources in the more
lucrative -- but far more risky -- world of trading with its own cash. A growing portion of this type of trading is
so-called proprietary trading in which Goldman makes its own bets on things like the direction of interest
rates.
Goldman also puts an increasing amount of capital on the line for its customers. For instance, Goldman might
buy a mortgage portfolio from a customer and try to resell it for a profit. The firm's trading prowess helped
drive its 2003 profit to $3 billion in a difficult market, up from $2.1 billion the year before.
Now other Wall Street firms are following suit, building up their own desks so that their traders can bet the
house's money. Over the past year, Credit Suisse Group's Credit Suisse First Boston has added more
proprietary traders. Even gargantuan Citigroup Inc., which for years sneered at the higher risks of proprietary
trading, is considering entering the arena in a serious way, the firm told investors late last year.

--- Page 113 ---
Page 113 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"We believe increasingly that we need to lead by example and put our own capital in the game for the sake of
our shareholders," says Michael Clark, head of proprietary trading at Credit Suisse First Boston.
Until two years ago, Mr. Blankfein was still personally placing trades for the firm, one of the few senior
executives on Wall Street to do so. His ascent, though, comes with its own risk for Goldman -- a trading
blow-up. Goldman, like most Wall Street firms, has two main camps: traders who, among other things, try to
outsmart the market using the firm's money; and bankers who collect fees by advising corporate clients on
everything from mergers and acquisitions to issuing new shares.
Historically, the power base at Goldman tends to swing between the bankers and the traders, with one rising
as the fortunes of the other falls. The current chairman, Mr. Paulson, is a banker by trade and got his job
when Mr. Corzine had come up through the ranks as a bond trader. In 1998, when Goldman lost more than
$650 million after Russia defaulted on some of its debt, Mr. Corzine was forced out.
Mr. Corzine says it's inevitable that Mr. Blankfein also will wrestle with trading blow-ups. "And people will use
that as a basis to be damning of the proposition that long-run benefits [of trading] aren't worth the short-run
pain," says Mr. Corzine.
Already there has been a test: Under Mr. Blankfein's watch the firm lost almost $400 million in late 2003
because it bought mortgage portfolios, which plummeted in value after interest rates briefly shot up,
according to people familiar with the matter.
But Goldman traders were on the right side of more deals last year, and the bad mortgage-portfolio bet hardly
caused a hiccup to its earnings. The firm notes that its capital, currently $52 billion, has tripled since 1999,
increasing its ability to absorb any losses.
Goldman believes its risk-taking culture -- its willingness to stomach losses in the hopes that it will lead to
greater gains -- sets it apart from its rivals. Although there are no exact gauges of trading risk on Wall Street,
the most commonly used barometer is "value at risk," a figure that is supposed to measure how much money
can potentially be lost due to bad market conditions and the likelihood of such losses happening.
Under the direction of Mr. Blankfein, the amount of money at risk has been rising steadily. Goldman's last
quarterly results indicate that the average sum it could lose on any given day grew to $71 million, up from just
$28 million in the fourth quarter of 2000. By contrast, Morgan Stanley, Goldman's main rival in trading and
investment banking, estimates that in the third quarter, the last data available, it could lose a much more
modest $39 million on a bad trading day.
Mr. Blankfein, the son of a U.S. mail sorter, was born in the Bronx section of New York City. After graduating
from law school in 1978, he landed a job as a corporate tax lawyer at the firm of Donovan, Leisure, Newton
&Irvine in New York. Lawyer John Baity, who hired Mr. Blankfein during a campus-recruiting trip to Harvard in
1977, remembers him as a "solid B-plus student with a remarkable intellect."
Mr. Blankfein spent most of the time at Donovan Leisure shuttling between Los Angeles and New York,
representing the film industry in a dispute with the Internal Revenue Service. In those days, he used to smoke
two to three packs of cigarettes a day. And on Friday nights he and fellow lawyer Greg Ho would sometimes
jump in a rental car and head to Las Vegas to play blackjack. "If we don't show up Monday it's because we've
hit the jackpot," read the memo the pair would leave on their boss's desk en route out of town, says Mr. Ho.
By 1981 Mr. Blankfein was on track to make partner at the law firm but decided instead to switch gears in
what he calls a "pre-life crisis" and move to Wall Street. His then-fiancee and now-wife Laura cried when he
told her he was leaving his comfortable job at Donavan Leisure to take a job as a gold salesman at J. Aron, a
relatively unknown commodities-trading house.
Goldman had bought J. Aron a few months earlier. For years afterward, however, the elitist Goldman treated
J. Aron like a poor cousin. J. Aron traders eventually moved into Goldman's Manhattan headquarters but
were forced to ride a separate elevator bank to their fifth-floor offices. Mark Winkelman, a now-retired
Goldman executive, recalls how, shortly after Mr. Blankfein arrived at J. Aron, he joined his colleagues in
wearing red suspenders to mock Goldman's immaculately dressed investment bankers.
At J. Aron, Mr. Blankfein quickly shined as one of the firm's top gold salesmen. He also had a knack for
spotting other money-making opportunities, which quickly brought him to the attention of Goldman higher ups.
For instance, in 1982 he figured out a novel way to structure a big trade for an Islamic client that got around
the religion's strict rules about receiving interest. The complex trade, which hedged the Standard &Poor's 500
index against S&P futures to get around the prohibition, was the largest trade of its kind that Goldman had
ever handled, more than $100 million.

--- Page 114 ---
Page 114 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
He also showed he had what it took to survive on a rough and tumble trading floor. Mr. Winkelman says Mr.
Blankfein first caught his eye when he saw him wrestle the phone away from one of J. Aron's top traders who
wanted to yell at a Goldman client over a transaction that had cost Goldman money. The trader felt he had
been shortchanged by the client, but Mr. Blankfein realized that blasting the client wasn't going to help the
situation.
By 1994, Mr. Blankfein was running all of J. Aron. Four years later, he was promoted to co-head of Goldman's
fixed income, currency and commodities division. Weeks after he took over, the unit lost hundreds of millions
of dollars, after the Russian debt default, hurting bond prices. By this time, Mr. Blankfein was part of the
committee that was in charge of risk, but executives higher up the ladder, notably Mr. Corzine, the senior
partner seen as the champion of the traders, took the fall.
(MORE)
After that debacle, Mr. Blankfein led an effort to measure Goldman's risk exposure by looking at it firm-wide,
rather than division-by-division. For the first time, Mr. Blankfein insisted that weekly meetings of its senior
managers begin with a discussion of risk.
In another move, he tapped Bill McMahon as head of Goldman's trading-risk operations, the first trader to
ever hold this position. "The classic construct for a risk manager is a back-office guy," says senior Goldman
executive Jon Winkelried. "But as we decided to take more risk, we felt we needed a more in-your-face type
of risk-management approach."
Mr. Blankfein also had a more in-your-face management style himself. Until a few months ago he kept his
desk on the trading floor. His job included knowing how quickly Goldman could liquidate positions or simply
knowing if Goldman had too much riding on certain trades.
Mr. Blankfein continued to trade. His favorite play: predicting the move of the dollar against the yen. Goldman
Sachs currency trader Bennett Grau used to ride the Seventh Avenue subway line with Mr. Blankfein, who
would get currency quotes on his pager on the way home. Mr. Blankfein often waited for the train at the top of
the subway stairs rather than stand on the underground platform where his pager wouldn't work. "Then when
we heard the train coming we would run for it," Mr. Grau recalls.
But despite his passion for it, colleagues say Mr. Blankfein is a capable, but not outstanding trader. "He's
great at managing traders, but would never claim to be a star trader himself," says Mr. McMahon, the man
Mr. Blankfein tapped to oversee trading risk.
The dollar/yen remains one of Goldman's favorite bets as well, in part because of Mr. Blankfein's interest in it.
At one point in 2003, the firm had a $4 billion bet that the yen would rise against the dollar, according to
people familiar with the matter.
Mr. Blankfein remained bearish on the dollar throughout the year. "The dollar still has room to fall," he told
Robert Kapito, vice chairman of Goldman client Blackrock, in mid-November. "I know it seems like everyone
has this trade on, but sometimes everyone can be right." As it turns out, the dollar continued to weaken
against the yen until February.
In addition to managing risk, Mr. Blankfein in recent years has been slashing costs in Goldman's equities
department, which has been under pressure since the Internet bubble burst and stock trading and equity
underwriting dried up. Mr. Blankfein has laid off hundreds of stock sales people while building muscle on
certain other trading and sales desks, like foreign exchange and commodities, which the firm feels have more
profit potential. "During the last few years, I was the cost cutter. But before cutting became necessary in the
bear market I was a revenue builder and guess what, now I am a revenue builder again," he says.
Personally, Mr. Blankfein is less of a risk-taker than he used to be. Years ago, he gave up smoking and
recreational gambling. More recently, he has lost 50 pounds on a low-carb diet.
As president, he's spending more time on the road with high-profile clients. But the call of the trading floor
never seems far away. On Jan. 26, in London on his way back from the World Economic Forum at Davos, he
left a voice-mail for Mr. Winkelried, the Goldman executive. "There is a sense out there the market is pretty
frothy," he said. "I think we need to reduce our risk scenario for now."
Two days later, the Federal Reserve's Open Market Committee signaled that a future increase in interest
rates may be in the cards, sending the Dow Jones Industrial Average down almost 150 points to 10468.37.

Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...And Not: Shifting Landscape Stirs Investors --- Worry Over Interest Rates And a...
Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...And Not: Shifting Landscape Stirs Investors
--- Worry Over Interest Rates And a Slowdown in China Create Unease in Markets
By Craig Karmin
627 words
3 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
CONCERNS OVER rising U.S. interest rates and a slowdown in China's economic growth made April the
cruelest month for many global investors.
Securities across the world felt the pain. U.S. corporate and government bonds retreated, and commodity
prices fell. Speculative plays did even worse: Emerging-market stocks and bonds fell hard, especially Asian
markets with close links to China. European stock markets, which are less directly influenced by U.S. interest
rates, were among the few bright spots.
"People are thinking about what's the appropriate mix of securities to have in an environment of higher
interest rates, a stronger economy and higher inflation," said Mark McAllister, portfolio manager of Smith
Barney Capital &Income Fund. "It's tricky out there right now."
Surprisingly strong U.S. employment numbers a month ago ignited early worries that the Federal Reserve
was going to have raise interest rates sooner than economists expected. Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan
hinted shortly afterward the market should be prepared for high rates, which further fueled these concerns.
That meant bad news for bonds. U.S. government, corporate and high-yield (or junk) bonds all fell in the
month. The J.P. Morgan Emerging Market bond index, a star performer for most of the past two years, fell 6%
last month. Real-estate investment trusts, which are also greatly influenced by rates, did worst of all, falling
about 15%.
The long rally in emerging-market stocks also screeched to a halt last month. Russia's RTS Index tumbled
13%, and both Mexican and Brazilian stocks were weaker.
Securities tied closely to Chinese consumer demand suffered as Chinese policy makers tightened monetary
policy in an effort to slow that country's fast-growing economy before it overheats.
"The coming slowdown in China will undermine the region's newest source of external demand," Stephen
Roach, Morgan Stanley chief economist, wrote in a Friday report about Asia, "whereas a likely normalization
of U.S. interest rates could put downward pressure on the region's oldest source of external demand -- the
American consumer."
In South Korea, where sales to China represented 36% of export growth last year, stocks fell 2%. In Japan,
which is also becoming increasingly reliant on Chinese consumers, industrial stocks retreated about 7%. The
Shanghai SE Composite Index, meanwhile, tumbled 8%.
Commodity prices were also broadly lower, pinched by anticipation of waning Chinese demand. But crude oil
remained strong, rising 4.5% for the month, as supply limitations and growing global economic growth kept
prices firm.
European stocks, perpetual laggards over much of the past few years, were one of the lone bright spots for
the month. Benchmark indexes for Italy, Switzerland, and Germany saw modest gains. The Dow Jones Stoxx
Index of 600 European blue-chip companies rose 1%.
"European equity markets are in a sweet spot," said Michael Hartnett, global macro strategist for Merrill
Lynch. "The earnings picture looks good but there is no threat of rising rates."
Moreover, he adds, this year's weakness in the euro has helped lift sentiment for European exporters, which
gain a competitive advantage when the currency falls versus the dollar. J.P. Morgan's U.S. dollar index
gained 3% in April.

--- Page 118 ---
Page 118 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A rising dollar did take a toll on gold; the price of bullion typically moves in the opposite direction of the U.S.
currency and fell 9%.
But Abhijit Chakrabortti, global equity strategist for J.P. Morgan, predicts that gold won't lose its luster for
long. "Inflation expectations are coming back," he warns. "That means you want to be in gold."

Investors Await Factory Data, Fed Meeting and Jobs Report

English

NEW YORK -- The earnings season is winding down, but some investors continued buying index options to
protect their portfolios from further stock-price declines and a possible volatile market.
Among the catalysts that traders say can move the markets this week: data about manufacturing activity, a
Federal Reserve policy-making meeting and the closely watched April employment report due Friday.
The Chicago Board Options Exchange volatility index, or VIX, rose for a fifth straight day and increased 0.59
to 17.19 Friday. This forecast of short-term market volatility climbed 23% last week.
Optionetics analyst Frederic Ruffy was among those who think the market could become more volatile. "One
of the stabilizing factors through April was actually earnings," he said. Although the uncertainty about
corporate profits can sometimes stir the markets, the strong first-quarter profits many companies reported
actually had helped counter the jitters over rising interest rates and geopolitical tension. "But earnings is
almost over now," he said.
Gold and metals were among the most volatile sectors recently, and the Philadelphia Stock Exchange Gold
and Silver Index, or XAU, tumbled 22% in April.
Stacey Briere, Susquehanna Financial Group's capital-markets analyst, noticed some investors buying
options in general and bullish calls in particular on the gold and metals sector. "Given the significant selloff,
some investors may be positioning for a rebound, but they may not want to buy stocks outright and are using
option leverage," Ms. Briere said. Call holders lock in the right to buy stocks at certain prices, but don't risk
more than the price paid for the calls.
On Friday, the XAU index slipped 0.12 to 81.94. Its July 80 calls were off 20 cents to $6.30 as 2,512 contracts
traded in Philadelphia, compared with 19 outstanding contracts.
Nortel Networks Corp.'s calls also traded heavily. The stock tumbled about 35% last week amid widely
reported accounting problems that led to the firing of some senior officials, including its chief executive. It isn't
clear what drove the call trading. Some investors might be replacing their stocks with calls to reduce their
stock risk, or they might be using calls to position for a possible rebound.
Nortel stock closed down 11 cents to $3.74 Friday. The September 4 calls traded 23,246 contracts, compared
with 8,451 outstanding contracts, and were off 10 cents to 65 cents at the American Stock Exchange.
