Forex View: Federal Reserve, April Jobs Report May Affect Dollar
Currency Trading
Forex View: Federal Reserve, April Jobs Report May Affect Dollar
By Jamie McGeever Dow Jones Newswires
574 words
3 May 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- At something of an inflection point, the stakes are high for the dollar this week.
There are two potentially crucial events standing out in a week packed full of important data and
central-bank decisions: the April employment report and the Federal Reserve's meeting.
The dollar has rebounded impressively in recent months from the record and multiyear lows plumbed earlier
this year, as confidence in the strength of the U.S. economy and expectations of rising interest rates have
grown.
Verbal intervention by euro-zone politicians and European Central Bank officials, unprecedented actual
intervention in the currency market by Japanese monetary authorities, and an unwinding of overextended
bets against the dollar in favor of higher-yielding currencies -- known as "carry trades" -- have also
contributed to the dollar's recovery.
Indeed, the dollar last week hit fresh five-month highs against sterling, six-month peaks against the Swiss
franc and Australian dollar, and seven-month highs against the Canadian dollar. The price of gold, which
invariably moves in the opposite direction to the dollar, fell to a six-month low while yields on the 10-year
Treasury note reached their highest level in almost eight months.
Late Friday afternoon in New York, the euro was unchanged at $1.1981, and sterling gained to $1.7780,
compared with $1.7741 on Thursday. The dollar rose to 1.2957 Swiss francs from 1.2900 and to 110.51 yen
from 109.82 yen.
Emboldened by a string of better-than-expected U.S. economic data, global financial markets are preparing
for higher U.S. interest rates. Nobody expects the Fed to suddenly raise rates at its policy-setting meeting
tomorrow. But many observers expect the Fed to tweak the language in its statement to be released after the
meeting, taking out the pledge to be "patient" in removing its policy accommodation, while also changing its
assessment of inflation risks.
Bill Hummer, chief economist at Wayne Hummer Investments in Chicago, thinks this move toward a more
balanced view on rates is timely and welcome. "But it's also been factored into the [currency] market and the
10-year note, so I don't see it as being too earth-shattering" in terms of market impact, he said.
He expects the strength of the U.S. economy to continue to attract foreign capital into U.S. asset markets,
which should push the euro down toward $1.14 in coming weeks.
Given the choppy trading conditions lately, a smooth ride is by no means certain, however. Witness the
dollar's sharp gains last week against currencies linked to commodity production, such as the Australian and
New Zealand dollars. Those moves came on reports that China may be trying to slow its overheating
economy, which would damp demand for commodities and weigh on overall global demand.
The dollar slid sharply Thursday on data showing the U.S. economy grew 4.2% in the first quarter, less than
the forecasts for 5% or more.
Steve Barrow, chief currency strategist at Bear Stearns in London, expects that the Fed in its statement will
come across as slightly more optimistic on the outlook and that nonfarm payrolls will rise by between 150,000
and 200,000. "If these expectations prove wide of the mark, we could see some big moves in the dollar," he
said.

Review / Books: Exuberant Genius
Review / Books:
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Review / Books: Exuberant Genius
By Steve Forbes
1,050 words
30 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
W6
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ALEXANDER HAMILTON
By Ron Chernow
(Penguin Press, 818 pages, $35)
"IF WASHINGTON was the father of the country and Madison the father of the Constitution," writes Ron
Chernow, "then Hamilton was the father of the American government." You would think that such a
formidable achievement would make Alexander Hamilton as familiar, and as revered, as the many other great
figures of America's founding epoch. But he is strangely underappreciated, at times even scorned. Such
disrespect may soon be a thing of the past, thanks to Mr. Chernow's impressively thorough, superbly written
and carefully researched biography.
That we now have the richest economy that the world has ever seen is the fruit of Hamilton's astonishing
statecraft. As our first Treasury secretary, and against fierce opposition, he pushed through a far-sighted
program of financial management that not only revived a moribund economy but also gave it a durability and
flexibility that has made it a wonder ever since. We are all Hamiltonians today -- a hustling, bustling,
never-stay-still, always-looking-ahead, trying-to-get-better people.
"Hamilton took Constitutional principles and infused them with expansive life, turning abstractions into
institutional realities," writes Mr. Chernow. Among those realities were "a budget system, a funded debt, a tax
system, a central bank, a customs service, and a coast guard." What is more, Hamilton eloquently defended
his ideas in his writings and public statements. No other founder, notes Mr. Chernow, "articulated such a clear
and prescient vision of America's future political, military and economic strength or crafted such ingenious
mechanisms to bind the nation together." It helps for us to remember, when thinking on Hamilton's legacy,
that America's success was hardly an inevitability.
This "exuberant genius," as Mr. Chernow calls him, has long had a bad rap from historians, who too readily
swallow the partisan spinning of that formidable political propagandist Thomas Jefferson. Mr. Chernow's
biography puts paid to the "crude historical cartoon that pits `Jeffersonian democracy' against `Hamiltonian
aristocracy.'" Hamilton was a true democrat in that he created a society that would allow people like himself,
who start life with little or nothing, to achieve success. His "three most savage critics of the 1790's" -- that is,
Jefferson, Madison and Adams -- had a "static, archaic worldview that scorned banks, credit, and stock
markets," observes Mr. Chernow. "From this perspective, Hamilton was the progressive figure of the era, his
critics the conservatives."
When Washington took office, America's finances were a shambles. The country was suffering an acute
shortage of capital. Government bonds sold for pennies on the dollar. Hamilton consolidated the national and
state debts of the Revolutionary War. This so-called assumption gave bond holders around the country a
stake in the success of the new national government. Hamilton established a new system of taxation to
service those bonds, making them money good. This move, in turn, made the bonds themselves a form of
currency -- useful for paying taxes -- and a source of liquid capital as collateral for loans and bank notes.
Naturally, critics carped that Hamilton was rewarding speculators who had bought the bonds from deserving
but destitute Revolutionary War veterans. Hamilton responded by saying, as Mr. Chernow puts it, "that the
first holders were not simply noble victims, nor were the current buyers simply predatory speculators. The
original investors had gotten cash when they wanted it and had shown little faith in the country's future." In
such a way did Hamilton establish the "legal and moral basis for securities," showing them to be "freely
transferable," with buyers assuming "all rights to profit or loss in transactions." In short, the government must
not interfere. To Hamilton, the security of liberty and property were inseparable.

--- Page 124 ---
Page 124 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Hamilton's life story defies credulity. He was born out of wedlock on a Caribbean island (the savage system of
slavery there made Hamilton a lifelong abolitionist). By the time he was 14, his father had abandoned the
family, his mother had died and his would-be guardian had committed suicide -- not to mention other family
hardships, like bankruptcy and disinheritance. Mr. Chernow ably chronicles Hamilton's near miraculous
ascent from impecunious teenage orphan to Gen. George Washington's de facto chief of staff at age 22.
After the Revolutionary War, Hamilton played a key role in bringing about the Constitutional Convention in
1787 and then agitating for the Constitution's ratification. Under intense deadline pressure, he wrote most of
the essays in "The Federalist." Washington, when elected, made Hamilton his finance minister, from which
post he soon became, in effect, the nation's prime minister.
With success came controversy. During no other period in American history, not even the pre-Civil War
1850s, was political life so venomous. Mr. Chernow shakes his head in amazement at how vicious our
brilliant founders could be. Of course, Hamilton helped his opponents along, if inadvertently. At the
Constitutional Convention, he made a reference to monarchy in a way that allowed Jeffersonians to paint him
as a man who pined for kings and aristocrats. Years later, he allowed himself to be seduced by a woman
whose husband then blackmailed him.
The only thing missing in Mr. Chernow's biography is a proper discussion of Hamilton's decision to put the
dollar on the gold standard, where it remained -- except for the Civil War period -- until 1971. The gold
standard played a critical part in America's impressive growth, since it saved the dollar from Latin-American
or French-style inflations. Currency debasements undermine entrepreneurship and the rule of law.
In the 1800 presidential election, because of a quirk in the Constitution (subsequently fixed), Thomas
Jefferson and his vice-presidential candidate, Aaron Burr, ended up in a tie in the Electoral College. Hamilton
thought that his detested rival would be the better president and got enough of his party to throw the election
to Jefferson. Four years later, Burr and Hamilton ended up in a duel, a confrontation that Mr. Chernow vividly
recounts. Hamilton aimed his shot to miss. Burr aimed his to kill -- and he did. Two hundred years later,
however, Hamilton's spirit of energy, innovation, opportunity and mobility still vibrantly lives.
---
Mr. Forbes is editor in chief of Forbes magazine.

NEW YORK -- The Russell 2000 Index of small-capitalization stocks underperformed the broad market amid
a drop in metal prices that hammered stocks related to gold, steel and aluminum.
Commodity prices, particularly gold, dropped amid fears that efforts by China to slow its economy could
curtail demand for raw materials.
Large-cap Newmont Mining's first-quarter-earnings miss added pressure on gold stocks, which were the
worst industry performers. Bema Gold tumbled 41 cents, or 15%, to $2.40; Canyon Resources fell 41 cents,
or 11%, to 3.24; and Cambior lost 23 cents, or 8.3%, to 2.53, all on the American Stock Exchange.
Declines by steelmakers closely trailed those of gold producers. Carpenter Technology lost 1.83, or 6%, to
28.91; AK Steel Holding dropped 23 cents, or 4.3%, to 5.08; and Ryerson Tull declined 42 cents, or 3.5%, to
11.59, all on the New York Stock Exchange.
In the aluminum sector, Tredegar lost 80 cents, or 5.6%, to 13.55, on the Big Board.
Ned Riley, chief investment strategist at State Street Global Advisors, said: "The fact that China is attempting
to rein in some of its growth to avoid an inflationary spiral has raised a serious question about the longer-term
demand for copper, steel, aluminum and oil."
Concern surrounding demand for commodities weighed on a market already nervous about the likelihood of
rising interest rates.
Broad selling ensued. The Russell 2000 Index of small stocks lost 13.70, or 2.3%, to 577.06, and the
Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600 Index fell 5.49, or 1.9%, to 283.05. The Nasdaq Composite Index of
large-cap and small-cap stocks dropped 42.99, or 2.1%, to 1989.54.
On the upside, Dynegy (NYSE) rose 42 cents, or 11%, to 4.36, despite reporting a decline in first-quarter net
income. The advance was enough to lift the stock into large-cap status with a market value above $1.5 billion
and to push the electric-utility group higher. Duquesne Light Holdings closed up eight cents, or 0.4%, to 19.33
on the NYSE.
Makers of electric parts and equipment dropped as a group after large-cap Flextronics International said its
quarterly profit fell. Three-Five Systems (NYSE) tumbled 1.24, or 18%, to 5.55, after posting a wider
first-quarter loss than a year earlier.
Bucking the downdraft, Littelfuse edged up 52 cents, or 1.4%, to 38.30, after saying first-quarter earnings
rose significantly from a year earlier.
Large-cap Network Associates' disappointing outlook hurt the software sector. Retek lost 55 cents, or 6.9%,
to 7.46, and Openwave Systems slumped 38 cents, or 3.3%, to 11.17.
CSP soared 2.04, or 33%, to 8.14. The Billerica, Mass., Internet-software company reported net income of 13
cents a share for the second quarter, compared with a loss of six cents a share a year earlier.
Candela lost 5.34, or 32%, to 11.55. The Wayland, Mass., medical-laser concern said third-quarter net
income slipped to nine cents a share from 13 cents a share a year earlier.
Guitar Center rose 4.33, or 12%, to 41. The Westlake Village, Calif., retailer of guitars and amplifiers said
first-quarter net income rose to 46 cents a share from 22 cents a share a year earlier.

Metals Fall as Leaders of China Suggest Their Economy Will Cool
Commodities Report
Metals Fall as Leaders of China Suggest Their Economy Will Cool
By Peter A. McKay and Gavin Maguire
767 words
29 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- China blinked and precious metals took a hit.
Gold and silver prices sank after Chinese officials made remarks suggesting that country's economy will
slow. China is a massive consumer of precious metals.
The drop in metals weighed on prices of stocks such as U.S. Steel and Dow Jones Industrial Average
component Alcoa.
Analyst James Steel, of the commodity brokerage firm Refco, said metals were hurt by comments from
Chinese Premier Wen Jiabao, who said China will take steps to cool its economy, which has been a major
driver of world commodity markets for more than a year.
Also, China's State Council late Tuesday issued guidelines for companies in the cement, steel, aluminum and
property-development sectors to use more of their own capital toward their investments. The move could
force these companies to rein in their expansion and curtail fast credit growth, according to Chinese state
media.
While the guidelines didn't specifically say how Chinese companies' credit would be affected, commodity
funds viewed the event as a severely negative development for the metals markets in general.
Jim Pogoda, division vice president at Mitsubishi International Corp. said, "You can see how important a
factor Chinese demand has been in all the commodity markets by the sharp losses incurred today, and we
could well not have seen the end of it.
A surging dollar and rising interest rates also weighed on metals.
The June gold futures contract, the most-active, fell 3.5%, or $13.20, to $385.90 a troy ounce, the lowest
close in more than five months on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. May silver
futures plummeted 36.8 cents, or 6.7%, to $5.867 a troy ounce on heavy fund and bullion-bank sales.
Mr. Steel said this morning's expected announcement of strong first-quarter economic growth in the U.S. also
weighed on the markets, especially gold, which is often viewed as an investor haven during times of
economic weakness. A rising economy, and the increases in interest rates it would bring, would buoy the
dollar and hurt gold.
Gold could stabilize, since terrorism worries could keep some investors nervous enough to hold onto limited
quantities of gold, Mr. Steel said. But other metals may not fare as well in the short- to medium-term.
"What we're seeing right now is an affirmation of the market psychology expecting higher rates," Mr. Steel
said. "All the economic numbers have been good, so we'll probably see the Federal Reserve act sooner
rather than later."
Other metals fell in sympathy with gold and silver. Platinum declined $37 to $796.50 a troy ounce on Nymex,
with prices hitting contract lows of $791 during the session. Palladium was $24.35 lower at $253.60, its lowest
close since early March.
Because silver has widespread industrial applications for photography materials and other items, the white
metal prompted other manufacturing-oriented metals like copper, aluminum and nickel to move lower.
In particular, chart and momentum-following fund players sold silver aggressively during the first half of the
session. Compounding the softer tone of silver trade was the resilient tone of dollar trade through the day,

--- Page 130 ---
Page 130 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
which remained on a firm footing against its rivals to render dollar-denominated silver less appealing to
non-U.S. consumers.
"If the U.S. dollar manages to push higher in the near term, that could apply even more pressure to metals
prices and cause more weakness," said Mitsubishi's Mr. Pogoda.
Indeed, the persistently resilient tone of the dollar, among other factors, helped push gold to $384 by
mid-morning. Thereafter some funds bought, as did retail investors, but dealers said more weakness should
be expected in the near term should the dollar rally from here.
In other commodity trading:
CATTLE: Chicago Mercantile Exchange futures set new contract highs after beef packers competed
aggressively for near-term supplies of slaughter-ready live cattle. June live-cattle futures topped 80 cents for
the first time in history, hitting a contract high at 80.80 cents a pound before closing 2.62 cents higher at
80.45 cents a pound.
COTTON: Futures on the New York Cotton Exchange fell on massive selling by speculative funds amid
concerns that China could raise its interest rates to slow its booming economy. The worries also sparked a
selloff in other commodities such as soybeans. July cotton futures fell 3.0 cents to 59.63 cents a pound.

Dollar Gains Against Its Rivals Amid Optimism on the Economy
Currency Trading
Dollar Gains Against Its Rivals Amid Optimism on the Economy
By Jamie McGeever Dow Jones Newswires
974 words
29 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar rose across the board amid optimism over the strength of the U.S. economy, a
growing belief U.S. interest rates will have to rise soon, and a sharp slide in commodity prices, particularly
gold.
News that Beijing is taking steps to rein in domestic bank lending sparked fears that the rampant Chinese
economy might be about to slow down, which would likely herald a decline in global demand for raw
materials. That put heavy downward pressure on the Australian, Canadian and New Zealand dollars, and
commodities, especially gold.
Gold lost over 3% of its value, hitting a 5 1/2-month low of $383.65 a troy ounce as bond yields rallied and
stocks fell.
The prices of gold and the dollar often move in opposite directions. Against a backdrop of rising expectations
of a strong first-quarter U.S. gross-domestic-product report today and the upward implications this has for
interest rates, the dollar rose while gold declined.
"It's not clear to me that the dollar should be the big beneficiary [amid the global commodity selloff] and it's
noticeable that the euro held up above $1.1800," said Robert Sinche, chief global currency strategist at
Citibank in New York. "But what you had was a capitulation . . . of a lot of over-owned positions," he said,
citing long-sterling positions as an example.
The "constant factor" in financial markets, Mr. Sinche said, was the prospect of higher U.S. interest rates.
There was no major U.S. economic data on tap yesterday so attention is shifting to today's first-quarter GDP
report, the first of several top-tier releases in the coming week or so. Economists surveyed by Dow Jones
Newswires expect the U.S. economy to have expanded by an annualized 5.0% in the first quarter.
In late trading in New York yesterday, the euro was at $1.1834, down over a cent from $1.1939 late Tuesday
in New York and down at 130.24 yen from 130.64 yen. A large Asian central bank was said to be a big buyer
of euros at the lows around $1.1820-30, dealers said.
The dollar was at 110.12 yen, up from 109.43 yen late Tuesday. With liquidity thinning out in yen trading
ahead of Japan's Golden Week holidays that get under way today, there was less in the way of exporter
selling to prevent the dollar from scaling 110.00 yen for the first time since March 16.
The dollar was up against the Swiss franc at 1.3072 francs from 1.2968 francs, while sterling was weaker at
$1.7719 from $1.7925 late Tuesday in New York. Gold was off $13.20, at $385.90 an ounce.
But the dollar's biggest gains, however, were against currencies of commodity-exporting countries. It soared
to a seven-month high against the Canadian dollar at C$1.3744, well up from around C$1.3504 late Tuesday.
The Australian dollar plunged to 72.19 U.S. cents from around 73.49 U.S. cents late Tuesday.
Late Tuesday, China's State Council issued guidelines for companies in the cement, steel, aluminum and
property development sectors to use more of their own capital toward their investments.
Reports of a temporary moratorium on new lending by banks in China and repeated rallying calls by the
nation's top leaders to curtail overinvestment raised jitters that another round of credit tightening could soon
be on the way.
"Once it [China] starts slowing, it will be done at the sacrifice of commodity-producing countries," and their
currencies will suffer, said a dealer at a Japanese bank in New York. "The Canadian dollar and Aussie [dollar]
have played a larger role in currency markets" yesterday, than anything else, he added.

--- Page 132 ---
Page 132 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Developments at Canadian telecommunications-equipment titan Nortel Networks, which sent shares in the
company plunging as much as 28%, also weighed on the Canadian dollar, analysts said. The chief executive
was fired and the company said it plans to restate and revise 2003 and prior-year financial results. This year's
first-quarter earnings report, due out today, will also be delayed.
Alan Ruskin, director at currency researchers 4Cast in New York, reckoned commodity and metals markets
were overbought and "ripe for a slide on the stronger dollar, higher rates story anyway." This also applied to
emerging-market currencies, which suffered as investors sold emerging-market debt.
The Mexican peso closed at 11.3973 pesos per dollar compared with 11.2765 pesos at Tuesday's close.
Meanwhile, U.S. Treasury officials will travel to China in early May to continue discussions about China's shift
to a flexible exchange rate, U.S. Treasury Secretary John Snow said.
John Taylor, Treasury undersecretary for international affairs, and Paul Speltz, Mr. Snow's economic and
financial emissary to China, will represent the Treasury in the discussions with China next month, Mr. Snow
said.
"It is best for the global system, for the U.S. and for China itself, for China to move to a flexible exchange rate
regime," Mr. Snow said at a news conference that Bush administration officials held to speak about the
U.S.-China trade relationship.
"Importantly, China acknowledges this and is making progress toward this goal. The Chinese are actively
taking steps to modernize their financial infrastructure with the goal of achieving a flexible currency," Mr.
Snow said.
Mr. Snow and three other members of President Bush's cabinet appeared together at the conference to
explain the decision to reject a petition by the AFL-CIO to penalize China's poor labor standards with trade
sanctions, saying engagement is a more effective way to press for change.

Wednesday's Markets
Blue Chips Fall On Rate Fears, Middle East
By E.S. Browning
751 words
29 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES INDUSTRIAL Average suffered its worst decline in more than six weeks, falling 135.56
points, as fears about rising interest rates and Middle East violence eclipsed strong earnings reports.
Treasury bonds also fell sharply.
After knocking stocks down late Tuesday, worries about terrorist activity in Syria and fighting in Fallujah, Iraq,
carried over into the start of trading yesterday. With the first-quarter earnings season winding down after a
series of robust reports, investors are turning their attention to the future -- and what they see is Middle East
violence, rising interest rates and corporate scandal.
"Earnings are good, fundamental news is good, but geopolitical is bad, interest rates are bad," said David
Briggs, head of stock trading at Pittsburgh mutual-fund group Federated Investors. Figures for first-quarter
economic growth are due today and forecasts have been rising, Mr. Briggs noted. But instead of encouraging
investors, the anticipation of a strong economy is reinforcing fears the Federal Reserve will raise its target
interest rates to cool things off.
"Now you are hearing people say that the first rate hike is closer than we thought; it is going to be bigger than
we thought," Mr. Briggs said. The Fed's target rate for overnight bank lending is at 1%, a 46-year low, but
many analysts expect it to rise to 1.75% by year end.
The Dow industrials fell 1.29% to 10342.60, pushing them back into the red for the year -- down 1.1%. The
Nasdaq Composite Index, full of volatile technology stocks, suffered its own worst decline in six weeks, falling
2.12%, or 42.99 points, to 1989.54, its first dip below 2000 in a week. It also is back under water for the year,
down 0.7%.
Trading volume was heavy; the 1.84 billion shares that traded on the New York Stock Exchange marked the
third-biggest day of the year.
The decline in bond prices sent yields higher. The yield of the 10-year note, which represents the annual
return a bondholder receives, rose to 4.493%, the highest since September. Rising bond yields depress
economic growth by pushing up market interest rates that many businesses and consumers pay, including
the rates for many new fixed-rate mortgages.
The dollar, which becomes a more attractive investment when rates rise, hit a six-week high against the yen
and neared a five-month high against the euro.
Metals including gold fell after China's prime minister said the government may take action to slow the
country's blistering growth rate. Gold finished in New York at $385.40, its lowest close in almost six months.
Its decline of $13.20, or 3.3%, was the sharpest in dollar and percentage terms since January. Oil prices
initially rose, but then fell late in the day.
Investors were shocked by news that Nortel Networks fired its chief executive and is revising last year's profit
sharply down. Record volume in Nortel shares contributed to the heavy Big Board trading.
Some investors were disappointed Comcast dropped its hostile takeover bid for Walt Disney, since takeover
news has contributed to stock gains.
Investment strategist Francois Trahan at New York brokerage house Bear Stearns warned clients that
first-quarter earnings gains could prove hard to repeat. "Investors have been left to wonder why it is that
stocks are no longer advancing in the face of all this good news," he wrote in a report. "The message from
these indicators is clear: The best of times are behind us for stock prices, and more measured returns should
be expected going forward."

English

SURPRISINGLY STRONG readings for April consumer confidence and March existing-home sales sent
stocks up in the morning, but then reports of a terrorist attack in a diplomatic neighborhood of Damascus,
Syria, knocked them back down in the afternoon.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, up as much as 92.55 points shortly after trading began, finished with a

The Nasdaq Composite Index, filled with volatile technology stocks, fell 0.21%, or 4.24 points, to 2032.53, still
up 1.5% on the year.
Treasury bonds, often treated as a haven in times of worry, rose, as did gold.
Crude oil hit a three-year high in London and also rose in New York. It was pushed up by the Middle East
violence, by the strong economy and by comments from OPEC's president that the oil cartel may set a new,
higher target-price range. The dollar was mixed.
The underlying problem for stocks is still that the market is caught between dueling forces. Surprisingly strong
earnings and economic indicators are pushing stocks up, but fears of inflation and higher interest rates are
providing head winds. That creates a level of uncertainty that permits sudden shocks such as the Syria news
to disrupt trading, said Warren Epstein, head trader at New York brokerage firm Kabrik Trading.
"All of a sudden you hear something and you have to give it some credence," Mr. Epstein said. "These events
might be blips, but they still affect the market."
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.23%, or 2.58 points, to 1138.11, up 2.4% this year.

Corrections &Amplifications
THE U.S. ARMY Materiel Command postponed withholding some payments to Halliburton Co. in its
troop-support defense contract until mid-June to give the company more time to formally detail expenses. In
addition, the fee for Halliburton's Kellogg Brown &Root unit to run dining halls hasn't yet been determined,
though it will be based on a negotiated, estimated cost, rather than the actual cost. A page-one article on
April 28 incorrectly said that the government had already begun withholding some payments and that the
dining-hall fee had already been set. Finally, at the time KBR was given approval to build an ice plant in
Camp Arifjan, the company was directed to produce four pounds of ice per soldier per day. The article's
description of the guidelines provided to KBR failed to include that detail.
(WSJ May 11, 2004)
(END)
KUWAIT CITY, Kuwait -- Last fall, the U.S. military gave Halliburton Co.'s Kellogg Brown &Root unit a priority
order to provide troops in Kuwait and Iraq with ice before summer came around again. The guidelines were
minimal: "The contractor shall produce or subcontract and make available potable ice."
KBR took these vague instructions to their limit. It built a plant in southern Kuwait with a walk-in freezer and
delivery dock, and imported two industrial-size ice makers from east Texas that could churn out 40 tons a
day. It hired 28 people to run the plant around the clock. And it put its stamp on every bag of ice that gets
produced: "KBR Iceworks Inc. Serving the U.S. Military."
The deluxe ice service shows how KBR has made the most of its unique and powerful role as the sole
provider of many support services to the military. But it also suggests why the company faces tough
questions from government auditors about overbilling, complaints from suppliers about lost paperwork and
missed payments, and a scathing appraisal of its internal controls from KBR's in-house investigative team.
KBR's contract with the military contains big incentives to deliver goods and services in a hurry to keep Army
brass happy -- with little attention to the cost or efficiency of the solution. The intense conditions of wartime,
where last-minute orders are a matter of course, only increase the pressure to pay whatever is necessary to
complete a job fast.
At the same time, KBR has been surprised by how quickly its responsibilities expanded as the occupation of
Iraq progressed. Many of its systems, from procurement to billing, got overloaded, creating a breeding ground
for potential corruption and more inflated prices -- not to mention inefficiency on a huge scale.
Now the Pentagon's auditing agency is investigating KBR on several fronts, including whether it overcharged
the U.S. for fuel purchased in Kuwait and whether it billed for thousands of meals that weren't provided. The
problems uncovered so far, says the Pentagon's lead auditing agency, are "systemic."
In Kuwait, where the bulk of KBR's back-office operations are handled, paperwork is routinely lost and
purchased equipment is unaccounted for. Vendors are upset about millions of dollars in payments that are
months late. The top U.S. military officer in Iraq complained in March that its operations are being hindered by
delayed logistical support. Things have only gotten tougher for KBR during the heavy fighting in Iraq over the
past couple of weeks, as the company has been forced to halt convoys and take other safety measures.
Moreover, because KBR hasn't formally detailed all its expenses, the government has begun withholding
some payments to the company. Though Halliburton has billed the government for nearly $3 billion, as much
as $1.2 billion hasn't been reimbursed. The company has warned that a move to withhold more payments

--- Page 137 ---
Page 137 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
could hurt Halliburton's liquidity. Further, the stain of its performance woes could keep it from winning
contracts in the future.
Halliburton and KBR say they are working on the problems, which they say were caused largely by the rapid
expansion of its responsibilities. After winning a contract to provide logistical services and another to rebuild
oil fields in Iraq last year, the job grew exponentially. For instance, with little advance warning, the company
was asked to feed 130,000 troops in July, nearly triple the 50,000 troops it fed the month before.
"I don't think people appreciate the real-time nature of the work we're doing. This is not, `Can you do this in
two months?' This is, `Can you do this by the morning?' " said Dave Lesar, chairman and chief executive of
Halliburton, in a recent interview. The company was often forced to line up subcontractors for
multimillion-dollar contracts in a matter of days. Still, he acknowledges, "we could have anticipated the surge
better."
Company officials point out that responding quickly to Army needs is absolutely essential to its contracts.
They say the company's agreement also provides incentives to keep costs at or below budget.
Maj. Gen. Wade H. McManus Jr. of the Army Field Support Command says KBR must be able to respond to
military needs quickly, and costs can be high because "we are paying for that flexibility." But, he adds, even
during war, when budget constraints are relaxed, the military "is a pretty tough customer" and makes sure all
charges are reasonable.
Mr. Lesar says he expects government auditors eventually to vindicate the company, which in about a year's
time has pulled together 24,000 employees and subcontractors to launder thousands of bundles of clothes,
prepare millions of hot meals and provide other crucial services. A Pentagon agency that has broad oversight
over defense contracts reviewed KBR's Middle East purchasing system and found in April it was
"acceptable." A Pentagon official familiar with the process says the determination was based on one official's
summary look and isn't considered definitive.
Mr. Lesar blames politics for the focus on Halliburton's performance. Vice President Dick Cheney ran
Halliburton from 1995 to 2000, making the company a lightning rod for partisan attacks.
KBR's problems in Iraq trace back to decisions it made in setting up its back office. The company turned to
the playbook it had used as an Army contractor in the Balkans in the 1990s -- a much smaller operation with
very different needs.
KBR rehired dozens of former employees who had worked on that contract and transferred numerous others
directly from its offices in the Balkans. It adopted record-keeping methods it had used there -- writing some
orders by hand and generating others with simple computer spreadsheets. Only later, when that system got
swamped, did KBR add an enterprise-software system that allowed officials to track purchases more
efficiently.
Early on in Iraq, KBR even tried to order Volvo trucks with high-torque engines designed for the mountainous
Balkans. These trucks are poorly adapted to the flat Persian Gulf region, and in fact aren't even available
there.
Just as important was a lesson KBR imported from the Balkans: Keeping the military brass happy was more
important than focusing on spending. Indeed, that rule is built into KBR's agreement with the Army.
Under its contracts, the government pays KBR its costs plus a small profit margin of 1%. In addition to its
direct costs, it can bill as cost a percentage of its overhead, all the way up to its Houston headquarters offices
and executives.
Once the work is complete, a committee of military brass determines if KBR should get an additional
performance bonus of up to 2%. In the committee's scoring system, 60% of the grade is based on the
company's performance, such as adhering to schedule, quality of work and problem-solving, while only 40%
is based on cost control.
"You have to be responsive to their critical needs, and everything is critical," says Tom Crum, KBR's Middle
East region operations chief. "What you can't do is give them excuses why you can't do something."
This focus led to trouble in 2000, when Congress's investigative accounting arm criticized KBR and the Army
for cost overruns in the Balkans. Its report found that the Army didn't do a good job of controlling costs -- and
that KBR, in response, became overzealous in filling orders. For instance, KBR built a backup power system
for an entire base in Kosovo even though only the hospital and command center required dependable
electricity, according to the report. Another billing-related investigation -- potentially including fines -- hasn't
been resolved.

--- Page 138 ---
Page 138 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
KBR declines to comment on Kosovo. A KBR spokeswoman says it wasn't a mistake to use "lessons learned"
from the Balkans to set up operations in Middle East operations, but "no one" anticipated the huge amount of
work and the need to make adjustments.
In Iraq, staffers found themselves working seven days a week, often 12 hours a day, in the company's
regional headquarters -- an isolated former resort on Kuwait's beachfront, a 45-minute drive from Kuwait City.
KBR may have cut corners to get the job done. On at least two subcontracts let in July, KBR didn't "conduct
price analyses" of all subcontractor proposals, the federal Defense Contract Audit Agency found. Further, La
Nouvelle General Trading Co. was awarded a $44 million dining-hall contract without competitive bidding.
KBR also didn't provide a cost analysis showing that the bid was fair and reasonable.
Phone messages left at La Nouvelle's Kuwait City offices weren't returned. In a written response to the
Defense Contracting Audit Agency, KBR said the dining-hall contract was "a unique situation due to the
significant cost, amount of data and volume of effort." In general, KBR wrote, the company "strives to obtain
competition" on quotes, and provides cost analysis of proposed subcontractors when required.
But some former KBR employees say managers regularly pushed them to skirt competitive-bidding rules to
get work done quickly and meet military deadlines. "We all wanted to do competitive purchasing and do it
right," says Henry Bunting, who left Kuwait in July and returned to his home in Houston. "The supervisors
discouraged it. When you went out and got two bids, you caught hell." (Mr. Bunting left his job voluntarily and
didn't disclose any conflicts or grudges in sworn testimony before Congress.)
In particular, KBR employees say they were told to sidestep Pentagon regulations by splitting up large orders.
Under the rules, any order of more than $2,500 must be opened up for competitive bidding. In July, one KBR
supplier, Al Hasawi Industrial Group, received an e-mail at 6:15 p.m. seeking 500 sleeping bags to be
delivered to a military base that night. But when the purchase order was faxed, it was chopped into four
separate orders for 125 sleeping bags, with each order right at the $2,500 threshold, according to a purchase
order reviewed by this newspaper.
(MORE)
Emilio Bayhonan, retail manager for Al Hasawi, says he found it odd, but didn't press the issue. "They said
they had a budget system, so I didn't ask more questions," he says.
KBR says it doesn't condone splitting orders, and if its buyers did so, it was only to lighten their workloads --
not to deliberately skirt Pentagon rules. As for Mr. Bunting, KBR officials argue that if he had complaints, he
could have raised them over the company's ethics hotline.
KBR seems to have faced even more basic problems of internal organization as its systems were swamped
with orders. Many suppliers, and the government, began complaining about lost paperwork and other
logistical problems.
Late last year, Ahdy Boutros, managing director of KBR supplier Idris National Trading Co., called KBR after
a bill for leased generators wasn't paid within 30 days. At first, he says, he had trouble finding anyone at KBR
who was familiar with his multimillion-dollar contract. Then he says he was told the purchase order and
invoice had been misplaced. The payment paperwork needed a supervisor's signature, but the supervisor
was out of the office. Mr. Boutros ended up borrowing money from a local bank. KBR paid him five months
late.
KBR wouldn't comment on specific contracts. In general, the spokeswoman says, delayed payments are
often caused by subcontractors who aren't providing the data KBR needs.
With costs and criticism rising, the Pentagon sent auditors late last year to look into KBR's operations. They
found significant problems, including serious miscommunication between the company's Kuwait operations,
which awarded contracts, and its Houston offices, which billed the military. KBR, for instance, estimated it
was going to bill the government $208.8 million to run dining halls at seven locations, but the Kuwait office
ended up signing contracts for just $141.5 million. Under KBR's contract, the company's fee was based on
the much higher estimated figure, not actual costs.
"There are many excuses and reasons available -- but -- in the end, KBR did not include the most current
data in our proposal," KBR wrote in a letter to the Defense Contract Audit Agency. The company agreed to
withhold $176 million of food-services billing until a Pentagon investigation is concluded.
After the Pentagon auditors pressured KBR to beef up its internal controls, KBR dispatched an 80-person
"Tiger Team" to "tweak" the system, says Mr. Crum, KBR's head of Middle East operations. The team in
February produced a scathing internal memo, which the company called a working draft, that highlighted a
number of problems. For example, one of the government's principal concerns was KBR's handling of

--- Page 139 ---
Page 139 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
subcontractors, which accounted for half of contract costs, much higher than anticipated. The Tiger Team's
conclusion? "We did not equip, prepare or adapt to `outsourcing' work," the team wrote.
The Tiger Team found that in many cases the company couldn't locate equipment it had leased, such as
tents, generators and vehicles -- a state of affairs that frustrated and confused some suppliers. In February,
KBR supplier Al Hamra Kuwait Co. received an e-mail from KBR buyer Larry Ralls in Baghdad. He asked
about a shipment of building materials for the Perfume Palace, one of Saddam Hussein's former residences
that now houses military and the Coalition Provisional Authority offices. The e-mail read in part: "Please
provide some background details on the hijacking of 1st floor materials that just occurred."
Officials at Al Hamra were baffled. "Is this meant for us?" responded Tony Jeha, an Al Hamra engineer. "No
material has been hijacked. All requirements for Perfume Palace being presently loaded in Kuwait."
A KBR spokeswoman says that KBR has a reliable means of tracking shipments, but "occasionally"
information is delayed because of "communication challenges." In general, she says that working with a
mobilized military requires constant readjustments and can lead to misunderstandings. "It's not business as
usual in a war zone," she says.
KBR committed to installing e-commerce software after the Tiger Team found that "transaction processes are
disorganized and with weak internal controls." KBR also beefed up worker training on complying with
company policy in areas such as gratuities, kickbacks and record-keeping. Earlier this year, Halliburton fired
two Kuwait-based employees for accepting kickbacks in exchange for steering a contract to a local company.
Halliburton reimbursed the government $6 million to cover the overcharges. The government is investigating.
Many subcontractors are still trying to get paid. As of early March, KBR told the Pentagon, it owed nearly
$500 million to several dozen subcontractors and vendors. But the actual amount appears to be considerably
larger: About a dozen Kuwaiti companies say they were owed money that wasn't included in the tally. KBR
declined to comment on subcontracts awaiting payment.
Every day, Jaison Varghese, general manager of Fitco Technical Services, a computer and
electronics-supply firm, updates a three-page list of orders he has filled but hasn't been paid for. As of
mid-March, his company was owed about $730,000. The oldest past-due invoice is from last June.
Still, Mr. Varghese continues to work with KBR. "We're already neck-deep in the mud," he says. "We have to
keep the relationship to get the money out." But he has stopped offering KBR a wholesale price. He now
charges retail.
---
Neil King Jr. in Washington contributed to this article.

Halliburton's KBR unit faces numerous investigations, audits and other
problems.
-- The Pentagon has opened a criminal investigation into whether KBR
committed fraud by overcharging the government for fuel.
-- Vendors and subcontractors say KBR sometimes doesn't pay for goods and
services for months.
-- Pentagon auditors are investigating dining-hall contracts, some that
KBR awarded without competitive bidding.
-- The top U.S. military officer has criticized KBR for failing to provide
adequate logistical support.

recommends significant upgrades.

Stocks Drop as Rate Fears Surface Again
Monday's Markets
Stocks Drop as Rate Fears Surface Again
By E.S. Browning
432 words
27 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WITH THE BULK of the big-name quarterly earnings reports behind them, investors began wringing their
hands again over the likelihood of rising interest rates, and those worries helped knock down stocks.
Technology stocks, which rose nearly 3% last week, couldn't continue their gains, and they led the market
lower.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 28.11 points, or 0.27%, to 10444.73, pushing the blue chips back
slightly into the red for the year so far -- down nine points from where they began 2004. Trading volume was
below average.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, filled with technology stocks, declined 0.63%, or 13 points, to 2036.77, still up
1.7% on the year.
"The earnings were very good, but people are worried about interest rates," said Todd Leone, head of listed
trading at New York brokerage firm SG Cowen. "People just have no idea when the Federal Reserve is going
to raise rates."
New-home sales hit a record in March, fueling fears that the Fed will have to raise rates to cool the economy.
Higher rates, investors fear, could make it harder in the future for companies to keep recording strong
earnings gains, removing one of the market's main underpinnings.
Worries about terrorism and Iraq also contributed to the selling, driving up the prices of crude oil and gold
after reports of attacks on Iraqi oil-export terminals. Oil stocks also rose.
Treasury bonds were up slightly and the dollar declined.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.44%, or 5.07 points, to 1135.53, still up 2.1% on the year.
The Russell 2000 small-stock index fell 0.21%, or 1.26 points, to 589.45, still up almost 6% on the year.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.40%, or 0.61 point, to 152.53.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.29 billion shares traded, 1,223 stocks rose and 2,084 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 1/32, or 31.25 cents for each $1,000 invested. The yield,
which moves inversely to price, fell to 4.448%. The 30-year bond was up 4/32 to yield 5.235%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 108.71 yen, down from 109, while the euro rose against
the dollar to $1.1874 from $1.1837.

NEW YORK -- Small-capitalization stocks slipped with the overall market but biotechnology issues held on to

Small companies that are developing cancer treatments saw their shares soar after large-caps Genentech

lower late in the day. Some investors are also worried about whether companies can keep up the quarterly
profit gains.

Stocks Lift Off As Good News Is Finally Good
Thursday's Markets
Stocks Lift Off As Good News Is Finally Good
By E.S. Browning
836 words
23 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
OH, SO A STRONG economy is a good thing?
After almost a month of anemic trading driven by paradoxical fears that a reinvigorated economy is bad for
stocks because interest rates will rise, the market rallied on news that companies are raking in big bucks.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose almost 144 points -- its biggest gain since March. Companies
including eBay and Caterpillar reported strong quarterly earnings. eBay's stock rose 10% to a record finish,
and Caterpillar advanced 4%.
Trading was heavy, with volume on the New York Stock Exchange hitting 1.83 billion shares, the fourth
highest level this year.
After regular trading ended, Microsoft delivered a surprisingly strong earnings report and forecast
stronger-than-expected sales and profits to come. That news sent the software giant's stock up 4.8% in
afterhours trading and could affect the market today.
"Better corporate earnings finally outweighed the interest-rate anxiety that was out there," said Bob Basel,
senior trader at Citigroup's brokerage arm in New York. "We have had a slew of good earnings that people
haven't been paying attention to, and people finally started looking at them more closely. The question is, can
we sustain the move, and I would say, yes, there is some sustainability."
The Dow industrials rose 143.93 points, or 1.40%, to 10461.20. They now once again are showing a small
gain since the year began -- a razor-thin advance of less than eight points.
Other widely followed indexes advanced, as did bond prices, signaling that bond investors also overreacted
to interest-rate fears. Bond yields -- the return investors get -- fell. The dollar, which has been hitting recent
highs against the euro, fell back against Europe's common currency, but rose against the yen. Gold, which
has been falling, stabilized.
For most of this month, the big news for stocks has been the sudden realization that the Federal Reserve is
likely to begin raising its target interest rates some time this summer. Futures markets suggest that investors
expect the Fed to move by August, and possibly by as early as June.
Currently, the Fed's target for its benchmark federal funds rate (for overnight lending between banks) is 1%, a
46-year low. Many investors think it could be at 1.5% or even 2% by year end.
While that still would be very low by the standards of the 1990s, it represents a sharp change. And tiny rates,
which help businesses and consumers, have been one of the factors investors have used to justify the fact
that the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index currently trades at 22 times its component companies' earnings,
far above the historic average of about 15.
But after bidding stocks sharply down Tuesday, after Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan hinted that a rate
increase could come this year, investors now seem to be reconsidering. "What we may have is a period when
rates may go up, but the stock market may keep going up as well -- and the economy may rally and profits
may keep coming," said James Paulsen, chief investment strategist at Wells Capital Management, a
money-management arm of Wells Fargo.
Analysts have been reminding investors for several days that inflation and interest rates no longer are as
high as they were in the 1970s, 1980s and 1990s. They hope that means that a rate increase might not be as
stifling to growth and to stock values.

--- Page 144 ---
Page 144 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the 1950s and 1960s, when inflation and rates were lower, the stock market didn't tend to sag when rates
went up. It often rose, because rate increases were taken as a sign of economic strength.
Today, as they did back then, some people seem to have decided that a strong economy is good news for
stocks and that any Fed move will be mild.
The S&P 500 rose 1.41%, or 15.84 points, to 1139.93, leaving it up 2.5% this year. The Nasdaq Composite
Index, whose many technology stocks are rebounding after suffering during the period of interest-rate anxiety,
rose 1.87%, or 37.28 points, to 2032.91, back above the 2000 level and up 1.5% this year.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.10%, or 0.15 point, to 151.31.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, 2,465 stocks rose and 839 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 16/32, or $5 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, fell to 4.375%. The 30-year bond was up 19/32 to yield 5.193%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 109.52 yen, up from 109.43 yen, while the euro
strengthened against the dollar to $1.1906 from $1.1831.

Companies Regain Pricing Power, Brightening the Profit Picture
Companies Regain Pricing Power, Brightening the Profit Picture
By Jon E. Hilsenrath
1,554 words
23 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corporate executives are beginning to see glimmerings of a long-lost ingredient in their efforts to boost
profits: pricing power.
After three years of intense pressure to cut prices, in the past few weeks a widening circle of companies has
signaled a shift:
-- Motorola Inc. said average selling prices for its cellphones during the first quarter rose 16%, compared with
a year ago, and 12%, compared with the fourth quarter.
-- Vivendi Universal SA's Universal Music Group announced it was raising wholesale prices and suggested
retail prices on most compact discs after slashing prices last year.
-- Colgate-Palmolive Co. said earlier this week that its Hill's pet-foods unit raised prices by 3% to 4% earlier
this month.
-- Caterpillar Inc. expects to increase prices on its heavy machines by 2% to 3% starting in July.
-- In a sign of the global nature of the rebound, Mexico's Cemex SA, the world's third-largest cement maker,
said price increases in several of its 30 international markets helped its net profit to more than triple in the first
quarter. The average U.S. price for the ready-mix product that it delivers to job sites in trucks rose by 3% in
the first quarter.
For some companies, the brighter price picture has contributed to a thus far robust corporate earnings
season. The stock market gained sharply yesterday after several anemic finishes. The Dow Jones Industrial
Average jumped 143.93 points, or 1.4%, to 10461.20, while the Nasdaq composite surged 37.28, or 1.9%, to
2032.91.
"It's fairly apparent that pricing power is gradually being restored," Federal Reserve Chairman Alan
Greenspan said in comments to the Senate Banking Committee earlier this week.
Many of the price increases have been modest, and it's still possible that they will be reversed. Indeed Mr.
Greenspan said that the nation's overall inflation rate remains in check. But for executives who have spent
the past few years cutting prices to avoid losing customers to competitors, the return of some pricing power --
the ability to maintain or raise prices -- will offer welcome relief. It could help juice capital investment and
hiring.
After profits tanked in late 2000, executives frequently lamented a loss of pricing power. They regarded that
as a critical missing factor in the economic recovery, holding back their willingness to invest in new projects
and hire new workers. When companies are forced to cut prices -- as many were in 2001, 2002 and 2003 -- it
can squeeze profit margins and force companies to cut costs aggressively to buttress profits.
Renewed pricing power could be good news for workers, because it could give executives the confidence to
hire more aggressively. Still, there are few signs that workers' wages are rising along with prices. That means
many households could find themselves in the months ahead paying higher prices without much change in
their income.
Being able to hold the line on pricing can mean less pressure to cut costs and better prospects for strong
revenue growth. Among 248 companies in the Standard &Poor's 500 index that have reported first-quarter
earnings so far, revenue was up 12.5%, compared with a year earlier, the largest increase since the first
quarter of 2001, according to Thomson First Call, which tracks earnings estimates.
Operating earnings per share -- which exclude one-time items -- were up 26.1% among those 248
companies. That's a slowdown from 28% growth in the fourth quarter for the full S&P 500, but still much

--- Page 146 ---
Page 146 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
stronger than last year. Year-over-year comparisons are going to get harder as the year progresses and the
quarterly basis for comparison from last year improves, but analysts are still expecting double-digit profit
gains all year.
"We're going to continue to get good growth in profits," says John Lipsky, chief economist with J.P. Morgan.
"Part of the profit growth is going to be from raising prices."
For much of the past few years, intense competition at home and excess capacity after the boom of the late
1990s left many companies with little chance of making price increases stick. At the same time, a strong
dollar fostered another source of competition, making goods produced outside the U.S. relatively cheap.
Economists say four factors are helping to turn the tide on price pressures. First, low interest rates and
income-tax cuts have bolstered consumer spending on everything from cellphones to new homes to personal
travel. Second, the weakened dollar is making imports more expensive, which eases the pressure on
domestic producers to cut their own prices to compete. During the first quarter, import prices (excluding
petroleum) rose at a 5.9% annual rate, the fastest pace of increase since 1990.
Third, the world's rising appetite for a wide range of commodities, from oil to steel to paper, has overwhelmed
supply and sent prices surging. Increasing raw-materials prices have forced some companies to respond by
charging more themselves. Finally, the collapse of companies that couldn't keep up with the price-cutting has
helped to reduce excess capacity.
The trend is showing up in other ways. The Labor Department reported Thursday that producer prices of
manufactured goods -- excluding the volatile food and energy sectors -- were up 0.7% in March from a year
earlier. That was not as large as the 1.1% increase reported in February, but it still represented a rebound
after declines in 2002 and early 2003. Moreover, the National Federation of Independent Business, a trade
association for small companies, reported last month that 24% of its members reported raising prices, up
from 13% who raised prices a year earlier.
One example of revived pricing power is the Mandalay Resort Group in Las Vegas. The operator of popular
hotels such as Circus Circus and Mandalay Bay was hit hard by the 2001 recession and the Sept. 11 terrorist
attacks. Between 2001 and 2002, average room rates at Circus Circus dropped to $55 from $57 and rates at
its Gold Strike facility dropped to $62 from $69. With the overall drop in prices, the first in at least 20 years,
net income declined to $53 million from $120 million.
Now room rates are staging a comeback, says Chief Financial Officer Glenn Schaeffer, helped by the
economic recovery and by the weak dollar, which is keeping more U.S. travelers from venturing abroad. He
adds that the company has been able to raise prices without increasing its costs, meaning that the bulk of
every price increase leads directly to profit gains.
The turnaround hasn't favored all. The telecommunications and computer industries report persistent
pressure on prices. AT&T Corp. said Thursday that its first-quarter revenue declined 11% to $8 billion
because of pressure on prices.
But some other hard-hit sectors are trying to make price increases stick. Travelers have come to expect the
lower airfares ushered in by a growing number of discount carriers such as JetBlue and Southwest. But
Northwest Airlines, the typical spoiler of competitor-led fare boosts, appears to have succeeded with a 3%
fare increase initiated late Tuesday. By yesterday major airlines had matched the increase as had some of
the smaller low-cost carriers.
Some companies that are raising prices are still moving cautiously. Ford Motor Co. said earlier this week that
it was able to raise vehicle prices by 1.2% in the first quarter from a year earlier, but it lost some market share
in the process and the increase was driven largely by the success of its popular F-Series pickups. George
Pipas, a Ford sales analyst, notes that the industry faces a global capacity glut, which will maintain downward
pressure on prices for some time. "I don't think anybody has got their spinnaker out in the auto industry, riding
the wind of pricing power," Mr. Pipas said.
In many cases, companies have had to work hard to achieve price gains. One critical factor in Motorola's
price increases: The company launched 26 new models during the fourth and first quarters. New cellphones,
with more features, tend to sell for more than older models. The company said that about 60% of the phones
it shipped during the first quarter had color screens or built-in cameras or both. The increases may be
sustainable only if Motorola continues to introduce new features, such as built-in MP3 players, which the
company says it is planning to do.
In other cases, price gains haven't been enough to offset previous cuts. Universal Music Group's price
increases last week marked a turnaround from an aggressive cut in those prices last September, when the
industry leader attempted to reinvigorate sluggish sales by slashing wholesale prices as much as 25% and
suggested retail prices by about 33%. But Universal didn't reverse course entirely: Having cut wholesale

--- Page 147 ---
Page 147 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
prices to $9.09 from $12.12 last year, the company has now raised them just 40 cents, and raised suggested
retail prices to $13.98, from $12.98 -- still far below the industry standard of $18.98. Competitors haven't
followed Universal's lead.
---
Melanie Trottman in Dallas, Jesse Drucker in New York and Ethan Smith in Los Angeles contributed to this
article.

Stocks Rise Slightly On Strong Earnings From Motorola, Ford
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Rise Slightly On Strong Earnings From Motorola, Ford
By E.S. Browning
394 words
22 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS SHOOK OFF another speech by Alan Greenspan and stocks rebounded slightly, celebrating
good earnings from the likes of Motorola and Ford.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, which fell 123 points Tuesday after the Fed chairman signaled a coming
interest-rate increase, rose 2.77 points, or 0.03%, to 10317.27, still down 1.3% this year.
The expectation of higher rates helped send the dollar to another five-month high against the euro. Bonds,
which slumped Tuesday on the rate news, recovered some of their losses. "We had such an overreaction on
Tuesday to the Greenspan comments that we are just taking back some of what we shouldn't have lost," said
Stephen Sachs, director of trading at mutual-fund group Rydex Investments in Rockville, Md.
Gold continued to fall, declining $6.90 to $390.80, a five-month low. Silver suffered its biggest one-day
decline in 16 years. Crude oil also fell.
Ford's profit more than doubled and Motorola's more than tripled; both stocks surged. Technology stocks,
which had suffered the most Tuesday, led the rebound. The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.86%, or 17
points, to 1995.63, though it still is down 0.4% on the year. The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose
0.53%, or 5.94 points, to 1124.09, leaving it up 1% this year.
Mr. Greenspan indicated in comments to Congress's Joint Economic Committee inflation appears moderate,
but said rates "must rise at some point."
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 1.12%, or 1.71 points, to 151.16.
In major U.S. market action:
Major stock indexes advanced. But on the Big Board, where 1.71 billion shares traded, just 1,560 stocks rose
and 1,771 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 8/32, or $2.50 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, fell to 4.437%. The 30-year bond was up 16/32 to yield 5.233%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 109.43 yen, up from 108.66, while the euro weakened
against the dollar to $1.1831 from $1.1860.

The Curse of Currency Autarky
The Curse of Currency Autarky
By Benn Steil
1,179 words
22 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The challenge facing the IMF's International Monetary and Financial Committee as it assembles for its spring
meeting in Washington this weekend is to find a politic way of saying what its members already know: The
emperors who adorn the vast bulk of the world's monies have no clothes. Only by eliminating the deadly germ
of monetary sovereignty will currency crises become yesterday's disease, and will globalization live up to its
economic and political promise.
The past decade has been marked by a series of major financial crises in so-called emerging economies.
From Latin America to Russia to Asia and back to Latin America again, all of these have, to varying degrees,
strained America's political relations with the affected countries and transparently politicized the work of the
IMF. More importantly, in spite of the fact that all of these crises were ultimately brought on by currency
mismatches in national balance sheets, we have come not a jot closer either to integrating these currencies
into the global capital market or to figuring out how to prevent financial catastrophes when their governments
try.
The IMF was created after World War II specifically to support a dollar-centered world, not a world of over a
hundred autarkic currencies. The simple idea was that the Fund could assist countries in maintaining a fixed
rate to the dollar with limited, short-term financing to ease temporary balance of payments problems. Over the
past two decades, this world has changed beyond recognition, and has placed demands on the Fund it
cannot possibly meet. The Bretton Woods system of fixed exchange rates enabled countries to control their
interest rates while keeping their currency rate locked to the dollar (and gold) only because capital flows
were restricted. Yet as governments and private institutions in developing countries began seeking
international capital in the 1980s, many of their central banks and treasuries sought simultaneously to
maintain low domestic interest rates and a stable dollar exchange rate, with disastrous results for their
economies.
This put the Fund in an impossible position, from which it has been struggling unsuccessfully to adapt or to
extricate itself ever since. In affirming the benefits of international capital, the Fund did no more than ratify the
common sense that its growing clientele had already signed on to unilaterally: that access to a larger pool of
capital lowered its cost. Problems emerged, however, when these governments also adopted dollar pegs to
keep their exchange rates stable and simultaneously to anchor low inflation expectations domestically.
Despite muted concerns that Fund staff expressed periodically over the dangers of overvaluation, it was
impossible politically to challenge these policies openly, as to do so risked undermining their apparent
success by scaring off the eager foreign investors.
By the time investors, domestic as well as foreign, did eventually smell whiffs of default risk in the exchange
rate, it was too late for the Fund to do anything other than try to contain the flames. As a bank, the only way it
could do so was through a combination of loans to forestall default on existing debts and policy conditionality
to ensure that the governments would emerge capable of repaying the new debt to the Fund.
While the Fund has admitted mistakes in extending traditional conditionality, based on transparent
requirements for client fiscal probity, into peripheral and highly sensitive areas such as corporate governance
and "cronyism," it remains an ineluctable principle of banking that the bank must control its clients' behavior
sufficiently to ensure that loans can be repaid and recycled into future loans. Even a bank without a
profit-making mission must do so successfully in order to perpetuate itself. That the Fund has come popularly
to be seen, and indeed seeks to be seen, as an institution with a humanitarian mission, however, assured
that it would subsequently become popularly reviled in appearing to impose suffering on the poor in their
moments of crisis. This was the case even where it should have been clear that the governments in crisis
would have had to impose far harsher economic measures to avert default were IMF loans unavailable to
give them breathing space for longer-term reforms to take hold.
---

--- Page 152 ---
Page 152 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Yet the Fund has been subjected to another indictment, equally inevitable, from the right of the political
spectrum. In lending massive sums of money to indebted governments, the Fund is wide open to the charge
that it is "bailing out" irresponsible private lenders with public funds, and thereby encouraging even more such
irresponsible borrowing and lending in the future. This is known as the problem of "moral hazard," which
refers to the phenomenon whereby people who are insured against the consequences of risky activity will
engage in more of it.
In the relative calm of the aftermath of debt crises in Argentina and Brazil, the Fund has been concentrating
its energies on limiting future demand for its politically charged lending facilities by trying to transform itself
into a sort of international bankruptcy court. This initiative, known as the Sovereign Debt Reduction
Mechanism (SDRM), seeks not to prevent governments from defaulting, but to limit the economic damage of
default by giving creditors more incentive to reach a speedy compromise on debt restructuring and injecting a
mechanism to impose one where such compromise is not forthcoming. The attraction to the Fund itself is
obvious, as success in such an initiative would keep its lending practices out of the cross hairs of the
"anti-globalization" left and the "anti-moral-hazard" right when the next crisis hits. Unfortunately for the Fund,
the proposal has been subjected to powerful criticism from both private lenders and sovereign borrowers, the
former believing it will lead to more defaults and worse terms for creditors, and the latter that it will lead to
higher borrowing costs.
Whether this scheme or some similar one is eventually implemented, more or less widely, the Fund's
existential crisis will not end. The attractions of international capital will only increase as the financial services
industry continues to expand, increase efficiency, and reduce global capital costs. Developed country supply
of investable dollars and developing country demand for them will inevitably increase, to the clear net benefit
of both.
Nevertheless, financial crises will continue to emerge so long as governments continue to inject autarkic
national currencies into internationally integrated national economies. In such an environment, there is every
prospect that demands on IMF lending facilities will not only overstrain its lending capacity, but fatally
undermine its political legitimacy on both the left and right. Salvation lies in the Salvadoran solution,
dollarization; the continued march of euro-ization; and perhaps the spread of a single Asian currency built on
the back of political reconciliation between Japan and China. The only question is whether the IMF can
muster the political courage to pronounce the last century's experiment in developing country central banks
the failure it has so clearly been.
---
Mr. Steil is the Andre Meyer Senior Fellow in international economics at the Council on Foreign Relations.

Influence Wanes For Central Banks In the Euro Zone
International
Influence Wanes For Central Banks In the Euro Zone
By G. Thomas Sims
558 words
22 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A17
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
FRANKFURT -- Germany's appointment of a new president of its once-mighty Bundesbank -- a move
practically ignored by financial markets -- underscores a sharp decline in stature of the central banks of the
12 European nations using the euro.
Economics professor Axel Weber, named to succeed Ernst Welteke after Mr. Welteke resigned amid
ethics-violation allegations, will be only one of 18 members in the European Central Bank's often-unwieldy
governing council that decides on interest rates for the euro zone.
The Bundesbank's power to dictate monetary and economic policy for Europe's largest economy changed
with the creation of the euro five years ago. But its role as a mere branch of the ECB still was striking this
week, as the prices of assets in financial markets hardly budged.
"Not just the Bundesbank, but all of Europe's central banks are being newly defined," German Finance
Minister Hans Eichel told reporters in Berlin as he introduced Mr. Weber.
Mr. Weber said Germany's central bank is focusing on "core responsibilities" outside monetary policy. For
example, he said, he wants the bank to more actively promote integration of financial markets by producing
research and fostering discussion.
The 12 central bankers on the ECB's governing council -- who serve along with a six-member executive
board permanently based in Frankfurt -- will lose even more of their say when the 10 new countries joining
the European Union May 1 eventually begin to adopt the euro. Voting by national central bankers will start to
rotate at the ECB, a system much like that of the U.S. Federal Reserve's regional heads.
Meanwhile, many of the national central banks -- including those in Italy and France -- are now facing other
challenges, including ethics scandals, political pressure, falling profits and restructuring.
At the Italian central bank, for example, Governor Antonio Fazio has been wielding his power as the nation's
top banking regulator by blocking foreign banks from entering the Italian market. Now, Mr. Fazio is defending
himself from accusations that he failed to see the early warning signs of the collapse of dairy titan Parmalat
SpA, which was indebted to several banks.
Like Mr. Welteke, Mr. Fazio is also under fire for taking gifts from companies he regulates. Cesare Geronzi,
chairman of Capitalia SpA Bank, has said he took Mr. Fazio free of charge in a jet that Mr. Geronzi had
rented for his guests. A representative for the Bank of Italy wouldn't comment on any of the accusations.
The French government, meanwhile, is putting the Bank of France under pressure of a different sort. France
has one of the EU's largest budget deficits, estimated at 3.7% of gross domestic product for this year, and
Finance Minister Nicolas Sarkozy is looking for ways to plug the gap. Soon after taking the post this month,
Mr. Sarkozy -- echoing similar suggestions from other euro-zone finance ministers -- suggested selling some
of the Bank of France's 3,000 metric tons of gold, investing the cash and using the returns to reduce debt.
Christian Noyer, the central bank's governor, initially ruled out such a plan for the world's fourth-largest stash
of gold. But now he appears to be considering the issue, under certain conditions.
