Mind the Output Gap?
Mind the Output Gap?
By Lawrence Kudlow
1,124 words
21 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
All eyes will turn to Alan Greenspan as he testifies today on the economy, interest rates and inflation before
Congress's Joint Economic Committee. Financial markets are looking for hints of a possible change in the
Fed's rate-setting policies that might begin to normalize the level of the federal-funds rate in relation to the
strong economy.
Many investors would like to see a bit less money created by the central bank as a nod to strong gold and
commodity price trends, as well as rising bond yields, traditional harbingers of excess money and rising
inflation. I believe that a few small restraining steps now will save the Fed, the stock market and the
economy a lot of high interest rate angst down the road.
Up to now the Fed has ignored forward-looking commodity and financial price signals in favor of an "output
gap" model of inflation that argues against broad price increases so long as underutilized resources and idle
capacity exist in the economy. This output gap approach is reminiscent of the "cost push" arguments of the
inflationary 1970s, whereby wages, not money, were fingered as the principal inflation culprit. The Fed's
output-gap argument also looks a lot like the old Phillips Curve trade-off between falling unemployment and
rising inflation dressed up in new scientific garb. But that too failed dismally as a policy guide during the '70s.
Why monetary decision-makers fight the overwhelming historical evidence that inflation is always a
monetary problem is hard to fathom, yet they do. No one has waged this fight more energetically than Fed
Gov. Ben Bernanke, who in speech after speech argues that rising bond rates and a pronounced dollar
decline relative to gold, commodities and foreign currencies doesn't really matter. Ironically, the output gap
he keeps trying to sell to a skeptical financial market audience is really a fiscal indicator, not a monetary one.
The term "output gap" simply means that the actual level of today's inflation-adjusted GDP is below a
theoretical level of GDP that would have been achieved if the economy were growing at its fullest potential. In
this, the former Princeton professor is surely right about the gap. Since the onset of the 2001 recession, the
economy has fallen behind its long-run potential by $4.4 trillion.
This calculation assumes trend growth in GDP of 3.5% per year, which is the historical record of the U.S.
economy since 1947. For last year's fourth quarter alone, potential GDP was about $11 trillion, while actual
GDP came in at $10.6 trillion. In this case, the difference -- or output gap -- is roughly $400 billion.
Actual economic growth trends above or below a hypothetical level of full-employment GDP are the single
biggest factor in budget swings between surplus and deficit. As economic resources have been
underemployed for some time, the budgetary consequences have been severe.
Chairman Greenspan has suggested that full employment would today equate to a 4% unemployment rate.
The current rate is 5.7%. That "unemployment gap" of 1.7 percentage points represents underutilized labor
resources. It's a shorthand version of the overall GDP output-gap problem, which also includes
underemployed business assets.
So, by producing and employing below the long-run potential of the economy, a sizable shortfall of income
and wealth has occurred. Consequently, tax receipts from wages and salaries, corporate profits, and capital
gains have also fallen well short. This, above all else, has worsened the budget picture.
Assuming an average tax rate of 20% -- the rule-of-thumb used by most green eyeshades in government --
personal- and business-tax revenues in the last quarter fell about $80 billion short of potential. That's $320
billion at an annual rate and about two-thirds of the $480 billion budget deficit estimated by the Congressional
Budget Office.
It's clear then that the underemployment of the economy and the wide output gap between actual and
potential GDP are the major deficit-causing factors in today's fiscal picture. However, economic growth has

--- Page 155 ---
Page 155 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
quickened significantly since President Bush's across-the-board tax cut was implemented retroactively last
year.
And herein lies the solution.
If these new tax incentives are permanently left in place, as the president proposes, then the gap between
actual and potential economic growth will narrow rapidly. As a result, so will the budget deficit. Installing tax
incentives to generate a more rapid return to full employment throughout the economy is the most vital
deficit-reducing action the government can take.
Importantly, a full-employment economy, with 3% long-term growth and 4% unemployment in the years
ahead, will produce a new cycle of budget surpluses beginning in 2011. As the economic baseline used by
the CBO falls well below full employment -- the CBO figures only 2.8% yearly growth and 5% unemployment
-- today's long-range budget outlook appears much worse than is actually the case.
Mr. Bernanke has helped make this picture clear. Where he errs is in linking the underemployed economy
and the continued absence of inflation. Between 1965 and 1981, policy makers learned painfully that virulent
inflation is quite capable during periods of rising unemployment and slowing economic growth. That's
because inflation is a monetary problem. At any given level of output, or output gap, the Fed can still supply
more money than the economy requires. It is this excess money that drives up prices and drives down
consumer purchasing power.
---
Economic history and analysis show that forward-looking inflation-sensitive indicators such as bond rates,
gold and commodity prices, and the exchange value of the dollar best reflect the inflationary risk of excess
money. These indicators have recently been warning of higher inflation despite the fact that the economy is
still underemployed.
Over the past three months the basic inflation rate (excluding food and energy) has moved up near 3% at an
annual rate, about 1 percentage point above the Fed's 2% target. Overall inflation in the first quarter
increased 5.1%. Producer price inflation is also moving up.
In all likelihood, U.S. economic efficiency would be maximized with a consumer price trend of roughly 2% per
year. Not too hot, not too cold. Permitting inflation to rise beyond that, however, would threaten the national
goals of price stability, full employment, and fiscal balance.
Mr. Bernanke's output gap is surely a budgetary problem. But it's not an inflation solution. To maintain price
stability the central bank must normalize its target rate and remove excess money from circulation. The
sooner they act, the less it will take.
---
Mr. Kudlow is co-host of CNBC's "Kudlow &Cramer."

NEW YORK -- Small-capitalization stocks dropped with the broad market as concerns about higher interest
rates ignited late-session selling.
Gold-related stocks led industry decliners as gold prices sagged ahead of Federal Reserve Chairman Alan
Greenspan's testimony to the Senate.
Among decliners in the gold group, Bema Gold lost 36 cents, or 11%, to $2.81, Canyon Resources dropped

Bookshelf: Tempest in a Tax Code
Bookshelf:
LEISURE &ARTS
Bookshelf: Tempest in a Tax Code
By Todd G. Buchholz
1,075 words
21 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D10
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE COMING GENERATIONAL STORM
By Laurence J. Kotlikoff and Scott Burns
(MIT, 274 pages, $27.95)
A few pages into "The Coming Generational Storm" and you'll want to throw your favorite chair and your
flat-panel TV into your SUV and, like "The Beverly Hillbillies" in reverse, drive back to the Ozarks beyond the
pale of civilization. And beyond the absurd promises of Social Security and Medicare. Unlike Jed Clampett,
you won't want to hitch granny to the roof, because she'll just bankrupt you with the cost of her
blood-pressure medicine and arthritis pills.
"The Coming Generational Storm" makes "The Perfect Storm," Sebastian Junger's best-selling 1998 tale of
coastal gales, sound like a rain delay at Fenway Park. Economist Laurence J. Kotlikoff has teamed up with
financial journalist Scott Burns to expose our cherished entitlement programs as chicanery, foolishness and
cowardice.
For more than a decade, Mr. Kotlikoff has been crunching numbers and leading the charge to create a
"generational accounting" model that asks the question: "How much are we ripping off our children and
grandchildren by promising retirement and medical benefits to ourselves?" The answer would make even
Dennis Kozlowski blush behind his $6,000 shower curtain. The authors estimate that tax rates will have to
rise to nearly 70% to pay the bills now accruing.
How did we get into this mess? We've spent 70 years building a pyramid scheme. When Franklin Roosevelt
set up the Social Security system, the median age of a retired worker was, well, dead. It was easy to promise
benefits when most people died in their 50s. A pyramid scheme works as long as you've got an ever bigger
supply of drones to lug the bricks and blocks. Pyramids worked fine for the pharoahs until Charlton Heston
led the Hebrew slaves through that river.
But now the U.S. pyramid looks like it could turn upside-down. "In 2000 there were 82 million people under
the age of 20 in the United States. Their numbers dwarfed the 35.5 million seniors." By 2080, there will be
more seniors than young people. That means more walkers than strollers. We already have more golf
courses than McDonald's. And that was before Phil Mickelson inspired perennial losers to head back to the
links.
There is plenty of blame to go around, and Messrs. Kotlikoff and Burns delight in pointing their fingers at the
presidents, bureaucrats and congressmen who won't listen. They especially mock President Clinton's fiscal
"rectitude." After mentioning Monica Lewinsky's name, they state that the "president no doubt had just too
many things in his lap to come up with long-term solutions for Social Security, Medicare, or Medicaid." This
failure dwarfed whatever progress Mr. Clinton made on the debt, which fell to "only 35 percent of GDP by the
time his moving van pulled up to the White House and started pilfering the place."
Nor do Republicans get off easy, as the authors invoke the dreaded "voodoo" word to describe the policies of
Presidents Reagan and George W. Bush. All I know is that Mr. Reagan's voodoo was preferable to the
doo-doo that Jimmy Carter left us with. (Remember stagflation?) This book's bipartisan slaps -- bred of
frustration with futile congressional testimony -- can be summarized as a "Paul O'Neill on both your houses."
Perhaps the authors go a bit too far. Because the Treasury Department has issued 75-year projections for
entitlement spending, but not 100-year forecasts, Messrs. Kotlikoff and Burns insist that they are hiding the
ugly truth. Gosh, 75 years sounds pretty far away to me. What is the confidence interval on a 100-year
econometric forecast? Might as well consult Nancy Reagan's astrologer.

--- Page 158 ---
Page 158 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Having painted a fiscal picture as awful as "Guernica," the authors unveil two bold plans. Their Social
Security proposal pulls out the plug for young people, liberating them to invest in a government-run index
fund of stocks, bonds and real estate. They cancel the payroll tax and substitute a new federal sales tax that
would pay off accrued benefits. Their Medicare plan revokes the "fee for service" system that gives patients
no incentive to curb their spending. Instead, older people would receive customized vouchers based on their
current health. A "perfectly healthy 67-year-old might get a voucher for $6,000 whereas an 85-year-old with
pancreatic cancer might get a voucher for $100,000." Their ideas are thoughtful and, whatever doubts one
might have, preferable to the status quo, which sticks the country's entire financial future in the ICU.
The authors give some personal financial advice toward the end of the book, including buying your home and
dabbling in gold and commodity funds. But they don't suggest moving to Europe or Japan. Those national
pyramids are flipping over sooner than ours. Japan is aging faster than sushi. And Helmut Kohl caught hell for
just proposing that German workers cut back their annual spa visits, in which the government pays them to
loll about in bubbly waters.
Which is the only country to tackle the problem? Britain, thanks to the Iron Lady, who partially privatized that
country's social security. But if most other countries are dumb and indebted, why do Messrs. Kotlikoff and
Burns tell readers to invest money in international bond funds? They themselves predict that interest rates
will be rising sharply, which hurts bonds, and other issuers of debt appear even more reckless than the U.S.
Of course, politicians will be politicians. The lesson is to give them less power over our money. This engaging
book recounts the harrowing story of Rep. Dan Rostenkowski, who pushed through a health-care plan for the
elderly and then asked them to help pay for it. At one event, he was "booed and chased down a Chicago
street . . . by a group of senior citizens." Eventually he "cut through a gas station, broke into a sprint and
escaped in a car, which minutes earlier had one of its protesters, Leona Kozien, draped over the hood."
Well, if granny is going to jump on your car anyway, you might as well, like Jed Clampett, take her with you to
escape the Coming Generational Storm.
---
Mr. Buchholz's forthcoming book, "Bringing the Jobs Home," will be published by Penguin this fall.

Dollar Shows Broad Strength On Expectation of a Rate Rise
Currency Trading
Dollar Shows Broad Strength On Expectation of a Rate Rise
By Jamie McGeever Dow Jones Newswires
513 words
21 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar strengthened across the board on mounting expectations the Federal Reserve will
raise interest rates soon, hitting fresh five-month highs against the euro and Swiss franc.
Dallas Fed President Robert McTeer got the ball rolling in U.S. trading, saying that interest rates could rise
and still be accommodative. Referring to the current 1% fed-funds rate (which governs overnight interbank
loans), Mr. McTeer told CNNfn that the level "could be a bit higher, and policy would still be very
accommodative."
Then Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan, in Congressional testimony on banking issues, weighed in later in the
New York session, saying in response to a question that deflation is "no longer an issue," and that pricing
power is picking up.
Mr. Greenspan's comments gave the dollar a second wind after it had appeared to be settling up on the day
but off its highs. His apparently hawkish comments also roiled other financial markets: Treasurys and equities
plunged, gold fell back and bond yields soared.
"Given the [economic] evidence [Fed officials] have seen, they have to prepare the market for a tightening,"
said Ram Bhagavatula, chief economist for North America at Royal Bank of Scotland in New York. "The
whole yield curve is up" around 0.50 percentage point this month.
A recent string of surprisingly strong jobs, retail sales and inflation data is raising expectations the Fed will
tighten monetary policy soon. This is seen as dollar-positive because it increases the appeal of
dollar-denominated assets to investors.
"I definitely think the dollar is remaining well supported as interest-rate expectations are adjusting in the U.S.,"
said Daniel Katzive, foreign-exchange strategist at UBS in Stamford, Conn.
At one point, the euro was at a new five-month low of $1.1853. Late in the afternoon, it was at $1.1860, well
down from $1.2019 late Monday in New York. The dollar, having already broken key technical resistance
against the Swiss franc at 1.3080 francs, was at one point in New York trading at 1.3120 francs, also a
five-month high. Late in the day, the dollar was even a bit stronger, at 1.3129 francs, up from 1.2903 francs
late Monday.
The dollar also spiked against the yen to 108.66 yen from 108.46 yen late Monday, while sterling slumped to
$1.7877 from $1.8075.
Mr. Greenspan's comments sent the Dow Jones Industrial Average spinning lower, with the benchmark index
closing the session at its lows, off 123 points or 1.2%. The yield on the 10-year Treasury note surged to a
five-month high of 4.47% and spot gold fell around $5 on the day to $395 a troy ounce.
But Lara Rhame, senior economist at Brown Brothers Harriman &Co. in New York, said she was surprised by
the market's sharp reaction to Mr. Greenspan's comments, which she described as "benign."

Tom Barkley contributed to this article.

Greenspan's Hint on Rate Jolts Markets
Tuesday's Markets
Greenspan's Hint on Rate Jolts Markets
By E.S. Browning and Aaron Lucchetti
932 words
21 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A STRONG HINT from Alan Greenspan jolted both the stock and bond markets.
Little changed ahead of the Federal Reserve chairman's comments before the Senate Banking Committee in
the afternoon, but stocks and bonds both fell sharply following his indication that inflation worries now have
overshadowed deflation concerns. Investors concluded that, to fight inflation, the Fed will be raising its
short-term interest-rate target sooner rather than later.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished down 123.35 points, or 1.18%, at 10314.50, and is now down
more than 1% on the year. That decline is slightly less severe than the skid last Tuesday, when strong
retail-sales and earnings figures also sparked fears of inflation and higher rates.
But the broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, whose many financial stocks would be especially hurt by an
interest-rate increase, suffered its sharpest reversal in point or percentage terms since September. It fell
1.56%, or 17.67 points, to 1118.15. It now is up less than 1% since the year began.
Bonds, whose performance depends almost entirely on the interest-rate outlook, also fell. The yield of the
10-year Treasury note, which rises when the price falls, moved up to 4.47%, its highest level in late New York
trading since September. The dollar, which would benefit from higher rates, advanced to $1.186 against the
euro, its strongest New York finish since November.
"Mr. Greenspan left the impression that we have a stronger economy and that interest rates are probably
going to start reflecting that," said Robert Harrington, co-head of U.S. stock trading at brokerage firm UBS. "It
was pretty clear that things have changed."
Technology stocks, which have been among the most volatile sectors for years, suffered the sharpest
declines. The Nasdaq Composite Index, dominated by large technology shares, fell 2.07%, or 41.80 points, to
1978.63. After being up for most of this year, the index now is down 1.2% since the year began. Investors
fear that higher inflation could boost commodity costs for tech companies and that efforts to slow the
economy could hurt demand for new tech gear.
Extraordinarily low rates over the past 18 months encouraged investors to take on riskier investments, from
unproven biotechnology shares to speculative junk bonds. Those investing instruments have slumped in the
past several sessions as the rate outlook has shifted.
Companies still are deep in earnings season, reporting their quarterly results. Those results generally have
been positive, which has helped prevent stocks from falling even more. But the market's attention clearly has
shifted now toward the interest-rate outlook.
As is usually the case in these settings, Mr. Greenspan didn't make any explicit comment about his plans for
rates. But his body language seemed clearly to have changed, indicating that he is worried more about
fighting inflation by raising rates than fighting deflation by keeping them low.
Responding to a question from Sen. Richard Shelby of Alabama, Mr. Greenspan said that until recently, it
wasn't clear whether companies had the ability to raise prices, but that now, the data suggest that they are
succeeding in doing just that.
"It's fairly apparent that pricing power is gradually being restored." Mr. Greenspan said. "Threats of deflation,
which were a significant concern last year, by all indications are no longer an issue before us. But clearly it is
a change that has occurred in the last number of weeks."
And that, said bond analysts, means that the Fed will likely this summer raise its target for the federal-funds
rate, the rate at which banks lend one another money overnight.

--- Page 161 ---
Page 161 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The bond market has been priced on an unsustainably low fed-funds rate," said Keith Anderson, chief
fixed-income investment officer at BlackRock Inc., a New York money manager. Mr. Anderson said Mr.
Greenspan's comments "give me greater confidence" that the Fed will signal at its May meeting that it intends
shortly to raise its target rate from the current 46-year low of 1%. Then, he said, it is likely to raise the rate to
1.25% in June.
"The market is saying that the Fed will tighten sooner rather than later," said Gerald Lucas, chief Treasury
strategist at Banc of America Securities. "We're going to find out more" today, when Mr. Greenspan is
scheduled to speak at length about the economy before Congress's joint Economic Committee, "but it
appears he gave us a preview."
Mr. Lucas said he is sticking with his prediction that the Fed will wait to raise its target until its August
meeting.
Trading in fed-funds futures contracts on the Chicago Board of Trade indicated that traders were pricing in a
48% chance of a quarter-point increase at the Fed's meeting June 29 and June 30, compared with a 32%
chance before Mr. Greenspan spoke.
Oil and gold prices both fell on the news of a likely interest-rate increase. Gold dropped $2.90 to $397.70, its
lowest finish since March 12.
The 10-year Treasury note fell 24/32, or $7.50 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which moves inversely to
price, rose to 4.47%. The 30-year bond was down 30/32 to yield 5.267%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 108.66 yen, up from 108.46, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.1860 from $1.2019.

ASIA/PACIFIC
North Korean Leader May Ask Beijing for Aid
North Korean leader Kim Jong Il is paying a rare visit to his most important foreign benefactor, China,
presumably to discuss assistance to his struggling economy and glacial negotiations with the U.S. over
Pyongyang's nuclear programs.
Mr. Kim, who is habitually secretive and is widely believed to dislike flying, traveled by a specially outfitted
train. Chinese officials refused to confirm Mr. Kim's arrival in Beijing yesterday, though South Korean media
and Reuters cited unnamed officials as saying he was in the Chinese capital.
The visit is Mr. Kim's first in more than three years. Chinese specialists on North Korea and foreign diplomats
in Beijing said it was chiefly routine, providing the North Korean leader a chance to get acquainted with
President Hu Jintao and other younger leaders elevated to power 18 months ago. But its timing was raising
speculation, coming less than a week after Vice President Dick Cheney traveled to Beijing to impress upon
Chinese leaders the need for progress in the six-nation talks over North Korea's nuclear programs.
Mr. Cheney and U.S. officials have said that Chinese leaders agree that urgency is needed in getting North
Korea to shelve the programs, which the U.S. says Pyongyang is using to make nuclear weapons. But
Chinese officials also have expressed exasperation with both Pyongyang and Washington, contending that
their inflexibility is causing the Beijing-sponsored negotiations to founder.
Mr. Cheney last week didn't offer new initiatives to move the talks forward, the diplomats said. And with the
U.S.'s troubles in Iraq making military action against Pyongyang less likely, China sees less need for quick
action, the diplomats said. Thus, the negotiations, while on Mr. Kim's agenda in Beijing, aren't likely to be
dealt with in any detail, specialists and diplomats in Beijing and Seoul said.
Pyongyang also needs Chinese support in buttressing the North Korean economy that has rebounded from
the famine years of the late 1990s, analysts and diplomats said.
-- Charles Hutzler, Gordon Fairclough
China's Top Banker Spurs Talk on Yuan Policy
SHANGHAI -- A weekend pledge by China's top central banker to create a market-driven trading system for
the yuan heightened talk that Beijing was on the verge of loosening its currency peg and strengthening the
yuan.
A spokesman at China's central bank yesterday said no change was forthcoming in yuan policy and most
local market participants in the tightly managed spot market shrugged off the talk. Still, the country's
leadership repeatedly has stressed that full convertibility of the yuan on the capital account remains a
long-term goal and that it gradually would adopt measures toward that end. The comments by the
central-bank governor, Zhou Xiaochuan, added to the volume of official statements on the currency.
"The demand for making the [yuan] fully convertible is becoming higher and higher as the [yuan] becomes an
important currency in Asia and even globally," Mr. Zhou said, according to the Financial Times.
The yuan's slight gain against the U.S. dollar yesterday highlighted nervousness ahead of meetings this week
among the Group of Seven leading industrial nations, the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund.
Chinese vice-ministerial officials are scheduled to meet G-7 counterparts Thursday, ahead of the main G-7
meeting of finance ministers and central bankers in Washington.

BEIJING -- China's retail sales swelled during the first quarter, helped by a surge in spending over the
weeklong Lunar New Year holiday in January, while higher food costs drove a rise in the consumer-price
index.
For the first three months of 2004, China's CPI rose 2.8% from the year-earlier period, according to the
National Bureau of Statistics. Retail sales expanded 10.7% to 1.283 trillion yuan ($155.01 billion) during the
first quarter, the statistics bureau said.

government efforts to boost consumer spending, particularly in rural areas, rather than spending on
large-ticket infrastructure projects. Recently, several government officials have cautioned that inflationary
pressures in the country are growing.

Squatters Boost Calls for Land Reform in Brazil
SAO PAULO, Brazil -- Squatters staged mass property invasions in Brazil's largest city yesterday as
President Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva defended his government's effort to redistribute land to the poor.
Riot police used tear gas to eject hundreds of squatters who broke into a vacant building in Sao Paulo's
center. But hundreds more successfully invaded a vacant plot on the city's outskirts and started setting up
shacks.
The invasions came in the wake of a wave of rural property takeovers by landless Brazilians across the
country upset with the pace of land reform. Mr. da Silva, speaking out about the land invasions for the first
time since they escalated last month, said that his government is trying to grant 130,000 titles to people
squatting on unused land across the country.
He said Brazil's landless are free to protest but suggested the rise of property invasions -- criticized by
business groups, ranchers and the country's centrist politicians -- could hurt the government's land-reform
efforts.
Squatters' movements supported Mr. da Silva ahead of his landslide 2002 electoral victory. But 15 months
after he took office, squatters' leaders say land-reform efforts by his administration have fallen short. About
90% of Brazil's land is owned by 20% of the country's 178 million people. The poorest 40% of the population
hold 1%.
-- Associated Press

Gold Standard: Incredible Demand For Commodities Just Might Persist
Commodities Report
Gold Standard: Incredible Demand For Commodities Just Might Persist
By Peter A. McKay and Sara Calian
1,204 words
19 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE TWO-YEAR bull market in commodities still could have some room to run, despite last week's drop in
the price of everything from gold to hogs.
That is because expanding economies, especially in the U.S. and China, are fueling demand for raw
materials for industrial use. Meanwhile, the supplies of many commodities are tight, due to lack of investment
in mines and other facilities in recent years, low stockpiles at the supplier level and transportation bottlenecks.
A continued rise in prices could help convince the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates sooner rather than
later, to keep America's economy from overheating. Still, some commodity analysts say U.S. interest rates
are so low that they have a long way to go before they can damp demand enough to hurt commodity prices.
"You've got a lot of room to go" for commodity prices, says Jeff Christian, managing director of commodities
consulting and research firm CPM Group. "There are bigger longer-term issues, economically, beyond
cyclical reflation or a movement in the dollar."
Last week's selloff was triggered partly by the dollar's rally. Commodities are priced in dollars and, when the
currency strengthens, people tend to sell. Analysts say the drop also was driven by speculators who sold at a
profit, fearing that higher interest rates would dent demand. The selling, analysts add, could continue in the
short term as investors who trade commodities purely as financial bets -- rather than to obtain raw materials
to produce goods -- run for the exits.
But CPM Group's Mr. Christian predicts that old-fashioned supply and demand will drive commodity prices.
While he says the prices of some commodities such as gold will begin easing around June, he adds that
silver and other commodities should finish the year higher. Silver is used in photography products and
electronics, such as computer chips.
"We're going from a shotgun approach, where everything was up, to a rifle approach, where you have to pick
one thing at a time," Mr. Christian says.
Last week, investors got a taste of that dynamic, when many precious metals and natural gas declined. Gold
futures, which are generally bid up when the dollar is weakening, fell $19.10, or 4.5%, for the week to
$401.60 a troy ounce -- 6.3% below the 15-year high of $428.80 hit on April 1. The Dow Jones-AIG Index of
20 commodities fell 1.8% last week, while the Reuters-CRB Index, which comprises 17 items, lost more than
2%.
There were some pockets of strength. Crude oil rose 60 cents, or 1.6%, for the week to $37.74 a barrel on
the New York Mercantile Exchange amid terrorism jitters. Copper rose about 3% to $1.3425 a pound on the
Comex, boosted by U.S. data showing a pickup in construction of new homes. The data also helped lumber
rise more than 3% to $412.90 per 1,000 board feet at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange.
For more than two years, such gains have been the rule in the commodities market, but they have triggered
few inflation worries because they have been fueled largely by a decline in the dollar. From the beginning of
2002 until the beginning of this year, the U.S. currency lost about 41% against the euro and retreated 19%
against the yen. In those currencies, commodity prices rose less dramatically. For example, crude oil rose
64% in dollars during that period, but only 16% in euros.
The traditional inverse relationship between the dollar and commodities began to evaporate earlier this year,
when the dollar started strengthening and commodity prices kept rising. So far this year, the J.P. Morgan
dollar index is up more than 1.6%, including a 4.7% rise by the dollar against the euro and a 0.4% rise
against the yen.

--- Page 168 ---
Page 168 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
That hasn't kept pace with the major commodity indexes, which are up more than 8% this year. Although
analysts expect the dollar to rise a little more, few people believe that it will make up all it has lost in the past
couple of years.
"The global recovery looks for real," says James Glassman, senior economist at J.P. Morgan Chase. But, he
adds, "Maybe what's happening is we're reaching the point where it's harder for commodities to keep going
up" if rising interest rates start to restrict demand.
Some fund managers see copper, nickel, aluminum and related metal and mining shares rising further this
year. "We believe metals have detached from the dollar like the last big bull market in metals in 1986 and
1987," says Paul Galloway, mining analyst at UBS in London.
From now until September, UBS estimates commodity prices will rise an average of 15%, with copper
increasing 18%, zinc up 28% and aluminum climbing 22%.
"Unless the dollar strengthens dramatically -- 15% or more -- then I do not believe it will have any material
impact on the cycle of supply and demand," says Lewis Johnson, commodities analyst at fund manager T.
Rowe Price in Baltimore.
He says his firm is buying shares of metals companies on price dips. He continues to see the best
opportunities in steel companies such as Nucor Corp. and International Steel Group Inc. He adds he has sold
some shares in smaller metals companies at a profit and bought larger diversified-metals companies such as
Australia's BHP Billiton Ltd. and Brazil's iron-ore miner Cia. Vale do Rio Doce SA.
Others, such as U.S. Global Investors portfolio manager Aaron Dunn, who handles his firm's $241 million
Global Resources Fund, are playing the dollar's moves. The Global Resources Fund was up 99.6% in the
fourth quarter of 2003 because 80% of its holdings were in metals and basic-materials stocks. Early this year,
the fund started reducing its position in metals and basic materials and by the end of the first quarter it had
50% of its holdings in energy and 50% in metals and basic materials. "We went into energy to protect
ourselves from a bounce in the dollar," he says, noting he will take advantage of short-term currency moves.
But "what is driving commodities is the lack of supply and the strong demand," Mr. Dunn says. "We are very
bullish long term on commodities even though in the short term we see fluctuations based on the currency
issue."
---
Friday's Commodity Trading
COPPER: Futures soared on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange as speculative funds
stepped in to buy amid fresh signs of U.S. economic strength. May copper soared as high as $1.3480, its
strongest level since April 5, before ending up 5.40 cents at $1.3425.
LEAN HOGS: Futures at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange closed lower, with nearby contracts down their
daily permissible trading limits, on technically inspired liquidation of long positions, including heavy selling by
speculative commodity funds. Lean hogs for June delivery fell two cents to 70.8 cents a pound.

Is This the End of Easy Money? --- As Interest Rates Move Higher, Big Bond Investors Are Heading For the Exits, Roiling the Market
Heard on the Street
Is This the End of Easy Money? --- As Interest Rates Move Higher, Big Bond Investors Are Heading
For the Exits, Roiling the Market
By Gregory Zuckerman, Ian McDonald, Peter A. McKay, Craig Karmin and Aaron Lucchetti
1,520 words
19 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE CARRY TRADE. Curve flatteners. Call plays. Debt floats.
To those unfamiliar with the often-dense world of bonds, these things mean little. To players in the $22 trillion
U.S. bond market they all are high-octane ways to profit from low interest rates.
In recent days, these terms all have meant the same thing: big-time losses. Falling interest rates have done
more than help consumers looking for a mortgage, and companies with heavy debt. They also have been a
green light for large bond investors to try all kinds of complex trades to rack up profits.
For most of the past few years the trades have worked. Now that key interest rates are moving higher, as
the bond market girds for the possibility of higher inflation and a possible move by the Federal Reserve to
boost short-term target rates, investors are heading for the exits, roiling the bond market and weighing on
stocks.
"A lot of people doing these trades are taking profits by dumping bonds," says Louis Brien, a strategist at
DRW Trading, a bond-trading firm in Chicago. "It's not calamitous yet, but it can get a lot worse, if rates keep
going higher and everyone heads for the doors at the same time."
The most popular of the trades has been the leveraged carry trade, a simple maneuver with a mouthful of a
name. Here is how it works: Borrow money at slim short-term rates, usually close to or below Libor, the
London interbank offered rate. Borrowing money for three months based on Libor is a skimpy 1.15% right
now. Then just take that money and buy bonds with higher yields, such as mortgage bonds, long-term
government bonds or foreign bonds.
The difference between, say, a mortgage bond with a 5% yield and the 1% or so borrowing cost can bring big
profits -- especially for investors who use heavy leverage to increase the size of the positions. Some hedge
funds and other traders have been using more than $25 of borrowed money for each $1 of their own to make
this trade, according to traders.
Among the biggest fans of this trade in the past year or so have been banks and securities firms making bets
with their own company's cash. Banks have been paying superlow rates to savers and taking that money and
buying a lot of mortgage bonds. Other investors have been buying long-term bonds on the bet that the yield
curve, or the plot of different bond yields, will "flatten." That just means that long-term bonds will rally, sending
rates on those bonds lower in relation to rates on short-term bonds. The only downside for these wagers is if
the longer-term bonds being purchased tumble in price, as they have lately. The yield on 10-year Treasury
notes, which moves in the opposite direction of their price, has climbed to 4.35% from 3.7% just in the past
few weeks. "If you got in the trade in the last month or two, you got burned. If you've been in the trade for a
while, you've still got a positive return," says Larry Dyer, senior interest-rate strategist at Credit Suisse First
Boston.
Now, some carry-trade investors are taking profits off the table from this trade and selling their holdings,
causing recent problems for the bond market. But another group is digging in its heels, according to Gerald
Lucas, a strategist at Bank of America, upping their bets as bond prices fall and yields go higher, making the
carry trade even more tempting. Their buying has helped the bond market, which rose on Friday for the first
time in 10 days.
Another hot trade has been buying callable, or redeemable, bonds with high yields. The idea here is that low
rates will encourage companies that sell such bonds, such as Fannie Mae, to redeem them so they can sell
even lower-rate bonds. So investors get a generous rate on what they expect to be a short-term bond. But
now that rates are higher, few expect these bonds to be redeemed as quickly as they were being redeemed a

--- Page 170 ---
Page 170 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
few months ago. As a result, prices of these bonds are falling because investors now view them as long-term
holdings with yields that look thin compared with others in the market.
Others losing are consumers and companies that took out adjustable or floating debt, which usually has a
lower initial rate than fixed-rate debt. Now that rates are moving higher these investors are going to find their
interest payments rise. Even gold is being jolted by moves in the bond market. When rates are low, and the
value of the dollar falls, gold prices usually climb, as some investors borrow and sell dollars to buy gold. Now
that borrowing rates have moved higher, these investors are dumping gold to end the trade.
Although gold usually rises on fears of inflation, gold fell $19.10, or 4.5%, to $401.60 a troy ounce in futures
trading last week. "Gold investors making this trade became sellers when rates turned higher this month,
which drove [gold] prices down," says George Gero, senior vice president at Legg Mason Wood Walker Inc.
For now, rates haven't climbed to troublesome levels. In fact, long-term bond yields were higher last year for
a bit. If rates keep climbing, some fear there will be more losses.
One area of concern is closed-end mutual funds. During the past three years, yield-starved investors lapped
up a gush of new closed-end offerings that invest in bonds or income-producing stocks to pay income to
shareholders. Nearly all of the closed-end funds launched during the bear market are allowed by their
prospectuses to use leverage to boost their yield. This strategy typically entails borrowing money at low
short-term rates or raising cash by issuing short-term preferred fund shares, then socking that cash into
higher-yielding securities such as longer-term bonds or real-estate investment trusts. As long as short-term
rates don't go up, this strategy provides ample extra income to pay out to fund shareholders, making these
funds attractive.
If the Fed raises rates, some of these funds will see steep losses, analysts say. "We wonder if the industry
was thinking about what's coming down the road in rolling this stuff out," says Don Cassidy, a senior fund
analyst at Lipper. "It's not going to be fun when rates go up."
To avoid problems of higher rates, some traders are scrambling to reconfigure their portfolios. Angelo
Manioudakis, a bond manager at OppenheimerFunds, is buying mortgage bonds with coupons as high as
7%. His thinking is that if a mortgage holder hasn't refinanced a 7% mortgage yet, they probably won't if rates
keep rising, reducing the chance that Mr. Manioudakis will lose a lot of money from holding the mortgage.
Others who have been borrowing in the U.S. to buy higher-yielding foreign bonds are quickly closing out their
trades, anticipating still higher U.S. borrowing rates and worried that foreign currencies could continue to
weaken against the dollar.
As bad as things are for bonds, however, there are good reasons to expect the pain to be limited. That is
because many larger investors and traders have been expecting a selloff for much of the past year. Many of
these people figured the rebounding economy would push the Federal Reserve to begin increasing interest
rates at some point. So when rates headed lower this year, despite the rebounding economy, many of these
people took steps to protect themselves, such as buying "put" contracts, investments that gain in value when
bond prices fall.
---
Seemed Like a Good Idea at the Time . . .
Savvy investors have been using low
interest rates to rack up gains via
complicated maneuvers. With rates climbing, some are scrambling for exits.
Some hot trades that are now going cold:
WHEN RATES ARE LOW
The Leveraged Carry: Borrow short-term on the cheap, plow the money into
higher-yielding, longer-term bonds, pocket the difference.
Callable Bonds: Buy callable bonds for a high yield but make short-term
bet that issuers will redeem quickly as rates fall.
Floating-Rate Debt: Take out adjustable-rate loans with lower initial
interest than fixed-term loans.
WHEN RATES RISE
The Leveraged Carry: Big losses as prices tumble; a narrowing gap between
long-term and short-term yields makes those losses bigger.

--- Page 171 ---
Page 171 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Callable Bonds: Issuers don't redeem, turning the short-term bet into a
long-term loser.
Floating-Rate Debt: Interest-rate payments on the fixed-rate loan spike.
(See related article: "Abreast of the Market: Stock Market Braces for Impact Of an Increase in Interest Rates,
But Some Insist Sky Won't Fall" -- WSJ April 19, 2004)

Defensive Buying Buoys Blue Chips; Nasdaq Retreats
Thursday's Markets
Defensive Buying Buoys Blue Chips; Nasdaq Retreats
By E.S. Browning
477 words
16 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS TURNED still more defensive, sending drug stocks sharply up and knocking computer-chip
stocks down.
Drug makers Merck and Pfizer -- the latter of which was added to the Dow Jones Industrial Average just this
month -- rose more than 3% each. Together, they more than accounted for the blue-chip average's gain of
19.51 points, or 0.19%, to 10397.46. The Dow is down 0.5% on the year.
The fear of higher interest rates helped knock Treasury bonds down for a sixth day.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many technology stocks also have suffered from rate worries, fell
1.12%, or 22.68 points, to 2002.17. On April 5, the index was up almost 4% for the year, but now it is back in
the red by 0.06%.
Investors moved toward drug and consumer-product stocks in a search for steady performers whose sales
would show consistent gains even if the Federal Reserve raised short-term rates this summer to cool the
economy and pre-empt inflation.
But nervousness was widespread enough that more stocks fell than rose on both the New York Stock
Exchange and Nasdaq.
"Everybody is focused on interest rates and on the next economic datapoint," said stock trader Michael
Driscoll at New York brokerage firm Bear Stearns.
Stocks rebounded late in the day, due in part to short-term trading ahead of today's expiration of monthly
options contracts.
Economic data reinforced the view that the economy is strong. Business activity in the Philadelphia region
and in New York state was stronger than expected, according to Fed reports. New jobless claims, however,
were somewhat higher than expected last week, a sign of economic softness.
The recently rebounding dollar pulled back slightly. Gold continued its slide, now down 5% in three days,
although oil prices rose.
The broad S&P 500 index inched up 0.06%, or 0.67 point, to 1128.84, up 1.5% on the year.
Chip stocks were hurt by a forecast of flat sales from Advanced Micro Devices. In after-hours news that could
affect trading today, International Business Machines failed to exceed analysts' earnings forecasts, and the
stock fell 2.8% in after-hours trading.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the NYSE, where 1.56 billion shares traded, 1,607 stocks rose and 1,689 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 7/32, or $2.1875 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.394%. The 30-year bond down 20/32 to yield 5.202%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 108.37 yen, down from 108.71, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.1977 from $1.1967.

Dollar Surges Against Rivals That Provide Higher Yields
Currency Trading
Dollar Surges Against Rivals That Provide Higher Yields
By Tom Barkley Dow Jones Newswires
666 words
15 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar surged to its strongest level in several months against higher-yielding currencies
after signs of a pickup in inflationary pressures reinforced expectations that U.S. interest rates would rise in
coming months.
The March consumer-price index, which showed the biggest increase in core inflation in almost 2 1/2 years,
was viewed in financial markets as the final piece of evidence needed to convince Federal Reserve policy
makers to lift U.S. official borrowing rates from a 46-year low of 1%.
The 0.5% rise in core inflation -- which excludes food and energy prices -- follows Tuesday's unexpected
1.8% jump in March retail sales and recent payroll data showing 308,000 new jobs created in the month.
The combined effect of the recent data has been a major shift in the outlook for U.S. interest rates, with
traders largely expecting a quarter-point rate increase at the Federal Open Market Committee's meeting in
August.
"We're getting a good re-evaluation of interest-rate expectations," said David Durrant, chief currency
strategist at Bank Julius Baer in New York.
He said the change in sentiment was "punishing" currencies from countries with higher interest rates than
the U.S., such as sterling and the Australian and New Zealand dollars. Investors continued to unwind the
popular "carry trades," in which they borrowed at low U.S. rates to buy assets denominated in higher-yielding
currencies.
The dollar also got a boost from data showing a narrowing of the U.S. trade gap in February to $42.09 billion
from a record high of $43.46 billion in January.
While the dollar's rally sputtered out against the euro after reaching a 4 1/2-month peak of $1.1872, the U.S.
currency held its gains against high-yielding currencies after surging over 3% versus the Australian and New
Zealand dollars.
Sterling, which is also backed by higher rates than in the U.S., slumped to a three-month low of $1.7852,
while the dollar's momentum helped push it up over 2% against the yen to just under 109 yen for the first time
in nearly a month. Yen weakness versus the euro, as well, helped the common currency recover against the
dollar.
Late yesterday, the euro was trading at $1.1967 from $1.1946 late Tuesday in New York. The dollar had also
given up its gains against the Swiss franc to trade at 1.2975 francs versus 1.2997 francs. Against the yen, the
dollar rose to 108.71 yen from 106.63 yen, while the euro climbed to 130.03 yen from 127.40 yen.
Among higher-yielding currencies, sterling fell to $1.7906 from $1.8192 late Tuesday in New York. The
Australian dollar ended at 73.48 U.S. cents, down from 75.48 U.S. cents late Tuesday. The New Zealand
dollar ended at 63.60 U.S. cents down from 65.39 U.S. cents late Tuesday.
Commodities also bore the brunt of the dollar's rise, with gold extending its heavy losses for a second day to
dip below $400 a troy ounce for the first time in a month. The Australian and New Zealand dollars often move
in tandem with commodity prices, in part because of the significant natural resources in those countries. As
both commodities and the commodity currencies were among the first to start gaining against the dollar
during the U.S. currency's downturn over the past few years, their retreat is being viewed as a possible
leading indicator of a broader turn in the dollar's favor.

--- Page 178 ---
Page 178 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
With the dollar breaking through or testing key technical levels against several of the currencies, Tim
Mazanec, senior currency strategist at Investors Bank &Trust in Boston, said another 5% gain against
high-yielding currencies could be in store over the next month or so.

Rate Worries Keep Stocks Under Pressure
Wednesday's Markets
Rate Worries Keep Stocks Under Pressure
By Justin Lahart
402 words
15 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
CONCERN THAT inflationary pressures will prompt the Federal Reserve to slow things down with higher
interest rates continued to weigh on stocks, and financial issues bore the brunt of it.
"People think the strong economic and inflation numbers are going force the Fed's hand to raise rates
sooner," said Todd Clark, head of listed trading at Wells Fargo Securities.
The market was reacting to a Labor Department report that consumer prices rose by 0.5% in March.
Excluding food and energy, prices rose by 0.4%, the biggest jump since November 2001. The inflation report
followed a report Tuesday that retail sales also had jumped sharply.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished the day down 3.33 points at 10377.95. Gains in the stocks of
health-care companies helped keep the index from falling much below the flatline. The Nasdaq Composite
Index slipped 5.23 points to 2024.85.
Treasury prices fell on the rate worries, pushing up the yield on the 10-year to 4.37%. Expectations that the
Fed may go into inflation-fighting mode helped spur selling in gold and oil, the prices of which would suffer if
the Fed succeeds.
Investors fret that for the Fed, rate increases will be like potato chips -- once it starts, it won't be able to have
just one. Such worries about rate increases have been creeping through the market, said Steven Goldman,
market strategist at Weeden &Co., with real-estate investment trusts and utilities -- whose dividends become
less attractive as rates go higher -- getting hit first. "Now it's spread to the financial sector," he said.
Some investors believe that higher rates may not be a negative for the overall market, figuring that the
stronger economic growth that might prompt them is good for profit margins. But given financial stocks'
prominence, trouble in the sector could roil the overall market.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks fell. On the NYSE, 1.55 billion shares changed hands; 2,617 issues fell and 735 rose.
Bonds fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 5/32, or $1.56 per $1,000 invested, to yield 4.366%. The 30-year
bond fell 3/32, putting its yield at 5.16%.
The dollar was mixed, at 108.71 yen, up from 106.63, as the euro rose to $1.1967 from $1.1946.

Options Report

English

NEW YORK -- Lackluster earnings reports from McDonald's Corp. and Intel Corp. and worries about rising
interest rates prompted investors to continue hedging some of their riskier stockholdings with options.
But there appears to be little anxiety about broad-market weakness, and the option market's risk forecast
declined. The Chicago Board Options Exchange volatility index, or VIX, reversed much of its Tuesday climb
and yesterday fell 1.64 or 9.5% to 15.62.
"The market seems to be treating this earnings season as a nonevent," said Michael Schwartz, Oppenheimer
Co.'s chief option strategist, referring to VIX's fairly complacent reading and the quiet trading.
Given the potential for worsening geopolitical tension and interest-rate increases, all of which could rattle
stock investors, Mr. Schwartz said that investors buying stock and taking on stock risk "should try and see
how they can modify that risk with option positions."
Option trading was guarded in Apple Computer, which reported after the markets closed profits that beat
consensus estimates. The company has outperformed the broad market this year, and cautious investors
likely were hedging their stock gains. The ratio of outstanding puts to calls in Apple has crept up in recent
sessions, and some contrarians think such caution might augur well for the stock.
"From a technical picture, Apple has held the low end of its trading range, and any good news on the
earnings tonight, along with its bearish option activity, could set Apple up to retest its highs," noted Jay
Shartsis, R.F. Lafferty &Co.'s director of option trading.
Apple closed down 29 cents to $26.64. Its April 25 puts traded 11,397 contracts, compared with 26,452
outstanding contracts, and gained 10 cents to 25 cents at the International Securities Exchange.
Traders picked spots to place bullish bets, too. Calls traded robustly in Newmont Mining after the mining
stock showed some technical resilience despite the recent drubbing suffered by the gold sector. Mr. Shartsis,
for instance, noted how Newmont Mining had held its March low while the Philadelphia Stock Exchange gold
and silver index, or XAU, had failed to do so.

Markets Say Good News Is Bad --- Solid Earnings, Economic Data Threaten Inflation Gyrations, Provoke Investor Consternation
Tuesday's Markets
Markets Say Good News Is Bad --- Solid Earnings, Economic Data Threaten Inflation Gyrations,
Provoke Investor Consternation
By E.S. Browning
781 words
14 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THERE ARE DAYS when Wall Street turns normal logic on its head, reacting badly to what most people
would consider good news. Yesterday was one of those days.
Reports of strong corporate earnings from the likes of Merrill Lynch, together with strong retail-sales figures,
which investors normally would have applauded, sent both stocks and bonds tumbling.
Instead of dazzling investors and creating hopes of strong earnings reports to come, the signs of a robust
economy provoked fears of inflation. Higher inflation likely would prompt the Federal Reserve to raise
interest rates to slow things down. The prospect of higher rates pushed the dollar to its highest level against
the euro since November. Crude oil was down. Gold, which had been rising as the dollar fell, turned sharply
down.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average dropped 134.28 points, or 1.28%, to 10381.28. It once again is in the red
for the year, down 0.7% since 2004 began. Even brokerage titan Merrill, whose stronger-than-expected
earnings pleased Wall Street briefly at the start of the day, finished down nearly 2%.
"I think reality set in," said John O'Donoghue, senior stock trader at New York brokerage firm Credit Suisse
First Boston. "The specter of higher interest rates is what is really spooking the market."
Traders said they saw signs that some big institutional investors were selling some of their stock holdings bit
by bit during the day, and those big, repeated sales weighed on the market.
Financial stocks led the declines. Banks and brokerage firms tend to suffer when interest rates rise because
they have to pay more to depositors and other lenders.
The 10-year Treasury note, whose price declines when investors expect higher interest rates, slid 29/32, or
$9.0625 for every $1,000 invested. Its yield, which rises when the note falls, jumped to 4.348%, its first push
above 4.3% since Jan. 5.
After regular trading hours, Intel released first-quarter earnings news and a forecast for second-quarter sales,
both of which fell short of expectations and could affect trading today. Curiously, investors, who had viewed
strong Merrill earnings as bad news, also initially reacted badly to Intel's weak earnings. Intel fell 1.5% in
after-hours trading.
Fears of higher interest rates could be seen in trading in interest-rate futures. On Monday, the trading
indicated that investors were forecasting a 73% chance that the Fed would raise its target short-term interest
rate one-quarter point by August. After yesterday's trading, the futures contracts indicated that the chances
had risen to 84%. The Fed's target rate, which determines the rate at which banks lend to one another
overnight, now stands at a 46-year low of 1%.
The reason for the worry in financial markets is that the exceptionally low interest rates that have prevailed
for months now have been one of the stock market's main underpinnings. As long as rates are so low, the
thinking goes, investors can bank on a recovering economy. What's more, alternatives to stocks, such as
money-market funds, certificates of deposit and the like, have limited appeal because they offer extremely
low returns.
Low interest rates were among the main reasons that investors pushed the Standard &Poor's 500-stock
index up to a level of 23 times the earnings of its member companies for the past 12 months. On average, the
S&P 500 has traded at about 15 times earnings in recent decades.

--- Page 184 ---
Page 184 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But with stocks trading at such high levels, compared with earnings, any sign of a problem can provoke
selling, and that appears to be what happened yesterday.
The broad S&P 500 fell 1.38%, or 15.76 points, to 1129.44, still up 1.6% so far this year. The Nasdaq
Composite Index, with its many technology stocks, fell 1.71%, or 35.40 points, to 2030.08, up 1.33% this
year.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.55%, or 0.86 point, to 154.55.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.42 billion shares traded, 437 stocks rose and 2,920 fell.
Bonds declined. The 30-year bond was down 1 12/32 to yield 5.154%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 106.63 yen, up from 105.35, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.1946 from $1.2071.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
414 words
14 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Today's Market Forecast]
Value Added
The government puts out its consumer-inflation statistics today, and investors seem to have decided on two
possible outcomes. Option one: Inflation is here and the consumer-price index will show it. Option two:
Inflation is here and the consumer-price index won't show it.
For well over a year now, select economists have predicted that inflation was coming back. But for a long
while, it seemed merely that expectations of inflation were back. Gold was rising and the difference between
inflation-protected bonds and regular government bonds widened. The dollar continued to fall, an inflationary
force. Then, commodity prices rose across the board.
Now, anecdotal evidence of inflation is rampant. The cost of a sneeze is rising, as Procter &Gamble,
Kimberly-Clark and Georgia-Pacific say they are going to raise prices on paper products. Restaurants are
raising prices. And the Folgers in your cup will cost more, too. Taxicabs in New York will charge more in May,
and as of this month it costs more to subscribe to The Wall Street Journal.
Hence the deep distrust of the government's inflation figures. "If you need the government telling you what
inflation is running at, you have no business in financial markets," says hedge-fund manager Bill
Fleckenstein.
The Fed has kept interest rates at radical emergency lows, repeating its fear of deflation. So, what were
investors to do but start to believe the Fed could print dollars to engineer inflation? In various forms over the
past year, investors have rushed into what is loosely being called the "reflation" trade. They bought stocks,
bought commodities, shorted the dollar, shorted bonds.
There are discrete reasons for each of these trades. Some trades are based on the housing explosion here in
the U.S. Some are predicated on the Chinese economic boom. Some, like buying gold and shorting the
dollar, are based on the view that the huge U.S. budget and trade deficits will catch up with the country.
If inflation emerges in earnest, the Fed will have to raise rates -- and sooner than expected. Perversely, the
emergence of inflation could mean the end of these reflation trades. Yesterday, stocks and gold fell sharply,
while the dollar rose. Since there has been extra speculative juice from fast-money hedge funds, that end
could be especially pronounced.

Tahoe Trading Shifts Its Focus
Options Report
Tahoe Trading Shifts Its Focus
By Kopin Tan Dow Jones Newswires
302 words
14 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Tahoe Trading Co., one of the largest independent options market-making firms, has
shuttered its trading operation to focus on what it says are other more promising areas of the derivatives
business.
Instead of making markets at options exchanges, the firm will devote its attention and resources to consulting
and technology.
The shift comes even as option volume has surged while stocks rallied during the past year. But competition
and technology have continued to keep the spreads between bid and ask prices narrow and profit margins
slim. Tahoe's technology unit has developed software to help traders price options.
Steve Braverman, Tahoe's managing partner, said that with more companies treating employee stock options
as an expense, and as the use of derivatives expands, he believes the advisory and technology arms
represent the best growth potential for the firm.
Meanwhile, strong retail sales raised concerns yesterday that the government might lift interest rates sooner,
and investors bid up Standard &Poor's 500 index puts to protect their portfolios from further market
weakness. That drove the CBOE volatility index up 1.98 or 13% to 17.26.
"Option traders are taking defensive positions in interest [rate] sensitive sectors," said Optionetics analyst
Frederic Ruffy, who pointed out active put trading in banks and utilities -- both sectors sensitive to
interest-rate movement.
In trading yesterday, the Philadelphia Stock Exchange Bank Index, or BKX, fell 2.22 to 98.48. Its May 100
puts gained 80 cents to $3 as 3,828 contracts traded.
The exchange's gold and silver index, or XAU, declined 6.16 to 95.56. The out-of-the-money May 80 puts
gained 25 cents to 40 cents as 1,035 contracts traded, compared with 443 outstanding contracts.

Dollar Climbs Against Its Rivals Amid View Fed Will Raise Rates
Currency Trading
Dollar Climbs Against Its Rivals Amid View Fed Will Raise Rates
By Jamie McGeever Dow Jones Newswires
456 words
14 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Growing expectations that the Federal Reserve will raise interest rates soon sent the dollar
leaping across the board as yet another solid piece of U.S. economic data appeared to confirm that the
economy is firing on all cylinders.
A 1.8% surge in March U.S. retail sales, reported yesterday, easily outstripped economists' forecasts of a
0.7% rise and extended the dollar's rally which began in London hours. The euro slumped to a four-month low
against the dollar, sterling lost over 1%, and the dollar jumped more than 1% against the yen from levels late
Monday in New York.
Coming less than a fortnight after the Labor Department announced a 308,000 surge in new hiring last month
and an upward revision to January and February's jobs growth, the retail sales data were clearly another
dollar-positive and led many hedge funds and other short-term speculative investors to turn against the euro.
"It's just another indication that the market is looking at the economic disparity between the U.S. and Europe,"
said Ronald Simpson, managing director of global currency analysis at research firm Action Economics.
"Things are coming together and the market is reaching a consensus that the U.S. is the place to be."
Anecdotal evidence would seem to support this. Stephen Smith, executive vice president at Brandywine
Asset Management, hedged all his exposure to European bonds two months ago and now has around
two-thirds of his entire portfolio in U.S. dollars up from 10% last year.
"We're very defensive from a currency standpoint," said Mr. Smith, who manages around $4 billion of
fixed-income assets.
The dollar's surge triggered a massive fall in the price of gold, which typically moves in the opposite direction
to the dollar. A stronger dollar makes dollar-denominated gold more expensive for overseas buyers.
Late yesterday in New York, the euro was at $1.1946, down from $1.2071 late Monday in New York. It had
traded as low as $1.1908, where it found support from the 200-day moving average around $1.1905,
technical analysts said.
Against the yen, the dollar was trading at 106.63 yen from 105.35 yen late Monday in New York, while the
euro, caught in the middle of the dollar's rise against both the yen and common currency, traded up a little to
127.31 yen from 127.24 yen.
The dollar was stronger against the Swiss franc at 1.2997 francs from 1.2830 francs while sterling was at
$1.8192 from $1.8390 late Monday in New York.
